Spiritual Exhortation: Dhikr, Submission, and Purification
The poem exhorts dhikr, fear, patience, and submission to qadar.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key spiritual practices advocated in this poem: dhikr with proper conduct, balancing fear and hope, repentance, gratitude and patience, submission to divine decree, following pious scholars, and constant mindfulness leading to perfection.

Core Idea
Spiritual growth requires disciplined remembrance, submission to God's decree, and inner purification.

This poem serves as a concluding spiritual exhortation, distilling essential practices for the seeker. It emphasizes dhikr with proper conduct, balancing fear and hope, and surrendering to God's decree. The verses guide the believer toward inner purification and constant mindfulness, culminating in a humble supplication.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem carefully
Read the Arabic and translation. Notice the poetic rhythm and the sequence of commands.
The poem is structure as a series of exhortations, each building on the previous.
2
Identify the core practices
List the main actions commanded: increase dhikr, make fear prevail, renew repentance, be grateful and patient, submit to decree, follow pious scholars, purify the heart.
These practices form a comprehensive spiritual program.
3
Understand the balance of fear and hope
The poem says 'make fear prevail over hope' but also 'do not despair of mercy.' Reflect on how fear and hope are balanced in Islamic spirituality.
Fear ensures humility, hope prevents despair.
4
Reflect on qadar and submission
The poem declares everything happens by decree and commands submission for safety. Consider the relationship between divine predestination and human responsibility.
Submitting to qadar is a key to inner peace.
5
Examine the final supplication
Note the humble prayer asking not to be cut off from God and for the radiant secret. Consider how this supplication encapsulates the seeker's ultimate desire.
The supplication shows reliance on God's mercy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَأَكْثَرَنْ مِنْ ذِكْرِهَا بِالأَدَبِ تَرْقَى بِهِذَا الذِّكْرِ أَعْلَى الرُّتَبِ وَغَلّبِ الخَوْفَ عَلَى الرَّجَاءِ وَسِرْ لِمَوْلَاكَ بِلَا تَنَاءِ وَجَدِّدِ التَّوْبَةَ لِلْأَوْزَارِ لَا تَيْأَسَنْ مِنْ رَحْمَةِ الغَفَّارِ وَكُنْ عَلَى آلَائِهِ شَكُوْرَا وَكُنْ عَلَى بَلَائِهِ صَبُوْرًا فَكُلُّ أَمْرٍ بِالقَضَاءِ وَالقَدَرْ وَكُلُّ مَقْدُوْرٍ فَمَا عَنْهُ مَفَرْ فَكُنْ لَهُ مُسَلِّمَا كَيْ تَسْلَمَا وَاتْبَعْ سَبِيْلَ النَّاسِكِيْنَ العُلَمَا وَخَلْصِ القَلْبَ مِنَ الأَغْيَارِ بِالجِدِّ وَالقِيَامِ فِي الأَسْحَارِ وَالفِكْرِ وَالذِّكْرِ عَلَى الدَّوَامِ مُجْتَنِباً لِسَائِرِ الآثَامِ مُرَاقِباً لِلَّهِ فِي الأَحْوَالِ لِتَرْتَقِي مَعَالِمَ الكَمَالِ وَقُلْ بِذُلٍّ رَبِّ لَا تَقْطَعْنِي عَنْكَ بِقَاطِعٍ وَلَا تَحْرِمْنِي مِنْ سِرِّكَ الأَبْهَى الْمُزِيْلِ لِلْعَمَى وَاخْتُمْ بِخَيْرٍ يَا رَحِيْمُ الرُّحَمَا وَالحَمْدُ لِلَّهِ عَلَى الإِنْعَامِ وَأَفْضَلُ الصَّلَاةِ وَالسَّلَامِ عَلَى النَّبِيِّ الهَاشِمِيِّ الخَاتِمِ وَآلِهِ وَصَحْبِهِ الأَكَارِمِ
So increase greatly in mentioning it with proper conduct; by this mention you will ascend to the highest ranks. And make fear prevail over hope, and proceed to your Lord without remoteness. And renew repentance for sins; do not despair of the mercy of the All-Forgiving. And be grateful for His blessings, and be patient in His trials. For every matter is by decree and predestination, and every decreed thing has no escape from it. So be submissive to Him that you may be safe, and follow the path of the pious scholars. And purify the heart from distractions with seriousness and standing in the early hours, and reflection and constant dhikr, avoiding all sins, being mindful of God in all states, so that you may ascend to the stations of perfection. And say with humility: 'O Lord, do not cut me off from You by any barrier, and do not deprive me of Your most radiant secret that removes blindness.' And end with good, O Most Merciful of the merciful. And praise be to God for completion, and the best of prayers and peace be upon the Hashimi Prophet, the Seal, and his noble family and companions.
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage is a concluding poem of spiritual exhortation. It begins by urging the reader to increase in 'dhikr' (remembrance of God) with 'adab' (proper conduct), promising that this remembrance elevates one to the highest ranks. The next line commands to make 'khawf' (fear of God) prevail over 'raja' (hope), a common emphasis in Islamic spirituality to ensure humility and vigilance. Then comes an injunction to renew 'tawba' (repentance) without despair of God's mercy. Gratitude ('shukr') in blessings and patience ('sabr') in trials are paired, followed by a declaration that all matters are by 'qada' (decree) and 'qadar' (predestination), with no escape from the decreed. The response is 'taslim' (submission) to God for safety. The path to follow is that of 'al-nasikin al-ulama' (pious scholars). Purification of the heart from distractions ('aghayar') is achieved through seriousness, standing in the pre-dawn hours, reflection, and constant dhikr, while avoiding all sins and being mindful of God ('muraqaba') in all states, leading to attainment of perfection ('kamal'). The poem concludes with a humble supplication asking God not to cut off the servant, to grant His radiant secret that removes blindness, and a request for a good end, followed by praise and salawat upon the Prophet.

المُرَاقَبَة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Making fear prevail over hope means one should never feel hope.
Correction: The instruction balances fear to ensure humility and vigilance, but hope in God's mercy remains essential, as the poem itself commands not to despair of mercy.

Checkpoint
According to the poem, which should prevail over the other?
A) Hope over fear
B) Fear over hope
C) Both equally
D) Neither

Key Takeaway
The poem offers a comprehensive spiritual roadmap: through remembrance, fear, repentance, patience, submission, and constant mindfulness, the believer ascends to perfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your daily life, does fear of God actually prevail over your hopes for His mercy, or do you find yourself taking His mercy for granted while neglecting repentance? When is the last time you truly feared losing His favor?
?
The poem says 'be submissive to Him that you may be safe.' When a trial or loss hits you, do you first react with resentment and complaint, or do you genuinely submit to that decree and then actively practice patience and gratitude? What is one recent trial where you failed to submit, and what would true submission have looked like?
?
You are commanded to 'purify the heart from distractions with seriousness and standing in the early hours, and reflection and constant dhikr.' What is one specific distraction—a worry, a desire, a relationship—that you have allowed to clutter your heart today? Will you commit to removing it before you sleep tonight?

Derivation of 'Ism' in Arabic Grammar: Basran and Kufan Views
Grammatical derivation of 'ism' from sumuww and its ten forms.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Basran and Kufan grammatical derivation of the word 'ism' and its ten recognized linguistic forms.

Core Idea
‘Ism’ is not primitive; it is derived from a root and has ten distinct forms.

This passage from a kalam commentary clarifies the linguistic origin of a fundamental term. Before theologians discuss the meaning of 'ism' as a name of Allah, they first examine its morphological derivation. The commentator explains the Basran view and lists the ten forms to ground the discussion in grammatical science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the literal translation. Let the grammatical structure emerge.
2
Identify the Basran derivation
Find where the commentator states that 'ism' is derived from 'al-sumuww' (elevation). Understand that this is the Basran position.
The Kufan view (not detailed here) derives it from 'wasm' (sign).
3
Analyze the original form 'simw'
Notice the morphological breakdown: sīn as fā', mīm as 'ayn, wāw as lām, on the pattern 'fa'l'. The lām (wāw) is dropped.
This shows the root structure.
4
Understand the ten forms from the couplet
The commentator cites a couplet that summarizes ten forms of 'ism'. Recognize that these involve dropping hamzah, shortening, and three short vowels.
The actual forms are enumerated in other sources.
5
Connect to kalam
Reflect on why this grammatical precision matters for theology: the term 'ism' must be defined before discussing divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وهو مشتق) : أي : مأخوذ . وقوله : ( من السُّمو) : أي : فالاسم مشتق من المصدر. قوله : (أي : يظهر) تفسير لـ ( يعلو). قوله: (فأصله سمو) مفرّع على قول البصري، وسمو بوزن (فعل)، فالسين فاء الكلمة والميم عينها، والواو لامها. قوله : ( بحذف لامه) : أي : التي هي الواو. قوله : ( بعد تسکین فائه) : هذا التَّعويض من جملة لغات عشرة في الاسم، جمعها بعضهم بقوله : (١) [ من الرجز] لغاتُ الاسم قد حواها الحصرُ في بيت شعر وهو هذا الشّعرُ اسم بحذف همزة والقصر مثلثات مع سمات عشر (1) البيتان للأشموني في شرحه على الألفية (٤٤/١).
His statement: (and it is derived) meaning: taken. And his statement: (from al-sumuww) meaning: so the name is derived from the infinitive. His statement: (i.e. it becomes apparent) is an interpretation of (it becomes elevated). His statement: (so its original is simw) branching from the Basran's statement, and simw is on the pattern of (fa'l), so the fā' of the word is the sīn, the 'ayn is the mīm, and the lām is the wāw. His statement: (by dropping its lām) i.e. which is the wāw. His statement: (after making its fā' sākin): this substitution is among the ten forms of the word 'ism', which some have gathered in his saying: (1) [from rajaz] The forms of 'ism' have been encompassed by restriction in a couplet, and this is the poetry: 'ism' with dropping of hamzah and shortening, with three short vowels and ten marks. (1) The two couplets are by al-Ashmuni in his commentary on the Alfiyya (1/44).
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage is a grammatical commentary on the derivation of the word 'ism' (name). The commentator begins by clarifying the statement 'and it is derived', explaining that 'ism' is taken (makhūdh) from a root. He then addresses the quote 'from al-sumuww', asserting that the name is derived from the infinitive (masdar). The expression 'i.e. it becomes apparent' interprets 'it becomes elevated' from the Basran view. Next, he analyzes the original form 'simw' and its morphological breakdown: sīn as fā', mīm as 'ayn, wāw as lām, on the pattern 'fa'l'. He explains that dropping the lām (the wāw) is part of the transformation. Then he notes that the substitution of hamzat al-wasl after making the fā' sākin is one of ten linguistic forms (lughāt) of 'ism'. To illustrate these ten forms, he cites a poetic couplet (rajaz) by al-Ashmuni: the first line states that the forms are encompassed in a couplet; the second line summarizes the forms: 'ism' with dropping of hamzah, shortening (qasr), three short vowels (muthallathāt), and ten marks (simāt). The passage aims to ground the grammatical understanding of a key kalam term.

Key Terms
مشتق
السمو
السمة
همزة الوصل
لغات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'ism' is simply the word 'name' without a root or derivation.
Correction: In fact, 'ism' is derived from 'sumuww' (elevation) according to the Basran grammarians, and it has ten recognized morphological forms.

Checkpoint
According to the Basran grammarians, from what word is 'ism' derived?
A) al-sumuww (elevation)
B) al-simah (mark)
C) al-wasm (sign)
D) al-ism (the name itself)

Key Takeaway
The word 'ism' is grammatically derived from 'sumuww' (Basran view) and has ten established forms, showing the importance of linguistic precision in theological terminology.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned the precise grammatical derivation of the word 'ism'. In your daily recitation and speech, do you consider the linguistic origins of the words you use when referring to Allah? Will you make an effort to understand the etymology of key terms in your faith, and how might that deepen your connection to the divine?

Meaning of Basmala and the Proper Name 'Allah'
Commentator explains the meaning of basmala and the nature of 'Allah' as a proper name, including the concept of ghalaba.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the linguistic derivation of the basmala and why 'Allah' is a proper name that does not fall under the two categories of linguistic predominance.

Core Idea
The basmala is a sign; 'Allah' is a unique proper name beyond actual or estimated predominance.

This lesson explores the opening lines of the commentary on the basmala. It explains how the word 'ism' is derived, what 'basmala' means as a sign of its referent, and then delves into the nature of the name 'Allah'. The commentator clarifies that 'Allah' is a proper name for the divine essence and, according to al-Sa'd, it is not a case of linguistic predominance (ghalaba), whether actual or estimated. Understanding this distinction helps avoid confusions about whether 'Allah' is a derived name or a common noun.

Guided Path
1
Basmala etymology
Notice how the word 'ism' is traced back to 'wasm' (mark) and the process of lightening the word. Understand that the basmala is a sign pointing to Allah.
The genitive construction 'bi-smillah' clarifies relation.
2
Meaning of basmala in context
The commentator discusses whether 'basmala' refers to the word or the named entity. See how both possibilities are considered, showing depth of analysis.
3
Proper name 'Allah'
The passage states 'Allah' is a proper name (alam) for the essence, a particular individual. Then it introduces al-Sa'd's comment that it is not a case of ghalaba.
4
Two types of ghalaba
Learn the definitions of actual predominance (e.g., 'al-najm' for Pleiades) and estimated predominance. The commentator says 'Allah' does not fit either category, which is a key theological point.
This refutes the view of al-Khalkhali.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
معنى البسملة لأنَّه علامة على مسمَّاه وأصله وسم، فخفف بحذف فائه ثمَّ عُوِّض عنها همزة الوصل . والمراد به هنا المسمَّى ؛ أي: مستعيناً بمسمى الله . والإضافة للبيان . و(الله): علم على الذات ... إلخ قوله : (لأنه علامة) : أي : دالٌ . قوله : وأصله) وسم) : أي : على وزن (فعل) بفتح الفاء، فالواو فاء الكلمة، والسِّين عينها، والميم لامها . قوله : (ثم عوّض عنها همزة وصل) : أي : توصُّلاً للنُّطق بالسَّاكن . قوله : (والمراد به هنا ... إلخ) : ليس بمتعين؛ لجواز أن يراد به اللفظ الدَّالُ على ذات الله ؛ لأنَّه يتبرَّك ويُستعان بالاسم، كما يُتبرك بالمسمى، والإضافة على هذا بمعنى اللام. قوله : (والله : علم على الذات ... إلخ) : أي : شخص جزئي، قال السعد وليس من باب الغلبة التحقيقيَّة ولا التَّقديريَّة، والغلبة أن يكون للفظ شمول لأفراد فيحصل له بحسب الاستعمال تخصيص ببعض أفراده؛ فإن وجد له أفراد فاختصّ ببعضها ؛ كانت الغلبة تحقيقيَّة ؛ كالنَّجم اسم لكل كوكب، ثمّ غلب على الثَّريا وإن لم يوجد له إلّا فرد؛ كانت الغلبة تقديرية، خلافاً لقول الخلخالي (۱) هو العلامة المتكلم المفسر حسين بن حسن الحسيني الخلخالي، أحد مشاهير
The meaning of the basmala: because it is a sign of its referent, and its original is wasm, then it was lightened by dropping its fā' and then a hamzat al-wasl was substituted for it. And what is meant by it here is the referent, i.e., seeking help by the named entity Allah. And the genitive construction is for clarification. And (Allah) is a proper name for the essence... etc. His statement: (because it is a sign) i.e., it indicates. His statement: (and its original is wasm) i.e., on the pattern of (fa'l) with a fatha on the fā', so the wāw is the fā' of the word, the sīn is the 'ayn, and the mīm is the lām. His statement: (then a hamzat al-wasl was substituted for it) i.e., to enable pronunciation of the sākin. His statement: (and what is meant by it here ... etc.) is not certain; because it is permissible that what is meant is the word signifying the essence of Allah, because blessing and seeking help are sought through the name just as they are sought through the named, and on this the genitive construction is in the sense of lām. His statement: (and Allah is a proper name for the essence ... etc.) i.e., a particular individual. al-Sa'd said it is not of the type of actual predominance (ghalaba haqīqiyya) nor estimated predominance (ghalaba taqdīriyya). Predominance is that a word has general applicability to individuals, then by usage it becomes specific to some of its individuals. If it has individuals and becomes specific to some, it is actual predominance, like 'al-najm' (the star) which is a name for every planet, then became predominant specifically for Pleiades. And if it has only one individual, it is estimated predominance, contrary to the statement of al-Khalkhālī (1), who is the distinguished scholar, interpreter, Husayn ibn Hasan al-Husayni al-Khalkhālī, one of the famous...
Source pages p. 62

Commentary
This passage is a combined explanation of the basmala's meaning and the nature of the proper name 'Allah'. It begins with the basmala: the word 'ism' (name) is a sign of its referent, derived from 'wasm' (mark). Its original form was 'wasm', then lightened by dropping the wāw and adding a hamzat al-wasl. The commentator clarifies that 'the meaning here' (i.e., in basmala) is not definitively the word or the named; it could be the word itself because one seeks blessing through the name. Then it examines 'Allah' as a proper name (alam) for the divine essence, described as a particular individual (shakhs juz'i). The commentary by al-Sa'd is cited: 'Allah' does not fall under actual or estimated predominance (ghalaba). The concept of ghalaba is defined: a word with general applicability becomes specific by usage to a subset. Actual predominance occurs when there are multiple individuals and the word becomes specific to one (e.g., 'al-najm' for all stars but predominantly Pleiades). Estimated predominance occurs when the word originally has only one individual. This refutes al-Khalkhali's alternative view. The passage ends with a biographical note on al-Khalkhali.

Key Terms
البسملة
علامة
مسمى
علم
غلبة
تحقيقي
تقديري

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking 'Allah' is a derived name or a common noun.
Correction: The passage establishes 'Allah' as a proper name (alam) for the divine essence, not derived from a root. It also clarifies it is not a case of predominance like common nouns.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is 'ghalaba haqīqiyya'?
A) When a word has only one individual from the start.
B) When a general word becomes specific to one of multiple individuals by usage.
C) When a word is always general and never specific.
D) When a word is a proper name like 'Allah'.

Key Takeaway
The basmala points to Allah, and 'Allah' is a unique proper name not subject to the categories of linguistic predominance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you say 'Bismillah' before an action, do you consciously treat those words as a sign pointing to Allah, or do they become a mere habit? How can you transform your recitation into a genuine reminder of His presence?
?
The name 'Allah' is unique, not subject to the categories of predominance that affect other names. Does your heart sense that uniqueness, or do you let His name become ordinary like any other word? What would it mean to truly honor that name in your speech and thoughts?
?
If the basmala is a sign of Allah, then every time you utter it, you are testifying that your action is for His sake. Search your past day: have you done anything 'bi-ismillah' in reality, or were you just saying the words? What concrete step will you take tomorrow to make your basmala meaningful?

Hope in Divine Forgiveness: Lessons from Two Hadiths
Commentary on hadiths about repentance and divine mercy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand two key hadiths on repentance and why hoping for Allah's forgiveness is preferable to fearing the recounting of sins.

Core Idea
Good deeds erase bad ones; Allah's shelter covers sins, but the hope of forgiveness is greater than the terror of standing.

This passage from the commentary presents two hadiths that illustrate the vastness of Allah's mercy. The first teaches that a good deed wipes out a sin, while the second describes Allah's intimate forgiveness on Judgment Day. The author then contrasts the hope of forgiveness with the difficulty of facing one's sins directly, emphasizing the former as the true object of hope.

Guided Path
1
Read the hadiths carefully
Read both hadiths in Arabic and translation. Notice the key phrases: 'follow an evil deed with a good one' and 'Allah places His shelter'.
The first hadith is about repentance in daily life; the second is about the Day of Resurrection.
2
Understand the author's preference
The author says 'the first' (the hope of forgiveness) is what is hoped from Allah's vast generosity. Why? Because the second matter involves standing before Allah and having sins mentioned, which is a great terror.
This shows the importance of repenting now rather than facing the reckoning later.
3
Reflect on the application
Consider how these hadiths encourage immediate repentance without despair. The hope is in Allah's mercy, not in the absence of accountability.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وورد في السُّنَّة ... إلخ) : أي : ففي الحديث: «وأتبعِ السِّيِّئَةَ الحسنةَ تمحُها (۱) ، وفي الحديث : «إنَّ الله تعالى يضعُ كنفه على عبده يومَ القيامة، ويخبره بجميع ما وقع ثمَّ يقول له: هذه ذنوبك سترتها عليك، والآن أغفرها لك» (۲) . منهُ ، قوله : (والمرجو من سعة كرمه تعالى الأوَّل) : أي : لما في الثاني من صعوبة الوقوف بين يدي الله ، وذكر المساوئ له وهو هول عظيم.
His saying: 'And it is mentioned in the Sunnah ... etc.': That is, in the hadith: 'Follow an evil deed with a good one, it will erase it (1), and in the hadith: 'Indeed Allah Almighty places His shelter over His servant on the Day of Resurrection, and informs him of everything that occurred, then says to him: These are your sins which I concealed for you, and now I forgive them for you (2).' From it. His saying: 'And what is hoped from the vastness of His generosity, the first': That is, because in the second there is difficulty of standing before Allah and mentioning bad deeds to Him, which is a great terror.
Source pages p. 118

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two phrases from the sharh text. First, the author explains the phrase 'And it is mentioned in the Sunnah ... etc.' by quoting two hadiths: one about following a sin with a good deed to erase it, and another about Allah forgiving sins He concealed on the Day of Resurrection. These hadiths support the idea of repentance and divine mercy. Second, the author explains the phrase 'And what is hoped from the vastness of His generosity, the first.' He says 'the first' refers to the hope for forgiveness through repentance mentioned in the first hadith, as opposed to the second matter—the difficulty of standing before Allah and recounting one's sins, which is a great terror. Thus, the author emphasizes the superiority of the first option (the hope of forgiveness) due to the terror involved in the second.

Key Terms
السنة
الحسنة
السيئة
الكنف
المساوئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The hadith about Allah covering sins on Judgment Day means we don't need to repent now, because He will forgive anyway.
Correction: The hadith actually shows that Allah conceals sins during life and forgives on that day, but it is presented in a context of repentance (the first hadith) and the author emphasizes the hope of forgiveness through repentance, not presumption.

Checkpoint
According to the author, why is the first matter (the hope of forgiveness) preferred over the second?
A) Because the second hadith is weaker.
B) Because the second matter involves standing before Allah and mentioning bad deeds, which is a great terror.
C) Because the first hadith promises immediate forgiveness without any reckoning.
D) Because the second matter is not mentioned in the Sunnah.

Key Takeaway
The hope of forgiveness through repentance is preferred because the alternative—standing before Allah with your sins exposed—is a great terror.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you commit a sin, do you immediately follow it with a good deed? If not, what holds you back from rushing to repentance?
?
Imagine standing before Allah with all your sins exposed—does that thought terrify you enough to change your habits today, or do you rely on His eventual forgiveness without remorse?
?
Allah concealed your sins in this world; do you take that concealment as license to sin more, or does it increase your gratitude and urgency to obey Him?

The Wondrous Marvel of Divine Knowledge and the Two Ways of Knowing
Al-Sawi glosses two phrases on spiritual knowledge and modes of knowing.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Sawi's explanation of the 'wondrous marvel' as the spiritual knowledge that illuminates hearts, and the two modes of knowing God: through rational proof and through direct experiential taste.

Core Idea
Spiritual knowledge is a wondrous gift, accessed either through proof or direct experience.

Al-Sawi begins by glossing the author's phrase 'wondrous marvel,' explaining it as the deep knowledges and secrets that settle in hearts and illuminate them. He then clarifies that recognizing God as the Beneficent Bestower can occur either through rational proof (dalil) or through direct spiritual taste and vision (dhawq wa 'iyan). This passage introduces a key distinction in Islamic spirituality between intellectual and experiential knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Let the words of al-Sawi settle in your mind before analyzing.
The passage is short, so read it slowly.
2
Identify the 'wondrous marvel'
Notice how al-Sawi defines 'the wondrous marvel' as 'knowledges and secrets that settle in hearts and illuminate them.' This is a direct reference to spiritual insight.
This is not a physical marvel but a spiritual one.
3
Recognize the two ways of knowing
Al-Sawi explains that knowing God as the Beneficent Bestower can be achieved either by proof (dalil) or by taste and direct vision (dhawq wa 'iyan). Reflect on the difference.
Proof is intellectual; taste is experiential.
4
Consider the complementarity
Both ways are valid and often complement each other. Think about how intellectual knowledge can prepare the heart for experiential knowledge.
5
Relate to your own experience
Reflect on a time you understood something about God through reasoning and another through a personal spiritual experience.
Optional but helpful.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
صفة الوجود
قوله : (إلى العجب العجاب أي من المعارف والأسرار التي تحلُّ في القلوب وتنوّرها .
قوله : (وعلمت أنَّه المحسن الوهّاب أي : إما بالدليل، أو الذوق والعيان .
قوله : (اللَّهم ؛ وفقنا ) : دعاء من الشَّيخ له وللمسلمين، وتقدم معنى التوفيق(١)
قوله : لما فيه رضاك) : أي : قبولك لنا وإثابتك إيَّانا .
قوله : (واقطعنا عن كل شيء سواك) : أي : فلا تجعل قلوبنا متعلقة به ولا ملتفتة إليه .
قوله: (واملأ قلوبنا بحبك ... إلخ) : طلب المحبَّة؛ لأنَّها رأس السعادة الأبدية.

(۱) انظر (ص١٥٥).
Attribute of existence. His saying: (to the wondrous marvel) i.e., among the knowledges and secrets that settle in hearts and illuminate them. His saying: (and You knew that He is the Beneficent Bestower) i.e., either by proof or by taste and direct vision. His saying: (O Allah, grant us success) is a supplication of the Shaykh for himself and the Muslims, and the meaning of tawfīq has preceded. His saying: (for what is Your pleasure) i.e., Your acceptance of us and Your rewarding us. His saying: (and cut us off from everything other than You) i.e., do not make our hearts attached to it nor turning to it. His saying: (and fill our hearts with Your love ... etc.) is a request for love, because it is the head of eternal happiness. His saying: (and make us taste the sweetness of union) i.e., resulting from love. His saying: (and take our hands if we slip) i.e., because the beloved lover has his sins forgiven; Abū al-Ḥasan al-Shādhilī said: 'Make our sins the sins of those You love.' (1) See p. 155.
Source pages p. 190

Commentary
This passage is a series of glosses by al-Sawi explaining the meaning of the author's supplicatory verses. Each gloss clarifies a phrase from the main text, focusing on spiritual meanings: the knowledge and secrets that illuminate hearts, the distinction between proof and direct experiential knowledge (dhawq), the meaning of success (tawfiq), the desire for divine acceptance and reward, detachment from other than God, love as the root of eternal bliss, the sweetness of union, and the hope for forgiveness through love. The commentator also quotes al-Shadhili. The passage is essentially a line-by-line spiritual commentary.

Key Takeaway

?

?

The Believer's Supplications: Tawfiq, Detachment, Love, Union, and Forgiveness
Al-Sawi explains the spiritual meanings of six supplicatory phrases.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual meanings behind al-Sawi's glosses on the author's supplications for divine success, acceptance, detachment from other than God, love, the sweetness of union, and forgiveness through love.

Core Idea
The pinnacle of spiritual life is love of God, from which detachment and union flow.

After glossing the foundational knowledge, al-Sawi turns to a series of supplications from the author. Each phrase is explained with a spiritual meaning: seeking tawfiq (divine success), asking for acceptance and reward, praying for detachment of the heart from everything other than God, requesting love as the essence of eternal happiness, desiring the sweetness of union with God, and seeking forgiveness and divine mercy in times of falling. These supplications outline the path of the seeker from beginning to end.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic supplications and al-Sawi's explanations
Read through each supplicatory phrase and its gloss. Let the sequence of requests sink in.
There are six distinct requests.
2
Identify each request
List them: tawfiq (success), acceptance/reward, detachment from other than God, love, union, and forgiveness upon slipping.
The phrasing is clear: 'grant us success', 'for what is Your pleasure', etc.
3
Notice the emphasis on love
Al-Sawi highlights that love is 'the head of eternal happiness.' This is central to the spiritual path.
Love is not just a feeling but the foundation of eternal bliss.
4
See the quote from al-Shadhili
Al-Shadhili's prayer asks God to make our sins like the sins of those He loves, implying that the beloved are forgiven.
This shows the hope of forgiveness through love.
5
Reflect on the sequence
The supplications progress from seeking success to detachment, love, union, and finally forgiveness. This mirrors the spiritual journey.
Each step builds on the previous.

Imam Ahmad's Dream: Evidence of Seeing Allah in Dreams
Al-Sawi narrates Imam Ahmad's dream as evidence for seeing Allah in dreams.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that seeing Allah in dreams is a real phenomenon, as evidenced by the dream of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal.

Core Idea
Seeing Allah in dreams is affirmed by the experience of saints like Imam Ahmad.

Al-Sawi begins his discussion by recounting the famous dream of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who saw Allah ninety-nine times and finally asked about the best act of worship. This story serves as a powerful proof that such visions occur for the righteous.

Guided Path
1
Read the story of Imam Ahmad's dream
Read the passage about Imam Ahmad's repeated dreams and his dialogue with Allah. Notice the number ninety-nine and the question about the best act.
The number is symbolic of near-completion.
2
Understand the dialogue
Imam Ahmad asks about the best means of drawing near, and Allah replies 'By reciting My words.' Then Ahmad asks about understanding, and Allah says both with and without understanding. Reflect on the meaning.
3
Reflect on the significance
This dream is cited as evidence that seeing Allah in dreams is possible. Consider why such an experience is important for the believer.
It shows that Allah can manifest Himself to His servants in dreams.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (وأما رؤيته تعالى في المنام فقد وقعت . . . إلخ): من جملة من رآه في المنام الإمام أحمد بن حنبل، فقد نقل عنه : أنه رآه في المنام تسعة وتسعين مرَّةً، وقال : لئن رأيته تمام المئة ؛ لأسألنَّه عن أفضل ما يتقرَّب به المتقربون، فرآه تمام المئة، وسأله فقال له : (بتلاوة كلامي يا أحمد)، فقال : بفهم وبغير فهم؟ فقال: (بفهم وبغير فهم) واعلم أنَّه إذا رئي في المنام؛ فقد يرى بالصفة التي ذكرت في : التوحيد وهي حقٌّ، وقد يرى بصفة الحوادث؛ فإنَّ رئي كذلك وأمر الرَّائي بما يخالف الشرع، كأن قال له : أسقطت عنك التكليف ؛ فهو الشَّيطان، فإن أطاعه وفعل بمقتضاه ؛ فهو ضال مضل قد خسر ضال مضل قد خسر الدنيا والآخرة، وإن لم يأمره بذلك؛ فهو رسول من من عند الله، فإذا علمت ذلك تعلم أنَّ الشَّيطان قد يتمثل بالمولى الله على أحد قولين .
His saying: (and as for seeing Him, Most High, in a dream, it has occurred... etc.): Among those who saw Him in a dream is Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, for it is related from him that he saw Him in a dream ninety-nine times, and said: 'If I see Him a complete hundred, I will ask Him about the best means by which those who draw near draw near.' So he saw Him the complete hundred and asked Him, and He said: 'By reciting My words, O Ahmad.' He said: 'With understanding or without understanding?' He said: 'With understanding and without understanding.' And know that when He is seen in a dream, He may be seen with the attributes mentioned in the [book] al-Tawhid, and that is true, and He may be seen with attributes of created things. If He is seen like that and commands the seer with something that contradicts the Sharia, like if He says to him: 'I have lifted obligation from you,' then it is the devil. If he obeys him and acts accordingly, then he is misguided and misguiding, having lost, misguided and misguiding, having lost this world and the Hereafter. And if he does not command him with that, then it is a messenger from Allah. So when you know that, you know that the devil may impersonate Allah, Most High, according to one of two opinions.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the author's statement about seeing Allah in dreams. It begins by citing the example of Imam Ahmad ibn Hanbal, who saw Allah in a dream numerous times and was instructed to recite Allah's words (the Quran) with and without understanding. This serves as proof that such visions occur to righteous individuals. The passage then provides a rule for interpreting dreams of Allah: if the vision presents Allah in His true transcendent attributes as known from the science of tawhid, it is genuine; but if He appears in attributes of created things and issues a command contrary to the Sharia (e.g., lifting religious obligations), then it is the devil impersonating Allah. The one who follows such a vision is misled. If no such unlawful command is given, the vision may be from a messenger (angel). The passage concludes by noting that the devil can impersonate Allah, a matter on which scholars differ.

Key Terms
رؤية في المنام
الإمام أحمد بن حنبل
تلاوة كلام الله

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What did Imam Ahmad ask Allah when he saw Him in a dream the hundredth time?
A) About the meaning of dreams.
B) About the best means of drawing near to Allah.
C) About the punishment of the grave.
D) About the number of prophets.

Key Takeaway
Imam Ahmad's dream confirms that seeing Allah in dreams is possible and that reciting the Quran is a superior act of worship.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite the Quran, do you ever hurry through it just to finish, without pausing to reflect on what Allah is saying to you? What would change if you treated every verse as a direct response from Allah to your heart's deepest questions?
?
Imam Ahmad persisted in seeking Allah's pleasure through a hundred dreams. What is one act of worship you have given up on because you didn't see immediate results? Will you resume it today, trusting that consistency itself pleases Allah?
?
Allah told Imam Ahmad that both reciting with and without understanding are means of drawing near. Are there parts of your life where you avoid doing something for Allah because you feel you don't 'understand' enough? How can you still take that step today, relying on the barakah of the action itself?

Discerning True Visions from Devilish Impersonation
Al-Sawi outlines rules for discerning true visions of Allah from devilish impersonation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rule for distinguishing a genuine vision of Allah in a dream from a satanic impersonation.

Core Idea
If a dream vision of Allah commands something against Sharia, it is the devil.

After establishing that seeing Allah in dreams occurs, Al-Sawi provides a crucial criterion: if the vision shows Allah with attributes of creation and issues a command contrary to Sharia, it is the devil. This lesson examines that rule and its consequences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of visions
The passage states that Allah may be seen with His true transcendent attributes or with attributes of created things. Recognize the difference.
True visions align with tawhid.
2
Understand the devil's trick
If the vision commands something against Sharia, like lifting religious obligations, it is the devil impersonating Allah. This is a key sign.
3
Learn the consequence
If a person obeys such a command, they are led astray. If no unlawful command is given, the vision might be from an angel.
This shows the importance of adhering to Sharia even in dreams.

Source Text

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some believe any dream of Allah is a direct communication from Him and must be obeyed without question.
Correction: This passage teaches that only dreams that align with Sharia are valid; commands contradicting Sharia indicate satanic impersonation.

?

The Definition of Tasawwuf and Its Relation to Fiqh
Al-Sawi defines tasawwuf and explains its necessary complementarity with fiqh.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold definition of tasawwuf (as a science and a practice) and its necessary relation to fiqh, as presented by Al-Sawi.

Core Idea
Tasawwuf is the life of hearts, but without fiqh it becomes heresy; together they lead to truth.

This lesson introduces Al-Sawi's definition of tasawwuf as both a science and a practice, grounded in purity of heart. He emphasizes that tasawwuf cannot stand alone—it requires prior knowledge of creed and fiqh. A famous saying warns against separating the two, highlighting their complementarity.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words and structure come through before interpreting.
Notice the poetic line quoted by Al-Sawi; it explains the etymology of 'Sufi'.
2
Identify the twofold definition
The passage defines tasawwuf in two ways: as a science (knowledge of principles for heart soundness) and as a practice (earnestness in Shariah, heart's standing with Allah, purification). Find both parts.
These two aspects are complementary, not separate.
3
Understand the derivation from safa'
Al-Sawi explains that 'tasawwuf' comes from 'safa' (purity), meaning inner freedom from desires. Reflect on how this etymology shapes the discipline's aim.
The poetic line reinforces the link between purity and the name 'Sufi'.
4
Grasp the relation to fiqh and the tripartite saying
Al-Sawi clarifies that knowing creed (and fiqh) is necessary for valid tasawwuf. The saying distinguishes three outcomes: tasawwuf without fiqh leads to heresy, fiqh without tasawwuf leads to transgression, and combining both leads to realization.
This shows that outward law and inner purification must go together.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا شروع منه - سامحه الله تعالى - في فن التصوف الذي – هو حياة القلوب، رتبه على معرفة عقائد الإيمان؛ لأنّه لا يمكن السَّيرُ إلى الله تعالى إلّا بعد معرفتها .
وحدُّ التَّصوُّف عِلماً : هو علم بأصول يُعرف به صلاح القلب، وعملاً : هو الجدُّ في العمل بأحكام الشريعة، وقيام القلب مع الله تعالى، وصفاؤه من الأكدار.
قوله : (في فنِّ التَّصوُّف ) : مأخوذ من الصَّفاء، وهو خلوص الباطن من الشَّهوات ،والكدرات، قال بعض العارفين : من المواليا ]
يا واصفي أنت في التحقيق موصوفي وعارفي لا تغالط أنت معروفي إنَّ الفتى من بوعده في الأزل يوفي صافي فصوفيّ لهذا سمي الصُّوفي قوله : (إلا) بعد معرفتها ) : أي : ومعرفة الأحكام الفقهيّة التي بها تصحُ عباداته، ولذا قيل : من تصوّف ولم يتفقه ؛ فقد تزندق، ومن تفقه ولم يتصوّف؛ فقد تفسق، ومن تصوّف وتفقَه ؛ فقد تحقَّق ).
And this is a beginning from him—may Allah forgive him—in the art of tasawwuf, which is the life of hearts. He arranged it after knowledge of the beliefs of faith, because journeying to Allah is not possible except after knowing them. The definition of tasawwuf as a science: it is a science of principles by which the soundness of the heart is known, and as a practice: it is earnestness in acting upon the rulings of the Shariah, and the heart's standing with Allah, and its purification from impurities. His statement: (in the art of tasawwuf): it is derived from purity (al-safa'), which is the sincerity of the inner self from desires and turbidities. Some gnostics said: [a line of poetry: O you who describe me, in reality you are described by me; O you who know me, do not delude yourself, you are known by me; verily the young man by his promise in pre-eternity fulfills; pure, so the Sufi is thus named]. His statement: (except) after knowing them: i.e., and knowledge of the fiqh rulings by which his acts of worship are valid. Hence it was said: Whoever practices tasawwuf without studying fiqh becomes a heretic; whoever studies fiqh without practicing tasawwuf becomes a transgressor; and whoever practices both has realized the truth.
Source pages p. 403

Commentary
This passage is a definition and methodological introduction to the science of tasawwuf. Al-Sawi begins by stating that this section is a beginning in the art of tasawwuf, which he describes as the life of hearts. He notes that it follows the knowledge of the beliefs of faith (i.e., kalam) because spiritual journeying to Allah requires prior knowledge of correct creed. He then provides a twofold definition of tasawwuf: as a science (ilm), it is a science of principles (usul) by which the soundness of the heart is known; as a practice (amal), it is earnestness (jidd) in acting upon the rulings of the Shariah, along with the heart's standing with Allah and its purification from impurities. The commentary explains that the word 'tasawwuf' is derived from purity (safa'), meaning the inner self's freedom from desires and turbidities. He supports this with a quotation from a gnostic in poetic form, which alludes to the pre-eternal covenant linking purity to the name Sufi. Al-Sawi then expands on the phrase 'after knowing them' (i.e., the creedal beliefs), clarifying that it also includes knowledge of fiqh rulings that validate one's worship. He concludes with a well-known saying that distinguishes three categories: one who pursues tasawwuf without fiqh becomes a heretic (tazandaq), one who studies fiqh without tasawwuf becomes a transgressor (tafassaq), and one who combines both has attained realization (tahaqqaq). This emphasizes the complementary nature of outward law and inward purification.

The Goal and Subject Matter of Tasawwuf
Al-Sawi states the goal and subject matter of tasawwuf with scriptural evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold goal of tasawwuf—rectification in this world and attainment in the afterlife—and its subject matter as the Muhammadan character traits derived from the Qur'an.

Core Idea
Tasawwuf aims to perfect heart and senses; its method is embodying Qur'anic ethics.

Al-Sawi concisely defines the purpose and focus of tasawwuf, grounding it in both inner purification and outer conduct. This passage establishes that tasawwuf is not mere mysticism but a practical science of character formation based on the Prophet's example. The scriptural evidence from ʿĀʾisha ties it directly to the Qur'an.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation once to grasp the overall meaning. Note the structure: goal first, then subject.
2
Analyze the goal
Identify the two aspects of the goal: rectification of heart and senses in this world, and attainment of highest ranks in the Hereafter. Understand that 'goal' means benefit.
3
Understand the subject
Notice that the subject is the Muhammadan character traits, and the commentary clarifies they are the commands and prohibitions of the Qur'an. Reflect on the hadith of ʿĀʾisha.
This shows tasawwuf is deeply scriptural.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وغايته: صلاح القلب وسائر الحواس في الدُّنيا، والفوز بأعلى المراتب في العُقبى . وموضوعه : الأخلاق المحمَّديَّة من حيث التَّخلُّق بها . قوله : ( وغايته : صلاح القلب : مراده بالغاية الفائدة. وقوله : (والفوز بأعلى المراتب) : هذا هو غايته. قوله: (وموضوعه الأخلاق المحمدية أي وهي أوامر القرآن ونواهيه ؛ لما ورد عن عائشة أنَّها سئلت أخلاقه قالت : (كان خلقه القرآن) (۱).
And its goal: rectification of the heart and all the senses in this world, and attainment of the highest ranks in the Hereafter. And its subject: the Muhammadan character traits with respect to adopting them as one's character. His statement: (And its goal: rectification of the heart) — he means by 'goal' the benefit. His statement: (and attainment of the highest ranks) — this is its ultimate goal. His statement: (and its subject the Muhammadan character traits) — that is, they are the commands and prohibitions of the Qur'an, based on what has been narrated from 'A'isha that when she was asked about his character, she said: 'His character was the Qur'an.'
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage defines the goal (ghāya) and subject matter (mawḍūʿ) of tasawwuf. The goal is twofold: first, rectification of the heart and all the senses in this world, meaning internal and external purification; second, attaining the highest ranks in the afterlife. The commentary clarifies that 'goal' here means the benefit or end result. The subject matter of tasawwuf is the Muhammadan character traits (akhlāq muḥammadiyya), specifically regarding their adoption as one's own character. The commentary explains that these character traits are essentially the commands and prohibitions of the Qur'an. This is supported by the famous ḥadīth of ʿĀʾisha who said that the Prophet's character was the Qur'an itself. Thus, tasawwuf is concerned with embodying Qur'anic ethics. The passage establishes tasawwuf as a practical science aimed at moral and spiritual perfection, grounded in revelation.

Definitions, Goal, and Subject of Tasawwuf
The author defines tasawwuf, its goal, and its subject, with commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition, goal, and subject of tasawwuf as explained by traditional scholars, emphasizing caution, effort, and mindfulness.

Core Idea
Tasawwuf is a path of caution, effort, and constant awareness, aiming for heart purity and highest ranks.

This passage presents multiple definitions of tasawwuf from early scholars, clarifying its goal and subject. Before we delve into the details, it's important to see that tasawwuf is not merely asceticism but a comprehensive discipline covering actions, intentions, and mindfulness. Let's explore the text to uncover these meanings.

Guided Path
1
Read the three definitions
Read the first part of the passage where the author gives three definitions of tasawwuf. Notice the emphasis on caution, earnestness, and guarding the senses.
The definitions are: (1) taking the most cautious, (2) earnestness in the path to God, (3) guarding the senses and observing breaths.
2
Identify the goal and subject
After the definitions, the author states the goal and subject of tasawwuf. Find these and note the dual purpose (this world and the next) and the subject as Muhammadan ethics.
Goal: soundness of heart and senses in this world, highest ranks in the afterlife. Subject: Muhammadan moral qualities.
3
Examine the commentary
Read the commentary on 'guarding the senses' and 'observing the breaths'. Understand what these entail in practice—avoiding displeasing God and not wasting a single breath.
The commentary also clarifies that all three definitions converge in meaning.
4
Reflect on the convergence
Consider why the author says the meanings are close. How do caution, earnestness, and mindfulness all relate to the same core idea of spiritual discipline?
They all involve conscious effort to align with divine will.

هو الأخذ بالأحوط من المأمورات واجتناب المنهيَّات، والاقتصارُ على الضَّروريَّات من المباحات . ويقال : هو الجد في السلوك إلى ملك الملوك، ويقال : هو حفظ الحواس ومراعاة الأنفاس، والمعنى متقارب. وغايته: صلاح القلب وسائر الحواس في الدُّنيا، والفوز بأعلى المراتب في العُقبى . وموضوعه : الأخلاق المحمَّديَّة من حيث التَّخلُّق بها . قوله : (حفظ الحواس) أي من كل ما يغضب الله تعالى . قوله : (ومراعاة الأنفاس) : أي : فلا يضيّع نفساً في غير طاعة؛ فإِنَّ الإنسان يخرج منه كلَّ يوم وليلة مئة ألف وأربعة وعشرون ألف نفس ينبغي له أن يراعيها ولا يضيعها . قوله : (والمعنى متقارب) : أي : في التعاريف الثَّلاثة . قوله : ( وغايته : صلاح القلب : مراده بالغاية الفائدة. وقوله : (والفوز بأعلى المراتب) : هذا هو غايته. قوله: (وموضوعه الأخلاق المحمدية أي وهي أوامر القرآن ونواهيه ؛ لما ورد عن عائشة أنَّها سئلت أخلاقه قالت : (كان خلقه القرآن) (۱).
It is taking the most cautious of the commanded things and avoiding the prohibited things, and limiting oneself to the necessary of permissible things. And it is said: it is earnestness in the path to the King of Kings, and it is said: it is guarding the senses and observing the breaths, and the meanings are close. Its goal: soundness of the heart and the rest of the senses in this world, and attaining the highest ranks in the afterlife. Its subject: the Muhammadan moral qualities in terms of adopting them. His saying: (guarding the senses) means from everything that angers Allah Almighty. His saying: (observing the breaths) means: he should not waste a breath in other than obedience; for man exhales every day and night one hundred and twenty-four thousand breaths, and he should observe them and not waste them. His saying: (the meaning is close) means: among the three definitions. His saying: (its goal: soundness of the heart) he means by 'goal' the benefit. And his saying: (and attaining the highest ranks) this is its goal. His saying: (its subject: the Muhammadan moral qualities) meaning they are the commands and prohibitions of the Quran; because it was narrated from Aisha that she was asked about his character and she said: 'His character was the Quran.'
Source pages p. 405

Commentary
This passage provides multiple definitions of tasawwuf, states its goal and subject, and then offers commentary on key phrases. The author first gives three definitions: (1) taking the most cautious in commands and avoiding prohibitions, limiting to necessities; (2) earnest effort in the path to God; (3) guarding the senses and observing every breath. All three converge in meaning. The goal of tasawwuf is twofold: soundness of the heart and senses in this world, and attaining the highest ranks in the hereafter. Its subject matter is the Muhammadan moral qualities—that is, adopting the character of the Prophet, which is embodied in the commands and prohibitions of the Quran, as confirmed by the hadith of Aisha. The commentary explains that 'guarding the senses' means avoiding anything that displeases God, and 'observing the breaths' means not wasting a single breath in disobedience, noting the daily count of breaths. The author clarifies that all three definitions are essentially close in meaning. The goal is the ultimate benefit, and the subject is the Quranic ethics.

The Three Realms: Shari'ah, Tariqah, and Haqiqah
The author defines shari'ah, tariqah, and haqiqah and states the relationship between them.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three interrelated levels of Islamic practice—shari'ah, tariqah, and haqiqah—and how haqiqah results from purifying the heart through practice.

Core Idea
Shari'ah, tariqah, and haqiqah are not separate; they form a single integrated path.

This lesson introduces the foundational distinction between divine rulings (shari'ah), their active practice (tariqah), and the inner spiritual realities (haqiqah) that arise from sincere practice. Recognizing their interconnection is essential for understanding Islamic spirituality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's definitions sink in.
2
Identify the three terms
Find the definitions of shari'ah, tariqah, and haqiqah. Notice how each is characterized: shari'ah as rulings, tariqah as action, haqiqah as inner sciences.
3
Understand the relationship
Note that haqiqah is described as 'the secrets of shari'ah and the result of tariqah.' This shows they are connected.
4
Reflect on the 'knowers' (arifun)
The passage mentions those who take this knowledge from the Prophet through an unbroken chain. Consider the importance of transmission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ التَّصرُّف بمعنى العمل هو الطَّريقة، وأمَّا الشَّريعةُ فهي الأحكام التي وردت عن الشَّارع المعبّر عنها بالدِّين، وأما الحقيقة أسرار الشريعة ونتيجة الطريقة، فهي علوم ومعارف تحصل لقلوب السالكين بعد صفائها من كدرات الطبائع البشرية . ولا شيء أقرب [؟] العارفون الآخذون له عن النَّبيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم بالسند المتصل. و نسبته : أنَّه فرع علم التوحيد . و استمداده من الكتاب والسُّنَّة . : واسمه : علم التصوف . وحكمه : الوجوب . ومسائله : قضاياه التي يُبحث فيها عن عوارضه الذاتية؛ كالفناء والبَقاء، والمراقبة والمشاهدة، والجلال والجمال، وغير ذلك. قوله : (المعبر عنها بالدِّين): أي: والملة. قوله : (الصفاء القلب) أي خلوصه من أدرانه وكدراته . : : قوله مع الآداب أي مع القيام بها والتزامها . قوله إلى مطلوبه ) أي : وهو صفاء القلب.
And know: that action in the sense of practice is the tariqah. As for the shari'ah, it is the rulings that came from the Lawgiver, expressed by religion (din). As for the haqiqah, it is the secrets of the shari'ah and the result of the tariqah, and it consists of sciences and knowledges that come to the hearts of the seekers after their purification from the turbidities of human natures. And there is nothing closer [؟] the knowers (arifun) who take it from the Prophet, peace be upon him, through an uninterrupted chain. Its relationship: it is a branch of the science of tawhid. Its source: the Book and the Sunnah. Its name: the science of tasawwuf. Its ruling: obligation. Its topics: its propositions in which its essential accidents are investigated, such as annihilation (fana'), abiding (baqa'), watchfulness (muraqaba), witnessing (mushahada), majesty (jalal), beauty (jamal), and others. His saying: (expressed by religion) meaning: the community (milla). His saying: (purity of heart) meaning its purification from its rust and turbidities. His saying: 'with the manners' meaning with performing them and adhering to them. His saying: 'to his object' meaning: and that is purity of heart.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage distinguishes three levels of religious practice: shari'ah (the divine rulings), tariqah (the active practice of those rulings), and haqiqah (the inner secrets and results of the practice). Haqiqah consists of spiritual sciences that arise after the heart is purified from base human traits. The 'knowers' (arifun) receive this knowledge from the Prophet via an unbroken chain. The passage then formally classifies the science of tasawwuf: it is a branch of the science of tawhid (theology), its sources are the Quran and Sunnah, its name is 'ilm al-tasawwuf, its ruling is that it is obligatory (wajib) upon individuals, and its topics include essential accidents such as fana' (annihilation), baqa' (abiding), muraqaba (watchfulness), mushahada (witnessing), jalāl (majesty), jamāl (beauty), and others. The commentary expands on some phrases: 'expressed by religion' also means the community (milla); 'purity of heart' means cleansing it from rust and turbidities; 'with the manners' means performing and adhering to them; 'to his object' refers to purity of heart.

حقيقة

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think shari'ah, tariqah, and haqiqah are separate, independent paths.
Correction: The passage presents them as interconnected: shari'ah provides rulings, tariqah is the practice, and haqiqah is the inner result of that practice.

Key Takeaway
The path to spiritual reality (haqiqah) begins with the rulings of shari'ah and requires their sincere practice (tariqah).

Classification of the Science of Tasawwuf
The author formally classifies the science of tasawwuf under five headings: relationship, source, name, ruling, and topics.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the science of tasawwuf is formally classified in terms of its relationship, source, name, ruling, and topics.

Core Idea
Tasawwuf is an obligatory branch of tawhid derived from Quran and Sunnah, investigating essential spiritual states.

This lesson outlines the formal classification of the science of tasawwuf, defining its place among the Islamic sciences. The author provides five key aspects—relationship, source, name, ruling, and topics—that establish tasawwuf as a legitimate and essential discipline, focused on the purification of the heart.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation, noting the five distinct aspects presented.
2
Identify each aspect
Look for: relationship (branch of tawhid), source (Book and Sunnah), name (tasawwuf), ruling (obligation), and topics (essential accidents like fana', baqa', etc.).
These are standard means of defining a science in Islamic scholarship.
3
Understand the commentary
The passage includes commentary explaining 'purity of heart' as cleansing from rust and turbidities, linking to the goal of tasawwuf.
4
Reflect on the topics
The topics listed—annihilation, abiding, watchfulness, witnessing, majesty, beauty—are core spiritual states studied in tasawwuf.
These are 'essential accidents' intrinsic to the science.

واعلم : أنَّ التَّصرُّف بمعنى العمل هو الطَّريقة، وأمَّا الشَّريعةُ فهي الأحكام التي وردت عن الشَّارع المعبّر عنها بالدِّين، وأما الحقيقة أسرار الشريعة ونتيجة الطريقة، فهي علوم ومعارف تحصل لقلوب السالكين بعد صفائها من كدرات الطبائع البشرية . ولا شيء أقرب [؟] العارفون الآخذون له عن النَّبيِّ صلى الله عليه وسلم بالسند المتصل. و نسبته : أنَّه فرع علم التوحيد . و استمداده من الكتاب والسُّنَّة . : واسمه : علم التصوف . وحكمه : الوجوب . ومسائله : قضاياه التي يُبحث فيها عن عوارضه الذاتية؛ كالفناء والبَقاء، والمراقبة والمشاهدة، والجلال والجمال، وغير ذلك. قوله : (المعبر عنها بالدِّين): أي: والملة. قوله : (الصفاء القلب) أي خلوصه من أدرانه وكدراته . : : قوله مع الآداب أي مع القيام بها والتزامها . قوله إلى مطلوبه ) أي : وهو صفاء القلب.
And know: that action in the sense of practice is the tariqah. As for the shari'ah, it is the rulings that came from the Lawgiver, expressed by religion (din). As for the haqiqah, it is the secrets of the shari'ah and the result of the tariqah, and it consists of sciences and knowledges that come to the hearts of the seekers after their purification from the turbidities of human natures. And there is nothing closer [؟] the knowers (arifun) who take it from the Prophet, peace be upon him, through an uninterrupted chain. Its relationship: it is a branch of the science of tawhid. Its source: the Book and the Sunnah. Its name: the science of tasawwuf. Its ruling: obligation. Its topics: its propositions in which its essential accidents are investigated, such as annihilation (fana'), abiding (baqa'), watchfulness (muraqaba), witnessing (mushahada), majesty (jalal), beauty (jamal), and others. His saying: (expressed by religion) meaning: the community (milla). His saying: (purity of heart) meaning its purification from its rust and turbidities. His saying: 'with the manners' meaning with performing them and adhering to them. His saying: 'to his object' meaning: and that is purity of heart.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage distinguishes three levels of religious practice: shari'ah (the divine rulings), tariqah (the active practice of those rulings), and haqiqah (the inner secrets and results of the practice). Haqiqah consists of spiritual sciences that arise after the heart is purified from base human traits. The 'knowers' (arifun) receive this knowledge from the Prophet via an unbroken chain. The passage then formally classifies the science of tasawwuf: it is a branch of the science of tawhid (theology), its sources are the Quran and Sunnah, its name is 'ilm al-tasawwuf, its ruling is that it is obligatory (wajib) upon individuals, and its topics include essential accidents such as fana' (annihilation), baqa' (abiding), muraqaba (watchfulness), mushahada (witnessing), jalāl (majesty), jamāl (beauty), and others. The commentary expands on some phrases: 'expressed by religion' also means the community (milla); 'purity of heart' means cleansing it from rust and turbidities; 'with the manners' means performing and adhering to them; 'to his object' refers to purity of heart.

The Distinction Between Tariqa, Sharia, and Haqiqa
Al-Sawi defines the three terms tariqa, sharia, and haqiqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the complementary relationship between the outward law (sharia), the spiritual path (tariqa), and the inner reality (haqiqa).

Core Idea
Haqiqa is the fruit of following sharia and tariqa, not a separate path.

In this passage, al-Sawi clarifies three foundational terms in Islamic spirituality. Understanding their interrelation is crucial: the outward law, the practical path, and the inner reality are not separate but sequential stages. This lesson will unpack each term and their connection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Let the definitions sink in before analyzing.
The passage is concise; note the logical connectors.
2
Identify the three terms
Find the definitions for tariqa, sharia, and haqiqa. Notice how each is introduced with 'أما'.
Each term has a distinct role.
3
Understand the sequence
Observe that haqiqa is described as 'أسرار الشريعة ونتيجة الطريقة' — the secrets of sharia and result of tariqa.
This shows a cause-effect relationship.
4
Reflect on the misconception
Consider the common error: that haqiqa can be attained without sharia. The passage clearly ties it to both.
This is a key point in Islamic spirituality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الفرق بين الطّريقة والشّريعة والحقيقة
واعلم : أنَّ التَّصرُّف بمعنى العمل هو الطَّريقة، وأمَّا الشَّريعةُ فهي الأحكام التي وردت عن الشَّارع المعبّر عنها بالدِّين، وأما الحقيقة أسرار الشريعة ونتيجة الطريقة، فهي علوم ومعارف تحصل لقلوب السالكين بعد صفائها من كدرات الطبائع البشرية.
The difference between the way (tariqa), the law (sharia), and the truth (haqiqa). And know that the conduct (al-tasarruf) in the sense of action is the way (tariqa). As for the law (sharia), it is the rulings that came from the Lawgiver (al-shari‘), expressed as religion (din). As for the truth (haqiqa), it is the secrets of the law and the result of the way; it is sciences and cognitions that are attained in the hearts of the wayfarers (salikin) after their purification from the turbidity of human natures.
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage defines three key terms in Islamic spirituality. It begins by stating the topic: the difference between tariqa, sharia, and haqiqa. Then it explains each: tariqa is the practical conduct, sharia is the revealed rulings, and haqiqa is the inner reality resulting from following the sharia and tariqa. It specifies that haqiqa consists of sciences and cognitions (ulum wa ma‘arif) that are attained in the hearts of the seekers after purification. This teaching establishes the complementary relationship between the outward law and inward reality.

Key Terms
الطريقة
الشريعة
الحقيقة
السالكين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think haqiqa is independent of sharia and tariqa.
Correction: Al-Sawi shows haqiqa is the secrets of sharia and result of tariqa, so it depends on both.

Checkpoint

A) Haqiqa is independent from sharia.
B) Haqiqa is the secrets and result of sharia.
C) Haqiqa is the same as sharia.
D) Haqiqa is contrary to sharia.

Key Takeaway
The three concepts are sequential: sharia provides the rulings, tariqa is the practical conduct, and haqiqa is the inner realization attained through purification.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This lesson teaches that haqiqa (inner reality) is attained only after purification from 'the turbidity of human natures'. What specific turbidities in your own character are you currently allowing to block your access to deeper spiritual truths? Will you begin to actively work on removing one today?
?
Al-Sawi describes tariqa as 'conduct in the sense of action'. Examine your daily rituals—prayer, charity, fasting. Are they truly actions of a wayfarer seeking haqiqa, or are they hollow motions done out of habit? What would it take for you to turn one of them into a sincere step on the path?
?
If you believe that you can reach haqiqa without fully committing to sharia and tariqa, you are deceived. Where in your life have you excused yourself from a ruling of the law or neglected the practical path, thinking you are beyond it? How will you repent and realign yourself today?

Definition of the Science of Tasawwuf and Its Issues
Al-Sawi defines tasawwuf as a science, gives its ruling, and lists its issues.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that tasawwuf is defined as an obligatory science with specific issues (essential accidents) such as annihilation, subsistence, watchfulness, witnessing, majesty, and beauty.

Core Idea
Tasawwuf is an obligatory science with defined issues.

Al-Sawi begins by formally defining the science of tasawwuf, stating its ruling and enumerating its key topics. This lesson establishes the foundational definition that positions tasawwuf as a legitimate, obligatory field of study within the Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning.
Notice the concise definition format: name, ruling, issues.
2
Identify the three components
The passage presents three elements: the name (علم التصوف), the ruling (الوجوب), and the issues (المسائل). Identify each and understand its role.
3
Reflect on the examples of issues
The passage lists examples like الفناء والبقاء, المراقبة والمشاهدة, الجلال والجمال. Consider why these are essential accidents of tasawwuf.
These terms are central to Sufi terminology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واسمه : علم التصوف . وحكمه : الوجوب . ومسائله : قضاياه التي يُبحث فيها عن عوارضه الذاتية؛ كالفناء والبقاء، والمراقبة والمشاهدة، والجلال والجمال، وغير ذلك. قوله : (المعبر عنها بالدِّين): أي: والملة. قوله : (الصفاء القلب) أي خلوصه من أدرانه وكدراته . قوله مع الآداب أي مع القيام بها والتزامها . قوله إلى مطلوبه ) أي : وهو صفاء القلب. (۱) أخرجه الإمام أحمد في مسنده» (٩٠/٦).
Its name: the science of tasawwuf. Its ruling: obligation. Its issues: the propositions in which its essential accidents are investigated, such as annihilation (fana') and subsistence (baqa'), watchfulness (muraqaba) and witnessing (mushahada), majesty (jalal) and beauty (jamal), and other than that. His statement: (expressed as religion) meaning: and the religious community (milla). His statement: (purity of heart) meaning: its purification from its stains and turbidities. His statement: with the etiquettes meaning: with performing them and adhering to them. His statement: to his goal meaning: and it is purity of heart. (1) Reported by Imam Ahmad in his Musnad (6/90).
Source pages p. 406

Commentary
This passage defines the science of tasawwuf. It first gives its name (ismuhu) as 'ilm al-tasawwuf, then its ruling (hukmuhu) as wajib (obligatory). It then lists its issues (masa'iluhu) as propositions investigating its essential accidents (a'rad dhatiyya), citing examples: fana' and baqa', muraqaba and mushahada, jalal and jamal. This shows that tasawwuf has a defined subject matter and methodology. The passage then includes commentary (qawlahu) on previous expressions: explaining 'expressed as religion' as 'and the milla', and 'purity of heart' as cleansing from stains, and 'with the etiquettes' as performing them, and 'to his goal' as purity of heart. It ends with a hadith reference.

Correction: The passage explicitly states that the ruling of tasawwuf is wajib (obligatory).

Adab with Brethren: Selfless Brotherhood
Lists etiquettes of a seeker towards his brethren

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the specific etiquettes a spiritual seeker should observe toward his fellow seekers, as a form of spiritual discipline.

Core Idea
True brotherhood in the spiritual path is built on selflessness and service.

This passage, though beginning mid-sentence, presents a beautiful summary of how a seeker should relate to his fellow travelers on the path. It is not about mere social niceties but about a deep spiritual discipline of preferring others, serving them, and maintaining a heart free from competition and self-interest.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice the extensive list of actions—this is a dense teaching.
The opening phrase 'نفسه بشيء دونهم' sets the tone of putting others before oneself.
2
Identify the underlying principle
The passage begins with 'يحب لهم ما يحب لنفسه' (loves for them what he loves for himself). This is the core principle from which all behaviors flow.
This is a direct reflection of the prophetic command.
3
List the concrete behaviors
Work through the passage and list each specific action: visiting the sick, greeting cheerfully, considering them better, etc. Notice the range from internal attitudes to external actions.
4
Reflect on the climax
The teaching ends with making forbearance (مسامحة) one's capital and serving even by presenting sandals. This shows that true brotherhood requires sacrifice and humility.
مسامحة here implies forgiving and overlooking faults.
5
Apply to your own interactions
Ask yourself: How can I transform my relationships with my spiritual brothers into a discipline of selflessness? Choose one behavior to implement this week.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نفسه بشيء دونهم، ويحب لهم ما يحب لنفسه، ويعودهم إذا مرضوا، إذا غابوا، ويبتدرهم بالسلام وطلاقة الوجه، وأن يراهم خيراً منه، ويطلب منهم الرضا ويسأل عنهم منه، ولا يزاحمهم على أمر دنيوي، بل يبذل لهم ما فتح عليه به، وأن يوقر كبيرهم، ويرحم صغيرهم، ويتعاون معهم على حب الله، وليجعل رأس ماله مسامحة إخوانه، يخدمهم ولو بتقديم النعال لهم.
Himself with something without them, and loves for them what he loves for himself, and visits them when they are sick, when they are absent, and precedes them with greeting and a cheerful face, and that he sees them as better than him, and seeks their pleasure and asks about them from him, and does not compete with them in worldly matters, rather he gives them what has been opened for him, and that he respects their elder, has mercy on their young, and cooperates with them in love of God, and let his capital be the forbearance of his brothers, he serves them even by presenting sandals to them.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage begins mid-sentence, but it is clearly part of the description of the etiquette (adab) of a spiritual seeker toward his brethren (fellow travelers on the path). It instructs that the seeker should prioritize his brothers, loving for them what he loves for himself, visiting them in sickness and when absent (asking about them), greeting them with a cheerful face, and considering them better than himself. He should seek their pleasure, not compete with them in worldly affairs, but give freely to them. He must honor the elder and show mercy to the younger, cooperate in love of God, make forbearance his capital, and serve them even in menial tasks like presenting sandals.

Misconception: These etiquettes are just general social courtesy.
Correction: They are specific spiritual disciplines intended to purify the heart and strengthen the bond of brotherhood in God.

C) Compete with them in worldly matters

Key Takeaway
A seeker's relationship with his brethren should be characterized by selfless love, service, and forbearance.

Adab with the General Public: Humility, Goodness, and Truthfulness
Enumerates four etiquettes toward the general public and links them to prior teachings.

Core Idea
Etiquette toward everyone is built on humility, generosity, peace, and integrity.

Having learned how to treat close brethren, the text now turns to the wider community. The seeker's courtesy must extend beyond the circle of fellow students to all people. This short passage encapsulates four foundational behaviors that summarize the spirit of Islamic social ethics.

Inner Asceticism and Avoiding Excess
Sanusi outlines foundational inner and outer disciplines.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the inner states of being occupied with Allah and asceticism, and the outer discipline of avoiding excess in permissible things.

Core Idea
True adab begins with inner detachment from the world and ends with moderation in worldly comforts.

This lesson explores the first set of personal etiquettes a seeker must cultivate. It begins with the heart's orientation: being constantly mindful of Allah and seeing the world as worthless. From this inner state flows the outer practice of avoiding even permissible excess in food, drink, clothing, marriage, and transportation, limiting oneself to what suffices.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the first part of the passage slowly. Notice the sequence of commands: inner states first, then outer actions.
The Arabic uses participles like مشغولاً, زاهداً, غاضاً, تاركاً, مقتصراً.
2
Identify the inner and outer elements
List what the passage says about inner states (occupied with Allah, ascetic, world worthless) and outer actions (lowering gaze, avoiding excess, limiting to sufficiency).
3
Reflect on 'excess of halal'
Contemplate why even permissible things can be excessive. What does 'sufficiency' mean in your own life?
The common misunderstanding is that this forbids all comfort; actually it forbids extravagance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما الآداب التي تتعلق به في نفسه: فأن يكون مشغولاً بالله زاهداً فيما سواه، غاضاً عن المحارم ليس للدنيا عنده قيمة، تاركاً لفضول الحلال؛ كالتوسعة في المأكل والمشرب والملبس والمنكح، والمركب مقتصراً على قدر الكفاية مديم الطهارة، لا ينام على جنابة، ولا يفضي بيده إلى عورته إلا في ضرورته، ولا يكشف عورته ولو بخلوة. ولا يطمع فيما في أيدي الناس، يحاسب نفسه على الدوام، لا يأكل إلا حلالاً، وهو ما جهل أصله يكابد نفسه عن النظر إلى الصور الجميلة النساء والأحداث؛ فإن تلك قواطع عن الله تعالى تسد باب الفتح، من أجارنا الله من ارتكابه.
As for the etiquettes that pertain to him in himself: that he be occupied with God, ascetic in what is other than Him, lowering his gaze from forbidden things, having no value for the world in his sight, abandoning excess of permissible things; like extravagance in food, drink, clothing, marriage, and mount, limiting himself to sufficiency, constantly maintaining purity, not sleeping in a state of janabah, not touching his private parts with his hand except in necessity, and not uncovering his private parts even in solitude. And he does not covet what is in people's hands, he holds himself accountable continuously, he eats only what is halal, and if he does not know its origin, he struggles his soul against looking at beautiful images of women and youths; for those are barriers from God Almighty, blocking the door of opening, may God protect us from committing it.
Source pages p. 409

Commentary
This passage enumerates the personal etiquettes a seeker must observe. It begins with inner states: being occupied with God (being mindful of Him) and being ascetic (zahid) in everything else. The seeker should lower his gaze from forbidden things, hold the world as worthless, and avoid even excess of what is permissible (e.g., too much food, drink, clothing, marriage, and transportation), limiting himself to what suffices. He must maintain ritual purity, avoid sleeping in a state of major impurity, and be modest. He should not covet others' possessions but hold himself accountable. He eats only lawful food; if unsure of its source, he abstains. He struggles his soul against looking at beautiful forms (women and youths) because such looks are barriers from God, blocking spiritual openings. The passage ends with a prayer for protection.

Key Terms
زهد
فضول الحلال
قدر الكفاية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Avoiding excess means one cannot enjoy any comfort in permissible things.
Correction: It means avoiding extravagance beyond need, not forbidding all comfort. The passage specifically says 'limiting to sufficiency', not complete deprivation.

Checkpoint

B) Excess in permissible things like food, drink, clothing

Key Takeaway
The seeker's adab begins with inner detachment and extends to moderate use of permissible things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?
The text says 'not for dunya any value.' But do you act as if dunya has value by chasing after luxuries? How would your spending change if you truly held that belief? Name one specific purchase you would cancel.

Sanusi continues listing personal etiquettes concerning purity, modesty, accountability, diet, and guarding the gaze.
