The Tranquil Soul (Nafs Mutma'inna)
Defines the tranquil soul and warns of hidden deceits.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will identify the characteristics of the tranquil soul (nafs mutma'inna) and understand that even this high station contains hidden deceits that can deceive the seeker into spiritual pride.

Core Idea
The tranquil soul, though near to God, still harbors subtle ego traps that only the spiritually illumined can detect.

The fourth stage of the soul is the tranquil soul, characterized by the replacement of blameworthy traits with praiseworthy ones and the adornment of divine attributes. This station is described as the beginning of reaching God, but the author warns that it is not free from deceits—deceits so subtle that they can make one believe they are the greatest imam.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the tranquil soul
Note the conditions: persistence in struggle, removal of desires, replacement of traits, and manifestation of divine attributes.
The soul becomes characterized by attributes like mercy, kindness, generosity.
2
Understand that this is the beginning of attainment
The text says this station is 'the beginning of attaining God Almighty'. Realize that it is not the end.
3
Identify the hidden deceits
The passage details subtle deceits: hidden polytheism, love of leadership. These are imperceptible to others because outward appearance is righteous.
Only those with illuminated insight can see them.
4
Recognize the specific danger of spiritual pride
The seeker may think he is the greatest imam. This is a major deception. The remedy is not mentioned here but implied earlier: reliance on shaykh.
The passage calls this 'among the deceits'.

Types of souls. If it (the soul) engages in struggle and exertion until it inclines toward the world of holiness and becomes illuminated so that it is inspired with its wickedness and piety, it is called the inspired soul (nafs mulhama), and it is the third. Its sign is that its possessor knows its subtle hidden deceits: from hypocrisy, self-admiration, and other things. If it persists in struggle until desires leave it, and blameworthy qualities are replaced with praiseworthy ones, and it becomes characterized by the beautiful attributes of God Almighty: compassion, mercy, kindness, generosity, and love, it is called the tranquil soul (nafs mutma'inna), and it is the fourth. This station is the beginning of attaining God Almighty, but it is not free from deceits. And his saying: ('it is called inspired, and it is the third'): meaning it is taken from His statement: (He inspired it with its wickedness and its piety) (1). His saying: ('and its sign is that its possessor knows its hidden deceits') – among its signs are longing, ecstasy, and intoxication, for in this station he is annihilated from all besides God. But this station is hazardous; usually no one escapes it except by completely relying on his shaykh. His saying: ('it is called tranquil, and it is the fourth') – this and what follows up to the seventh are taken from His statement: O tranquil soul, return to your Lord, well-pleased and pleasing, enter among My servants and enter My garden (2). His saying: ('it is the beginning of attainment') – meaning that is why they say: it is the first step that the seeker places. Very hidden, like hidden polytheism and love of leadership, except that due to their hiddenness and subtlety, only those whose insights God has illuminated perceive them; because their outward appearance is righteousness and being characterized with praiseworthy attributes like generosity, forbearance, trust, asceticism, piety, gratitude, patience, submission, and contentment with divine decree, along with the unveiling of some secrets, the breaking of some habits, and the appearance of some miracles (karamat). So perhaps its possessor may think he is the greatest Imam and that his station is the most magnificent station, and this is among the deceits. If divine care reaches him, and he completely relies on his shaykh, and persists in struggle until he becomes established in praiseworthy attributes, and the vein of hypocrisy is cut off, his soul becomes humble, praise and blame become equal to him, he enters the station of annihilation (fana'), and he is content with whatever occurs in existence with no objection at all – (this is) the fifth. It is called the content soul (nafs radiyya), and it is - but the vision of annihilation and sincerity may possibly cause some self-admiration, so he regresses. If he becomes annihilated from annihilation and freed from seeing sincerity, contentment manifests upon him, and He pardons all that has passed, and his sins are turned into good deeds, and the doors of spiritual tastes and manifestations open for him, and he becomes drowned in the oceans of divine unity (tawhid), and he becomes familiar with the seeker on the path, and before that he is called a seeker (murid). The Content Soul (Nafs Radiyya) and Annihilation
Defines the content soul and warns of the danger of self-admiration in annihilation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the content soul (nafs radiyya), its attributes including annihilation (fana'), and the danger of self-admiration from seeing one's own annihilation.

Core Idea
The content soul accepts all divine decrees without objection, but even the vision of one's own selflessness can become a trap.

The fifth stage is the content soul (nafs radiyya), achieved through divine care, reliance on the shaykh, and persistent struggle. The soul becomes humble, equal in praise and blame, and enters annihilation. Yet the author warns of a subtle danger: the awareness of one's own annihilation can breed self-admiration, causing regression. The remedy is constant remembrance and reliance on God and the shaykh.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the content soul
Note the conditions: divine care, reliance on shaykh, persistence in struggle, establishment in praiseworthy attributes, cutting off hypocrisy, humility, equality of praise and blame, entering annihilation, and complete contentment with all events.
This is the fifth stage.
2
Understand the danger of seeing one's annihilation
The vision of one's own annihilation (fana') and sincerity can lead to self-admiration. This is a subtle trap that can cause regression.
The passage says 'ربَّما أوقع في شيء من الإعجاب' – may cause some self-admiration.
3
Learn the remedy
Seek refuge in God, engage in constant remembrance (dhikr), and rely on the shaykh. Recognize that deliverance comes only through the shaykh's support.
4
Recognize the higher stage: annihilation from annihilation
If one becomes 'freed from seeing sincerity' and 'annihilated from annihilation', then true contentment manifests, sins become good deeds, and the soul drowns in tawhid.
This is the full realization of the content soul.

His saying: ('it is called tranquil, and it is the fourth') – this and what follows up to the seventh are taken from His statement: O tranquil soul, return to your Lord, well-pleased and pleasing, enter among My servants and enter My garden (2). His saying: ('it is the beginning of attainment') – meaning that is why they say: it is the first step that the seeker places. Very hidden, like hidden polytheism and love of leadership, except that due to their hiddenness and subtlety, only those whose insights God has illuminated perceive them; because their outward appearance is righteousness and being characterized with praiseworthy attributes like generosity, forbearance, trust, asceticism, piety, gratitude, patience, submission, and contentment with divine decree, along with the unveiling of some secrets, the breaking of some habits, and the appearance of some miracles (karamat). So perhaps its possessor may think he is the greatest Imam and that his station is the most magnificent station, and this is among the deceits. If divine care reaches him, and he completely relies on his shaykh, and persists in struggle until he becomes established in praiseworthy attributes, and the vein of hypocrisy is cut off, his soul becomes humble, praise and blame become equal to him, he enters the station of annihilation (fana'), and he is content with whatever occurs in existence with no objection at all – (this is) the fifth. It is called the content soul (nafs radiyya), and it is - but the vision of annihilation and sincerity may possibly cause some self-admiration, so he regresses. So he should seek refuge with God from that through constant remembrance and resorting to God, and observing that his deliverance is not completed except through the support of the shaykh. If he becomes annihilated from annihilation and freed from seeing sincerity, contentment manifests upon him, and He pardons all that has passed, and his sins are turned into good deeds, and the doors of spiritual tastes and manifestations open for him, and he becomes drowned in the oceans of divine unity (tawhid), and he becomes familiar with the seeker on the path, and before that he is called a seeker (murid). His saying: ('in the oceans of divine unity') – from the addition of the simile to the simile, and likewise his saying:
Source pages p. 420

Commentary
This passage is a classification and definition of three stages of the soul: the inspired soul (nafs mulhama), the tranquil soul (nafs mutma'inna), and the content soul (nafs radiyya). The author describes each stage's characteristics, signs, and hidden dangers. The passage begins by defining the third stage: the soul that through struggle in the spiritual path becomes illuminated and is inspired with its own vices and virtues. Its sign is that the person recognizes its subtle deceptions like hypocrisy and self-admiration. Then the author defines the fourth stage: the soul that persists in struggle until desires vanish, blameworthy traits are replaced with praiseworthy ones, and it is adorned with God's attributes. This station is the beginning of reaching God, but it contains hidden deceptions. The author then interjects commentary explaining that the names are derived from Quranic verses (Q. 91:8 and Q. 89:27-30). He also explains the signs of the third stage and warns that this stage is perilous, requiring reliance on a shaykh. For the fourth stage, he notes that it and the subsequent stages up to the seventh are taken from the Quranic verse about the tranquil soul. The author then elaborates on the hidden deceptions of the fourth stage: they are very subtle, like hidden polytheism and love of leadership; only those with divinely illuminated insight can perceive them because outwardly the person appears righteous with praiseworthy qualities and may even experience miraculous events. This can lead the person to think they are the greatest imam, which is itself a deception. The passage then moves to the fifth stage: when divine care reaches the person, they rely entirely on their shaykh, persist in struggle, become firm in praiseworthy attributes, break hypocrisy, become humble, equal in praise and blame, enter the state of annihilation (fana'), and are completely content with God's decree. This soul is called the content soul (radiyya). However, even this stage has a danger: seeing one's own annihilation and sincerity can cause self-admiration, leading to regression. The remedy is constant remembrance and reliance on God and the shaykh. Finally, if the person becomes annihilated from even the awareness of annihilation and frees themselves from seeing their own sincerity, then divine contentment manifests, sins are turned into good deeds, and they become immersed in the oceans of tawhid. At this point they are called a seeker (murid) who is familiar with the path. The passage ends with a grammatical commentary on the phrase 'oceans of tawhid.' Throughout, the author emphasizes that each stage has subtle hidden defects and stresses the necessity of a shaykh for guidance.

Key Terms
راضية
فناء
إعجاب
ذكر
تجليات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that reaching annihilation (fana') is the final and safe goal.
Correction: The passage warns that even in fana', seeing one's own state can cause self-admiration and regression. The safe state is beyond that.

Checkpoint
What is the danger that the passage warns about in the station of the content soul?
A) Falling back into worldly desires
B) Self-admiration from seeing one's own annihilation and sincerity
C) Losing faith in the shaykh
D) Becoming too attached to miracles

Key Takeaway
The content soul achieves deep peace and acceptance, but spiritual progress requires constant vigilance against the subtle ego that can reassert itself even in the midst of selflessness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When have you caught yourself feeling satisfaction in your own spiritual progress? What did you do with that feeling?
?
Are you willing to let go of the story that you are a 'humble seeker' if that very story is feeding your ego?
?
If your deliverance depends on the support of a shaykh, are you truly relying on one, or are you relying on your own idea of reliance?

The Sixth Soul (al-Nafs al-Mutma'innah) and Its Station
Describes the sixth soul and the seeker's journey beyond it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the attributes and station of the sixth soul (al-Nafs al-Mutma'innah) and why the true seeker does not stop at it.

Core Idea
The peaceful soul is not the final stop; the true seeker pushes beyond to union with Allah.

In this passage, the author describes the sixth soul, called al-Nafs al-Mutma'innah (the peaceful soul) and also al-Nafs al-Mardiyyah (the well-pleasing soul). However, he immediately cautions that the one of high aspiration does not rest here but continues from annihilation to subsistence. The source passage explains the spiritual journey beyond this station, with references to Quranic verses and poetry.

Commentary
This passage is a descriptive and explanatory passage about the sixth soul (النفس المطمئنة) and its station. It begins by stating that this soul is called 'مرضية' (well-pleasing) because it is under the sixth level of divine care. However, the one of high aspiration does not stop at this station, but seeks further, moving from annihilation (الفناء) to subsistence (البقاء) and desiring perfect union. The realities of existents remind him that they are but a trial and that the ultimate goal is God. When he reaches the stations of the valiant and abandons the world, God calls him with the verse from Surah al-Fajr: 'O soul at peace, return to your Lord...' The commentary then explains that 'بلابل الأسرار' (stirrings of secrets) refers to the motives for nearness to God, and 'بالتغريد' (warbling) originally means the beautiful sound of nightingales. The phrase 'فتناديه حقائق الأكوان' means the essences of things call out to him, and 'وأن إلى ربك المنتهى' means not to turn to other than God. The passage includes a poem by Ibn al-Farid illustrating the idea of singling out God's love and avoiding any attention to other than Him. Finally, the commentary notes that despite completing struggle and endurance, one is not safe, implying the need for constant vigilance.

The Perfect Soul and the Three Levels of Certainty
Defines the seventh soul and enumerates the three levels of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the seventh soul (النفس الكاملة) and the three stages of certainty (علم اليقين, حق اليقين, عين اليقين) and how they relate to spiritual ascent.

Core Idea
Certainty comes in three levels: knowledge, truth, and direct vision.

In this passage, the author describes the highest stage of the soul, the perfect soul, and its continuous ascent. He then explains the three levels of certainty that the soul attains: knowledge through proofs (علم اليقين), witnessing God in everything (حق اليقين), and direct vision before all creation (عين اليقين). This lesson will unpack these terms and show how they build on each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its English translation once. Pay attention to the terms for the soul and the levels of certainty.
The passage uses technical terms that are explained in the commentary.
2
Identify the three levels of certainty
Find the names of the three levels: علم اليقين, حق اليقين, عين اليقين. Note how each is defined in the text.
علم اليقين is knowing through proofs, حق اليقين is witnessing in everything, عين اليقين is witnessing before all.
3
Understand the relationship with the soul
Notice that the perfect soul (النفس الكاملة) never stops ascending. It attains these levels in order: first علم اليقين, then حق اليقين, then عين اليقين.
The soul's progression is endless because the perfect accepts perfection.
4
Examine the synonymous terms
The text equates عين اليقين with المعاينة and المراقبة, and مشاهادة with حق اليقين. Understand how these terms overlap.
This clarifies that multiple spiritual states refer to the same reality.
5
Reflect on the overall message
The passage ends with a poetic reminder that creation points to the Creator. Consider how this underlines the journey of certainty.
The highest certainty is direct vision, but it is rooted in knowledge and reflection.

The attributes of perfection become for it as a nature and disposition, and the soul in it is called the perfect (الكاملة), the seventh, and it is the greatest of souls in rank and the most complete in glory. Yet its ascent never ceases forever, because the perfect accepts perfection. It continues to ascend until it witnesses the Truth, exalted, before all existents. And witnessing Him, exalted, before everything is called among them 'المعاينة' (inspection), and this is 'عين اليقين' (the eye of certainty), after it has attained 'علم اليقين' (knowledge of certainty) – which is knowing Him, exalted, through proofs – then 'حق اليقين' (truth of certainty) – which is witnessing Him, exalted, in everything, without [?] itself, rather He constantly cares for it and nurtures it. Al-Sayyid al-Bakri said: 'The soul is a living entity striving, even if it has reached its seven stations.' His statement: (it is called among them 'المعاينة') means 'المراقبة' (vigilance). His statement: (after it has attained 'علم اليقين') means that it was characterized by that before entering the مطمئنة (tranquil) soul. His statement: (and it is witnessing Him, exalted, in everything) means it is called in their terminology 'المشاهدة' (direct witnessing). So it becomes clear that 'المراقبة' is also called 'المعاينة': he witnesses God before all existents, then establishes them through Him because they are His effects, as some gnostics pointed out with his saying, from the light meter: 'These traces of us indicate us, so look after us at the traces.'

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the seventh and highest soul (النفس الكاملة) along with the three levels of certainty. It begins by stating that the attributes of perfection become natural for this soul. It is called the 'perfect soul' and is the seventh and greatest in rank. Yet its ascent never stops because the perfect is open to further perfection. It continues to ascend until it witnesses God before all creation. This witnessing is called 'المعاينة' (inspection) and is 'عين اليقين' (eye of certainty). Prior to this, the soul had attained 'علم اليقين' (knowledge of certainty) which is knowledge of God through proofs, and then 'حق اليقين' (truth of certainty) which is witnessing God in everything. The commentary then clarifies that 'المعاينة' is synonymous with 'المراقبة' (vigilance), and 'المشاهدة' (direct witnessing) is another term. The passage concludes with a saying of al-Sayyid al-Bakri that the soul is ever-striving even if it has reached its seven stations, and a poem indicating that God's creation are His traces pointing to Him.

The Seventh Soul and the Stages of Certainty
Al-Sawi describes the seventh soul and the three degrees of certainty.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the nature of the seventh soul and the three degrees of certainty in the spiritual ascent.

Core Idea
The perfect soul never stops ascending, witnessing God before all things.

This passage from Al-Sawi explains the final stage of the soul's purification—the perfect soul—and the three levels of certainty that mark its journey. You will see how spiritual certainty deepens from intellectual knowledge to direct vision, and how the soul's perfection is never static but always growing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
2
Identify the seventh soul and its attributes
Find how Al-Sawi describes the perfect soul: it is the greatest, most perfect, yet always ascending. Notice the phrase 'ترقّيها أبداً' (its ascent never ceases).
The soul's perfection is dynamic, not static.
3
Track the three degrees of certainty
The passage names three degrees: علم اليقين, حق اليقين, and عين اليقين. Identify what each corresponds to in the soul's journey: knowledge through proofs, witnessing in everything, and direct vision before creation.
Notice that عين اليقين is equated with المعاينة (mu'ayana).
4
Understand the commentary on key terms
Al-Sawi glosses: mu'ayana is muraqaba (watchfulness), and witnessing God in everything is mushahada. See how these terms interrelate.
The soul first watches God before creation, then sees creation as His effects.
5
Reflect on the poetic verse
The poem 'هذه آثارنا تدل علينا' illustrates that creation points to the Creator. Consider how this confirms the third degree of certainty.

The attributes of perfection became for it nature and disposition, and the soul is called in it the perfect one, the seventh, and it is the greatest of souls in rank, and the most perfect in glory. Yet its ascent never ceases, because the perfect one accepts perfection, so it keeps ascending until it witnesses the Truth (Exalted) before the cosmic beings. And witnessing Him (Exalted) before everything is called among them 'mu'ayana' (vision), and this is 'ayn al-yaqin' (certainty of vision), after it attained 'ilm al-yaqin' (certainty of knowledge)—which is knowledge of Him (Exalted) through proofs—then 'haqq al-yaqin' (certainty of truth)—which is witnessing Him (Exalted) in everything without [being veiled by] itself, but rather He constantly cares for it and nurtures it. Al-Sayyid al-Bakri said: 'The soul is alive and active, even if it has attained its seven stations.' His statement: (It is called among them 'mu'ayana'): meaning 'muraqaba' (watchfulness). His statement: (After it attained 'ilm al-yaqin'): meaning that it was characterized by it before entering the 'mutma'inna' (contented soul). His statement: (And it is witnessing Him (Exalted) in everything): meaning it is what in their terminology is called 'mushahada' (direct witnessing). Thus it becomes clear that muraqaba is also called mu'ayana: he witnesses God before the cosmic beings, then establishes them through Him, because they are His effects, as some gnostics indicated in his saying (from al-Khafif): 'These are our traces that indicate us; so look, after us, at the traces.'
Source pages p. 423

Commentary
This passage is a definition and explanation of the seventh soul (an-nafs al-kamila) and the three degrees of certainty. It begins by describing the characteristics of the perfect soul: it attains perfection as its nature, it is the greatest of souls, yet it never stops ascending. It ascends until it witnesses God before creation. This witnessing is termed 'mu'ayana' (vision) and is identified as 'ayn al-yaqin' (the highest certainty). Before this, the soul had previously attained 'ilm al-yaqin' (certainty through proofs) and 'haqq al-yaqin' (certainty of truth), which is seeing God in everything without self-veiling. The glossator then comments on specific phrases: he equates 'mu'ayana' with 'muraqaba' (watchfulness), states that 'ilm al-yaqin' is the state before entering the contented soul (mutma'inna), and clarifies that witnessing God in everything is 'mushahada'. Thus, muraqaba/mu'ayana involves witnessing God before creation and then seeing creation as His effects. A poetic verse is cited to illustrate that creation points to the Creator.

Key Terms
النفس الكاملة
عين اليقين
علم اليقين
حق اليقين
المعاينة
المراقبة
المشاهدة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Thinking the three degrees of certainty are merely intellectual or epistemological concepts without spiritual transformation.
Correction: Each degree corresponds to a real station (maqām) of the soul: ʿilm al-yaqīn is before entering the contented soul, haqq al-yaqīn involves witnessing God in all things, and ʿayn al-yaqīn is direct vision before creation.

Checkpoint
What is the highest degree of certainty mentioned in this passage?
A) علم اليقين (ilm al-yaqin)
B) حق اليقين (haqq al-yaqin)
C) عين اليقين (ayn al-yaqin)
D) المشاهدة (mushahada)

Key Takeaway
The perfect soul attains the highest certainty—direct vision of God—after progressing through knowledge and witnessing Him in all things.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have read that the perfect soul never stops ascending and that it witnesses God before all things. Are you content with merely knowing about God through proofs, or do you long for that direct vision? What is the one attachment to this world that is veiling you from that closeness right now? Write it down and commit to a specific act of detachment today.
?
The passage says the soul reaches a point where it sees God in everything, not from itself. Do you live as if every moment and every created thing is a sign pointing to Him, or do you often forget and see things as independent? What would change in your next hour if you truly believed you could see God in every person and event? Choose one small way to practice this awareness before your next prayer.
?
Al-Sayyid al-Bakri says: 'The soul is alive and active even if it has attained its seven stations.' Do you feel your spiritual journey has become stagnant? What regular spiritual practice have you abandoned or neglected that once lifted you? Resolve to revive it tonight, no matter how small.

The Station of Mushahada: Witnessing God
Al-Sawi defines mushahada and describes its characteristics and the state of its possessor.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that mushahada is a spiritual vision of God in all things without incarnation or union, and the state of the one who attains it.

Core Idea
Mushahada is seeing God everywhere like a reflection, not a union.

Al-Sawi begins by defining the highest spiritual station of mushahada. He immediately clarifies what it is not: it is not incarnation or union, but rather a direct witnessing likened to seeing your face in a mirror. This experiential station transforms the believer, making worship natural and preserving them from sin. The passage explains the state of the one who attains this station and addresses a potential misunderstanding about the removal of obligation.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the definition and key negations. Notice the mirror simile and the quote from Sayyidi Muhammad Wafa.
Pay attention to the phrase 'لا حلول ولا اتّحاد' — it sets the boundaries.
2
Understand the mirror simile
The passage compares mushahada to seeing your face in a mirror: no incarnation or union, just a clear reflection. This illustrates the nature of spiritual witnessing.
3
Examine the state of the possessor
The one who attains mushahada worships constantly and is preserved from sin because of constant awareness of God. The quote from Muhammad Wafa emphasizes preservation after annihilation and subsistence.
The phrase 'فهو محفوظ من الوقوع في المخالفات' is key.
4
Clarify the highest station
The passage concludes that mushahada is seeing God in everything, and its possessor is called 'ahl al-jam' wa al-farq'. It is the highest station. The glossator explains that 'be however you wish' does not remove obligation but indicates preservation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشاهدة: لا حلول ولا اتّحاد، ولا اتصال ولا انفصال؛ كالمرآة ترى فيها وجهك من غير حلول الوجه فيها ولا اتحاد، وهذا مشهد ذوقي لا يدركه إلَّا أهله - وصاحب هذا المقام لا يفتر عن العبادة لأنها صارت طبعه، إما باللسان وإما بالجنان وإمّا بالأركان فحركاته حسنات، وأنفاسه عبادات، ولذا قال سيدي محمد وفا أبو سيدي علي وفا: (وبعد الفنا بالله كن كيفما تشا فعلمك لا جهل وفعلك لا وزر) فهو محفوظ من الوقوع في المخالفات؛ لحضوره دائماً الله في الحالات. وأنَّ المشاهدة هي : أن يرى الله في كل شيء، فلا تحجبه رؤية الله عنها، ولا يحجب بها عن الله ، ويقال لصاحبها : من أهل الجمع والفرق، وهو أعلى المقامات. قوله: (وبعد الفناء بالله ... إلخ) : أي : بعد انقضاء الفناء وثبوت البقاء، سواء كان في المراقبة أو المشاهدة. وقوله : (كن) كيفما تشا ليس المقصود رفع التكليف عنه، وإِنَّما المقصود بيان حفظه من الزَّلل بدليل قوله : (فعلمك لا جهل وفعلك لا وزر) وهو بمعنى قول ابن الفارض: (فليصنع القوم ما شاؤوا لأنفسهم هم أهل بدر فلا يخشون من حرج) وقد وضَّحه الشَّارح بقوله : ( فهو محفوظ . . . إلخ ) .
Witnessing (mushahada): no incarnation (hulul) nor union (ittihad), no conjunction nor separation; like a mirror in which you see your face without the face incarnating in it or uniting. This is a taste-based station (mashhad dhawqi) that only its people perceive. And the possessor of this station never ceases in worship because it has become his nature, either with the tongue, or with the heart, or with the limbs. His movements are good deeds, his breaths are acts of worship. For that reason, Sayyidi Muhammad Wafa, father of Sayyidi Ali Wafa said: 'After annihilation (fana') in God, be however you wish; your knowledge is without ignorance, your action is without sin.' So he is preserved from falling into violations due to his constant presence with God in all states. And that mushahada is: to see God in everything, so that seeing God does not veil him from things, nor does it veil him from God. The possessor of this station is called 'of the people of gathering and separation' (ahl al-jam' wa al-farq), and it is the highest of stations. His statement: (After annihilation in God... etc.): meaning after the passing of annihilation and the establishment of subsistence (baqa'), whether in muraqaba or mushahada. And his statement: (Be) however you wish does not mean the removal of obligation from him, but rather it indicates his preservation from error, as evidenced by his statement: (Your knowledge is without ignorance and your action is without sin). And it is equivalent to the saying of Ibn al-Farid: 'Let the people do whatever they want for themselves; they are the people of Badr, they do not fear a harmful consequence.' And the commentator clarified it with his statement: (So he is preserved... etc.).
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage defines the spiritual station of mushahada (witnessing). It begins by negating any notion of incarnation (hulul) or union (ittihad), conjunction or separation; the experience is likened to seeing one's face in a mirror without physical union. This is an experiential (dhawqi) station known only to its adepts. The person in this station worships constantly—with tongue, heart, and limbs—because worship has become second nature; every movement and breath is an act of worship. A quote from Sayyidi Muhammad Wafa emphasizes that after annihilation (fana) and subsistence (baqa), the person is preserved from sin due to constant awareness of God. Then the definition is restated: mushahada is seeing God in everything without being veiled by things from God or by God from things. The possessor is called 'ahl al-jam' wa al-farq' (people of gathering and separation), which is the highest station. The glossator then explains that the phrase 'be however you wish' does not remove obligation but indicates preservation from error, citing Ibn al-Farid's verse about the people of Badr being secure from harm. The commentator reinforces that the person is 'preserved' (mahfuz).

Key Terms
المشاهدة
الحلول
الاتحاد
الجمع والفرق
الفناء والبقاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mushahada involves incarnation (hulul) or union (ittihad) with God.
Correction: The passage explicitly denies such notions, comparing it instead to seeing a reflection in a mirror.

Checkpoint
How does the passage describe the relationship of mushahada to incarnation and union?
A) It involves a partial union
B) It involves a complete union
C) It involves no incarnation or union
D) It involves separation without connection

Key Takeaway
Mushahada is witnessing God in all things through spiritual seeing, not through physical union or incarnation, and it is the highest station of the spiritual journey.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you see God in everything around you, or do you find that the world veils you from Him? What would change in your daily life if you truly saw every moment as a reflection of God's presence?
?
The text says the possessor of mushahada worships continuously with tongue, heart, and limbs. Is your own worship like a natural instinct, or do you struggle to maintain it? What would it take for worship to become your very nature?
?
The author denies that after annihilation one is free from obligations. Are you quick to claim spiritual exemption from rules? Examine your heart: do you want the ease of license or the preservation that comes from true witnessing?

Mushahada: Definition and Spiritual States
Al-Sawi defines mushahada and explains the state of fana and baqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of mushahada as a non-physical witnessing, the state of fana and baqa, and the preservation of the spiritual adept.

Core Idea
Mushahada is seeing God everywhere without incarnation, union, or separation.

This passage from Al-Sawi's commentary defines the spiritual station of mushahada, distinguishing it from pantheistic concepts. He explains the transformation that occurs after fana leads to baqa, where the servant is preserved from sin. The lesson clarifies that this state does not suspend religious obligations.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's own wording convey the meaning.
Notice the negations at the beginning.
2
Understand the mirror analogy
Focus on the mirror analogy: seeing your face in a mirror without incarnation. This illustrates that mushahada is a spiritual perception, not physical.
This addresses a common misconception.
3
Analyze the quote and its clarification
Read the quote from Muhammad Wafa and Al-Sawi's explanation. Pay attention to how he clarifies that 'be however you wish' does not remove religious obligations.
The quote is often misunderstood; the commentary corrects it.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المشاهدة حلول ولا اتّحاد، ولا اتصال ولا انفصال؛ كالمرآة ترى فيها وجهك من غير حلول الوجه فيها ولا اتحاد، وهذا مشهد ذوقي لا يدركه إلَّا أهله - وصاحب هذا المقام لا يفتر عن العبادة لأنها صارت طبعه، إما باللسان وإما بالجنان وإمّا بالأركان فحركاته حسنات، وأنفاسه عبادات، ولذا قال سيدي محمد وفا أبو سيدي علي وفا: وبعد الفنا بالله كن كيفما تشا فعلمك لا جهل وفعلك لا وزر. فهو محفوظ من الوقوع في المخالفات؛ لحضوره دائماً الله في الحالات. وأنَّ المشاهدة هي : أن يرى الله في كل شيء، فلا تحجبه رؤية الله عنها، ولا يحجب بها عن الله ، ويقال لصاحبها : من أهل الجمع والفرق، وهو أعلى المقامات. قوله: (وبعد الفناء بالله ... إلخ) : أي : بعد انقضاء الفناء وثبوت البقاء، سواء كان في المراقبة أو المشاهدة. وقوله : (كن) كيفما تشا ليس المقصود رفع التكليف عنه، وإِنَّما المقصود بيان حفظه من الزَّلل بدليل قوله : (فعلمك لا جهل وفعلك لا وزر) وهو بمعنى قول ابن الفارض: فليصنع القوم ما شاؤوا لأنفسهم هم أهل بدر فلا يخشون من حرج. وقد وضَّحه الشَّارح بقوله : ( فهو محفوظ . . . إلخ ) .
Al-mushahada (witnessing) is not incarnation (hulul) nor union (ittihad), nor connection (ittisal) nor separation (infisal); like the mirror in which you see your face without the face incarnating in it nor union. And this is a taste-based witnessing (mashhad dhawqi) that only its people perceive. And the possessor of this station does not cease from worship because it has become his nature, either by the tongue, or by the heart, or by the limbs; his movements are good deeds and his breaths are acts of worship. Thus Sayyidi Muhammad Wafa, the father of Sayyidi Ali Wafa, said: 'After annihilation (fana) in God, be however you wish; your knowledge is not ignorance and your action is not sin.' So he is protected from falling into violations due to his constant presence of God in all states. And al-mushahada is: that he sees God in everything, so neither does seeing God veil him from things, nor does it veil him from God. And its possessor is called one of the people of gathering and separation (ahl al-jam' wal-farq), and it is the highest of stations. His saying: '(And after annihilation in God... etc.)' means: after the passing of annihilation and the establishment of subsistence (baqa), whether it be in vigilance (muraqaba) or witnessing. And his saying: 'Be however you wish' does not intend the removal of obligation (taklif) from him, but rather intends to explain his preservation from slipping, evidenced by his saying: 'your knowledge is not ignorance and your action is not sin.' And it is similar to the saying of Ibn al-Farid: 'Let the people do what they wish for themselves; they are the people of Badr, so they do not fear distress.' And the commentator clarified it with his saying: 'So he is protected... etc.'
Source pages p. 424

Commentary
This passage defines the spiritual state of mushahada (witnessing) and explains the condition of one who has passed through fana (annihilation) to baqa (subsistence). The passage begins by negating any physical or pantheistic interpretation: mushahada is not incarnation (hulul), union (ittihad), connection (ittisal), or separation (infisal). It is likened to seeing one's face in a mirror without the face actually entering the mirror—a pure metaphorical likeness. This is a taste-based (dhawqi) spiritual experience accessible only to its adepts. The person in this state does not cease worship because it has become his second nature, performing it with tongue, heart, or limbs such that all his movements and breaths are good deeds. A quote from Sayyidi Muhammad Wafa contrasts the state before and after fana: after annihilation in God, the person is preserved from sin. The passage then gives a formal definition: mushahada is seeing God in everything without being veiled from things or from God. The possessor of this state is called 'ahl al-jam' wal-farq' (people of gathering and separation), and it is the highest station. The commentary then explains the line 'Wa ba'da al-fana billah kun kayfa ma shi'ta': it refers to the period after fana has passed and baqa is established, whether in muraqaba or mushahada. The phrase 'kun kayfa ma shi'ta' does not mean the person is free from religious obligations (taklif), but rather indicates his preservation from error, as the line itself says his knowledge is not ignorance and his action is not sin. This is compared to Ibn al-Farid's verse about the people of Badr being fearless. The commentator confirms this with his remark 'fahuwa mahfuz' (so he is protected).

حلول (hulul)
اتحاد (ittihad)
فناء (fana)
بقاء (baqa)
مراقبة (muraqaba)
جمع وفرق (jam' wal-farq)
تكليف (taklif)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Mushahada involves incarnation or union with God (hulul/ittihad).
Correction: Al-Sawi explicitly negates hulul and ittihad, using the mirror analogy to show that witnessing is a spiritual perception without physical connection.

Checkpoint
What does al-Sawi emphasize about the state of mushahada regarding incarnation and union?
A) It is a form of union with God.
B) It is similar to incarnation.

D) It involves separation from God.

Key Takeaway
Mushahada is a spiritual witnessing of God in all things, distinct from physical union, and the person who achieves it is protected from sin but still bound by religious obligations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The one who truly witnesses God does not cease from worship; his movements and breaths become good deeds. But you and I often feel that spiritual experiences make us lazy about outward prayer. When have you used a feeling of closeness to God as an excuse to skip a prayer or delay an obligation? What is one specific act of worship you will perform today, even if you don't feel close, to prove that your claim of witnessing is not a lie?
?
The passage warns that the phrase 'be however you wish' does not mean you can do anything you want. Think of the last time you gave yourself permission to sin by thinking you were 'spiritual enough' to be above the rules. What was that sin? Will you repent from it now and recommit to the Sharia that God has placed on you, no matter how high your station?

The Rarity of the Kamilin: A Call to Struggle, Not Despair
Al-Sawi explains the rarity of the kamilin and refutes discouragement by clarifying the true purpose is incitement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the rarity of spiritual perfection is mentioned in Islamic texts—not to discourage, but to incite believers to greater effort.

Core Idea
Rarity is not a reason to give up; it is a mark of the path's value.

Al-Sawi addresses a common reaction when hearing about the rarity of the spiritually complete (al-kamilin). Some might feel it is hopeless. But he clarifies that the author's intent is precisely the opposite: to motivate believers to strive for those lofty stations, not to turn away. This lesson unpacks that critical distinction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Notice the descending order: salikun, wasilun, kamilun – each group smaller.
The passage begins with a stark statement.
2
Identify the cause of rarity
Look for the reason given: the journey is extremely difficult, requiring high aspiration and complete sincerity, and involves abandoning comforts.
3
Examine the clarification and evidence
Find where the commentator explicitly says the intent is not discouragement but incitement. Note the Quranic verse and the poetry of Ibn al-Farid.
The verse and poem reinforce the command to struggle.
4
Understand the conclusion
Connect the analysis back to the main point: mentioning rarity is meant to urge believers forward, not hold them back.
This resolves the apparent paradox.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ الكاملين في النَّاس من أقل الأقل، إذ السَّالكون إلى الله تعالى من المؤمنين قليلون، والواصلون منهم قليلون والكاملون منهم قليلون، إذ السَّير إلى الله تعالى صعب جداً لا يقدر عليه إلَّا ذو همَّة عليَّة وصدق كامل، إذ ترك المألوفات من الطعام والمنام وجمع الجاه وسائر الشَّهوات لا يقدر عليه إلَّا القليل من الأبطال، والطَّريقُ فيها مفاوز ومهلكات فالنَّاجي فيها قليل، ولذا قيل: كيف الوصول إلى سعاد .... قوله : (واعلم أنَّ الكاملين . . . إلخ) ليس قصد الشارح بتلك العبارة التنفير من مجاهدة النَّفس، بل هي مأمور بها ممدوح عليها سلك أو لم سلك؛ لقوله تعالى: وَأَمَّا مَنْ خَافَ مَقَامَ رَبِّهِ، وَنَهَى النَّفْسَ عَنِ أهْوَى... الآية ، وإنَّما المقصود زيادة التّحريض على تلك المقامات السَّنية نظير قول ابن الفارض: هو الحبُّ فاسلم بالحشا ما الهوى سهل ... إلى أن قال : ... نصحتك علماً بالهوى والذي أرى مخالفتي فاختر لنفسك ما يحلو. قوله : ( ولذا قيل) : أي : قولاً صحيحاً لبعض العارفين . قوله : (كيف الوصول ... إلخ ) : استفهام تعجبي استبعادي، وسعاد
And know that the complete ones (al-kamilin) among people are the least of the least. For those journeying to God from the believers are few, and those who arrive among them are few, and the complete among them are few. For the journey to God is very difficult; none can undertake it except one of high aspiration and complete sincerity. Indeed, abandoning accustomed things – food, sleep, accumulation of status, and all desires – none can do it except the few of the champions. And the path contains wildernesses and destructive places, so the one saved in it is few. Therefore it was said: 'How is the arrival to Su'ad...?' His saying: '(And know that the complete ones... etc.)' – the commentator's intention with this expression is not to discourage striving against the self (mujahadat al-nafs); rather, it is commanded and praised whether one traverses the path or not, due to His saying: 'But as for he who feared the station of his Lord and forbade the soul from desire...' [79:40-41]. The intended meaning is to increase incitement (tahrid) toward those lofty stations, analogous to the saying of Ibn al-Farid: 'It is love, so submit to it in the depths; love is not easy...' up to his saying: '...I advise you with knowledge of love, and what I see is my opposition to you, so choose for yourself what pleases you.' His saying: '(Therefore it was said)' means: a true saying of some arifs (gnostics). His saying: '(How is the arrival... etc.)' is an interrogative of wonder indicating its improbability, and Su'ad...
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage addresses the rarity of those who reach spiritual perfection (al-kamilin) and clarifies the purpose of mentioning this. The passage begins with a stark statement: those who are complete among people are the least of the least. It explains that the number of believers who set out on the path to God is small, those who actually arrive (al-wasilun) are even fewer, and those who become complete are the fewest of all. The reason given is the extreme difficulty of the spiritual journey: it requires high aspiration (himma 'aliyya) and complete sincerity (sidq kamil). It demands abandoning all comfortable habits – food, sleep, status, and all desires – which only a few heroic individuals (al-abtal) can do. The path is full of wildernesses (mafawiz) and destructive places (muhlikat), so only a few are saved. A poetic line is quoted: 'How is the arrival to Su'ad...?' (with dots indicating missing lines). The commentary then explains that the author's (al-sharih) intent in saying this is not to discourage people from engaging in struggle against the self (mujahadat al-nafs). On the contrary, such struggle is commanded and praiseworthy whether one completes the path or not, as evidenced by the Quranic verse about fearing the station of one's Lord and forbidding the soul from desire. The real purpose is to increase incitement (tahrid) toward these lofty stations. This is compared to the poetry of Ibn al-Farid, which speaks of love being difficult yet urges submission to it. The commentator then glosses 'wa lidha qila' as a true saying from some gnostics, and interprets the question 'kayfa al-wusul ila Su'ad' as an expression of wonder and improbability.

Key Terms
كاملون (kamilun)
سالكون (salikun)
واصلون (wasilun)
مجاهدة النفس (mujahadat al-nafs)
تحريض (tahrid)
همة عليّة (himma 'aliyya)
صدق كامل (sidq kamil)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the statement about the rarity of the kamilin is meant to discourage people from spiritual struggle because it seems too hard.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the purpose is incitement (tahrid), not discouragement (tanfir). Striving against the self is commanded and praised regardless of outcome.

Checkpoint

A) To discourage people from striving against the self.
B) To show that only a few can succeed, so don’t bother.
C) To incite and encourage people toward the lofty stations.
D) To prove that most believers will become complete.

Key Takeaway
Mentioning the difficulty and rarity of spiritual perfection is meant to inspire, not to discourage; the path is hard but the call is to strive.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The commentator says that the mention of rarity is meant to incite, not discourage. In your own spiritual journey, have you ever used the excuse 'it's too hard' or 'I'm not one of the few' to avoid real struggle? What would change today if you took the rarity as a challenge rather than a barrier?
?
Ibn al-Farid's poetry says: 'It is love, so submit to it in the depths; love is not easy... choose for yourself what pleases you.' Are you truly choosing the path of love, or are you settling for comfort? What is one comfortable habit you need to abandon today for the sake of Allah?

Rarity of Perfect Ones: Encouragement, Not Discouragement
Commentary clarifies rarity of perfect ones is to encourage striving, not discourage.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentary reframes the rarity of perfect spiritual seekers as motivation rather than discouragement, and why self-struggle is commanded regardless of outcome.

Core Idea
Rarity of perfect ones is an invitation to strive, not an excuse to give up.

The original text paints a stark picture: few even begin the journey to Allah, fewer reach the destination, and fewer still attain perfection. Reading this, one might feel despair. But the commentator immediately intervenes to correct that impression. In this lesson, we will see how he transforms a potentially discouraging statement into a powerful source of motivation, grounding his interpretation in the Quran and the poetry of Ibn al-Farid.

Guided Path
1
Read the original statement and translation
Read the Arabic passage from 'Know that the perfect ones' to 'the survivor in it is few'. Let the weight of the difficulty sink in. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the commentator's corrective move
Notice the shift from the original statement to the commentator's explanation. He explicitly denies the intention of discouragement and instead asserts that self-struggle is commanded and praised. Locate his supporting evidence from the Quran.
The verse he cites is Surah An-Nazi'at (79:40-41).
3
Explore the poetic analogy
Read the closing simile with Ibn al-Farid's poem. Why does the commentator compare the spiritual struggle to love? What effect does this have on the reader?
The poem's message is that love is difficult but worth the effort.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ الكاملين في النَّاس من أقل الأقل، إذ السَّالكون إلى الله تعالى من المؤمنين قليلون، والواصلون منهم قليلون والكاملون منهم قليلون، إذ السَّير إلى الله تعالى صعب جداً لا يقدر عليه إلَّا ذو همَّة عليَّة وصدق كامل، إذ ترك المألوفات من الطعام والمنام وجمع الجاه وسائر الشَّهوات لا يقدر عليه إلَّا القليل من الأبطال، والطَّريقُ فيها مفاوز ومهلكات فالنَّاجي فيها قليل، ولذا قيل (۱): [من الكامل] كيف الوصول إلى سعاد .... قوله : (واعلم أنَّ الكاملين . . . إلخ) ليس قصد الشارح بتلك العبارة التنفير من مجاهدة النَّفس، بل هي مأمور بها ممدوح عليها سلك أو لم سلك؛ لقوله تعالى: وَأَمَّا مَنْ خَافَ مَقَامَ رَبِّهِ، وَنَهَى النَّفْسَ عَنِ أهْوَى... الآية (٢) ، وإنَّما المقصود زيادة التّحريض على تلك المقامات من الطويل] السَّنية نظير قول ابن الفارض (۳) هو الحبُّ فاسلم بالحشا ما الهوى سهل إلى أن قال : .... .... نصحتك علماً بالهوى والذي أرى مخالفتي فاختر لنفسك ما يحلو
Know that the perfect ones among people are the fewest of the few, for those journeying to Allah Almighty among the believers are few, those who arrive among them are few, and those who are perfect among them are few. Indeed, the journey to Allah Almighty is very difficult, none can accomplish it except one with high aspiration and complete truthfulness, for leaving familiar things like food, sleep, accumulation of status, and all desires can only be accomplished by the few of the heroes. The path has deserts and mortal dangers, so the survivor in it is few. Hence a saying (1): [In the meter of Kamil] "How can one reach Su'ad..." ... His statement: "Know that the perfect ones... etc." — the commentator did not intend by that statement to drive away from self-struggle; rather it is commanded and praised, whether one traverses it or not, due to His statement the Exalted: "But as for he who feared the standing before his Lord and forbade the soul from [unlawful] inclination..." the verse (2). Rather, the intent is to increase incitement towards those noble stations, similar to the saying of Ibn al-Farid (3): "Love is easy? No, love is surrender to the innermost self" until he said: "... I have advised you with knowledge of love, and what I see is my opposing you, so choose for yourself what is sweet."
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the original statement about the rarity of perfect spiritual seekers. The commentator first quotes the original text, which emphasizes that only a few of the believers even set out on the spiritual path, fewer reach the destination, and fewer still attain perfection. It highlights the immense difficulty of leaving behind worldly comforts and desires. Then the commentator explains that the author's purpose is not to discourage people from self-struggle (mujahadat al-nafs); rather, such struggle is commanded and praiseworthy regardless of outcome. He cites the Quranic verse (79:40-41) about those who fear their Lord and restrain their souls from desire. The commentator clarifies that the intent is to increase motivation (tahrid) towards these high stations, not to create despair. He compares this to the poetic saying of Ibn al-Farid, whose lines about love (al-hubb) being difficult serve a similar hortatory purpose. The poem encourages the reader to choose the path of effort despite its hardship.

Reaching the Beloved through Incapacity
Paraphrases and parses the poetic line on reaching Su'ad.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentary on a mystical poem teaches that true closeness to Allah comes through recognizing one's own weakness and neediness.

Core Idea
The only way to reach Him is through your own incapacity.

The commentator takes a poetic line that seems to describe the impossibility of reaching the beloved and transforms it into a profound spiritual lesson. He explains that the barrenness and emptiness depicted are not complaints but metaphors for abandoning reliance on one's own power. This lesson explores that teaching and its implications for the seeker's path.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow, then read the literal translation. Notice how the commentator carefully parses each phrase.
The passage includes grammatical analysis, but focus on the overall meaning.
2
Identify the spiritual metaphor
Look for the commentator's explanation of words like 'barefoot' and 'empty'. He says these are metaphors for not relying on worldly means or one's own power. What is the intended lesson?
The key phrase is 'the intent is to reach Allah through incapacity and neediness'.
3
Reflect on the quoted saying
The commentator quotes a line: 'Be incapable of it, you will be capable through it.' Reflect on how this summarizes the lesson. Why is turning away from reliance on self considered injustice?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( ولذا قيل) : أي : قولاً صحيحاً لبعض العارفين . قوله : (كيف الوصول ... إلخ ) : استفهام تعجبي استبعادي، وسعاد قلَلُ الجبالِ وبينهنَّ حُتوفُ [والسير إلى الله ...] قوله : ( ودونها) : أي : سعاد. وقوله : (قلل الجبال جمع قلة والمراد بها شواهق الجبال، وهو من إضافة الصفة للموصوف، والظرف خبر مقدم، و(قلل) مبتدأ مؤخّر، والجملة حال من (سعاد). وقوله : (وبينهنَّ حتوف): الظرف خبر مقدم، و(حتوف) بالحاء والتّاء مبتدأ مؤخّر، جمع حتف بمعنى : مهالك لسعة المسافة، والجملة حال من (جبال) . وقوله : (والرّجل حافية مبتدأ وخبر، وكذلك ما بعده. : وقوله : (صفر) بكسر فسكون ؛ أي خليَّة من الدُّنيا التي يستعين بها على أجرة الرُّكوب والزَّاد الموصل، وهو كناية عن عدم تأهله للقرب من حضرة الحقِّ؛ لكونه نظر إلى حوله وقوته فرأى الأمر مستبعداً كبعد من كانت هذه أوصافه في وصوله إلى محبوبته وليس المقصود اليأس لنفسه ولا لغيره، وإنَّما المقصود الوصول إلى الله تعالى بالعجز والافتقار إليه لا بالحول ولا بالقوَّة قال بعض العارفين في هذا المعنى : من الطويل] وكن عاجزاً عنها تكن قادراً بها فعدلك عنها منك نحو السوى ظلم ومن ذلك المعنى قول السيد البكري :
His statement: "And therefore it was said" — meaning: a correct saying of some knowers [of Allah]. His statement: "How can one reach... etc." — it is an interrogative of wonderment indicating improbability. And Su'ad is [the beloved]? "Peaks of mountains and between them are deaths, and the journey to Allah..." His statement: "And below it" — meaning: Su'ad. And his statement: "Peaks of mountains" — “quṭṣul” is plural of “quṭṣa” (peak), and by it is meant the lofty mountains, and it is annexation of the adjective to the described, and the adverb is a fronted predicate, and “quṭṣul” is a delayed subject, and the sentence is a circumstantial clause from “Su'ad”. And his statement: "And between them are deaths" — the adverb is a fronted predicate, and “ḥutūf” with ḥā’ and tā’ is a delayed subject, plural of “ḥatf” meaning: mortal dangers due to the vastness of the distance, and the sentence is a circumstantial clause from “Ṽibāl” (mountains). And his statement: "And the foot is bare" is subject and predicate, and likewise what follows. And his statement: “ṣifr” with kasr of ṣād and sukūn of fā’ — meaning: empty of the world with which one assists himself for the fare of riding and the provisions that reach, and it is a metaphor for his lack of qualification for nearness to the presence of the Truth [Allah]; because he looked at his own capacity and power and saw the matter as distant, like the distance of one with these descriptions in reaching his beloved. And the intent is not despair for himself or for others, but rather the intent is to reach Allah Almighty through incapacity and neediness to Him, not through capacity nor through power. Some knowers [of Allah] said in this meaning: [In the meter of Tawīl] "And be incapable of it, you will be capable through it; for your turning away from it toward other than it is injustice." And from that meaning is the saying of al-Sayyid al-Bakri:
Source pages p. 425

Commentary
This passage provides grammatical and semantic commentary on a line of poetry (likely by Ibn al-Farid or another mystic) that describes the difficulty of reaching the beloved (Su'ad). The commentator explains each phrase: "And therefore it was said" indicates the preceding line is correctly attributed to some 'arifs. The phrase "How can one reach Su'ad" expresses wonder at the impossibility. The commentator then parses the poetic line: "Peaks of mountains and between them are deaths, and the journey to Allah..." He analyzes each word: "and below it" refers to Su'ad; "peaks of mountains" (qulal al-jibal) is an adjective-noun construction, with the adverb as fronted predicate and "peaks" as delayed subject, all as a circumstantial clause describing the state of Su'ad. "And between them are deaths" (wa baynahunna hutuf) has the adverb as fronted predicate and "hutuf" (a plural of death) as delayed subject, also circumstantial. The "hutuf" refer to mortal dangers due to the great distance. He then explains "and the foot is bare" (al-rijlu hafiyah) and "empty" (sifr) — meaning the traveler is empty of worldly goods that could pay for transportation and provisions. This is a metaphor for the seeker's lack of qualification to approach the Divine Presence because he relies on his own capacity and power, seeing the path as impossibly distant. The commentator explicitly states that the intent is not personal despair but rather to convey that the only way to reach Allah is through utter incapacity and neediness, not through one's own power. He then quotes another saying: "Be incapable of it, you will be capable through it; turning away from it toward other than it is injustice." The passage ends with a reference to a similar meaning in the saying of al-Sayyid al-Bakri.

Key Terms
سعاد
قلل
حتوف
حافية
صفر
العجز والافتقار

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might take the poetry's imagery of bare feet and emptiness as a complaint of poverty or physical hardship, or as an expression of hopelessness.
Correction: The commentator explicitly states that the imagery is a deliberate metaphor for relying on God's power, not one's own, and that the intent is not despair for oneself or others.

Checkpoint

B) The traveler is devoid of worldly means that he could use to assist himself, symbolizing reliance on God.
C) The traveler is carrying no luggage.
D) The traveler is spiritually empty and lost.

Key Takeaway
The only way to reach Allah is through recognizing one's own incapacity and turning to Him in complete neediness, not through self-reliance.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you face a difficult situation, do you first turn to your own capacity and resources, or do you immediately recognize your incapacity and turn to Allah? What does your habitual response reveal about your heart's true reliance?

The poem describes the seeker as barefoot and empty of worldly provision. In what areas of your life are you holding on to your own 'shoes' and 'provisions' (your skills, wealth, reputation) instead of surrendering to Allah? What would it cost you to let go?

Balancing Fear and Hope in Health and Illness
The author explains the ruling on preferring fear over hope in health and hope over fear in illness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Maliki view on balancing fear and hope in times of health and illness, and how it differs from the Shafi'i position.

Core Idea
Fear and hope are like two wings; their balance shifts with circumstance.

This lesson explores the Islamic spiritual principle of balancing fear (khawf) and hope (raja') in worship. The author explains that while both are essential, their predominance changes according to one's state: fear during health motivates worship, while hope during illness comforts the soul. We also learn about a difference between the Maliki and Shafi'i schools on this matter.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Carefully read the Arabic passage and its literal English translation to grasp the author's main points.
Notice the bird-wing analogy.
2
Understand the bird-wing analogy
The author compares fear and hope to the two wings of a bird. Both are necessary for spiritual balance, like both wings are needed for flight.
3
Compare Maliki and Shafi'i views
The passage ends by contrasting the Maliki position (fear in health, hope in illness) with the Shafi'i position (equal balance always). Reflect on the wisdom behind each approach.
This difference shows flexibility within Islamic tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وغلّب) في حال اشتغالك بالذكر المذكور (الخوف) من الله تعالى ما دمت في حال الصحة (على الرّجاء) في رحمته وعفوه، يريد أنه لا بدَّ للعبيد من الخوف والرَّجاء معاً ؛ لأنّهما كجناحي الطائر، متى فقد أحدهما سقط، إلّا أنَّه في حال الصحة والسَّلامة ينبغي تغليب جانب الخوف على جانب الرّجاء، لأنَّه كالسوط ينساق به إلى الاعتناء بالعبادة، وبه تزول الرُّعونات النّفسيَّة عن القلب إن شاء الله تعالى. فإذا نزل به المرض وأشرف على الموت فينبغي تغليبُ جانب الرَّجاء على الخوف لأنَّه حال القدوم على الكريم . وأتيت إلـيـك خـلـيّـاً مـن صومي وصلاتي مع حججي قوله : (ما دمت في حال الصحة . . . إلخ ) : هذا هو مذهب مالك، وعند الشّافعي يجعلهما كجناحي الطَّائر مستويين صحة ومرضاً .
And make fear of Allah Most High predominant in the state of your engagement in the mentioned remembrance, as long as you are in health, over hope in His mercy and pardon. He intends that the servant must have both fear and hope together, because they are like the two wings of a bird; whenever one is missing, it falls. However, in the state of health and safety, it is appropriate to make the side of fear predominant over the side of hope, because it is like a whip by which one is driven to care for worship, and by it the soul's follies depart from the heart, if Allah wills. Then if illness befalls him and he approaches death, then it is appropriate to make the side of hope predominant over fear, because it is the state of arriving at the Generous One. And I came to you empty from my fast, my prayer, and my pilgrimages. His statement: 'As long as you are in health... etc.': This is the school of Malik. According to al-Shafi'i, he makes them like the two wings of a bird, equal in health and illness.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage explains the ruling on balancing fear and hope. It begins by stating that fear should be predominant during times of health, because it motivates one to perform worship diligently, likening fear to a whip that drives one to action, and it removes psychological recklessness from the heart. Then it says that when illness and death approach, hope should be predominant because one is about to meet the Generous Lord. The author then notes that this is the Maliki position, while al-Shafi'i holds that fear and hope should be equal at all times, like the two wings of a bird that must be balanced.

Key Terms

رجاء
تغليب
جناحي الطائر
مذهب مالك
مذهب الشافعي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One should always maintain the same level of fear and hope regardless of circumstances.
Correction: The passage teaches that while both are always present, their predominance should shift: fear in health, hope in illness.

Checkpoint

A) In illness
B) In health
C) Always
D) Never

Key Takeaway
Fear and hope are both essential, but fear should predominate in health to motivate worship, and hope should predominate in illness in anticipation of Allah's mercy.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Which wing are you neglecting right now, and what one concrete action can you take this week to restore balance?

The Stages of Fear and Hope for Spiritual Travelers
Classification of fear and hope states across three spiritual stages.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the spiritual states of fear and hope are named differently at three stages of the spiritual journey.

Core Idea
Fear and hope are refined into higher states as the traveler progresses.

This passage from the commentary explains that fear and hope are essential for every spiritual traveler. However, the knowers (al-`arifun) give these states different names depending on the traveler's stage: beginner, intermediate, and perfect. Let's explore these names and what they reveal about spiritual growth.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and its literal translation to understand the classification of fear and hope states.
Notice the three distinct stages mentioned by the knowers.
2
Identify the three stages
List the three levels: the traveler (beginner), the intermediate, and the perfect. Note the names given to their fear and hope states.
For the beginner: qabd (contraction) and bast (expansion). For the intermediate: uns (intimacy) and haybah (awe). For the perfect: jalal (majesty) and jamal (beauty).
3
Reflect on the progression
Consider why the states are renamed. How do these names reflect spiritual maturity?
The progression moves from contraction/expansion to intimacy/awe to majesty/beauty, indicating a deepening of the soul's relationship with Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم : أنَّ الخوف والرجاء حالتان لا بدَّ لكل شخص منهما، ولا يخلو منهما أحد سلك الطريق أوَّلاً ، لكن قال العارفون : إنَّ خوف السائر إلى الله تعالى يسمَّى قبضاً، ورجاؤه يسمَّى بسطاً، والمتوسّط يسمَّى أنساً وهيبة والكامل يسمَّى جلالاً وجمالاً.
And know that fear and hope are two states that every person necessarily has; and no one who travels the path is devoid of them initially. But the knowers said: The fear of the traveler to Allah Most High is called qabd (contraction), and his hope is called bast (expansion); the intermediate one's [states] are called uns (intimacy) and haybah (awe); and the perfect one's [states] are called jalal (majesty) and jamal (beauty).
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage classifies the spiritual states of fear and hope. It states that every spiritual traveler necessarily possesses these two states from the outset. However, the knowers (al-`arifun) give them different names according to the level of the traveler. For the beginner (the traveler on the path), fear is called qabd (contraction) and hope is called bast (expansion). For the intermediate, the states are called uns (intimacy) and haybah (awe). For the perfect (the one who has reached the destination), the states are called jalal (majesty) and jamal (beauty). This shows that these qualities become refined as one progresses.

Key Terms
خوف
رجاء
قبض
بسط
أنس
هيبة
جلال
جمال
عارفون
سالك

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Fear and hope are simple, uniform emotions for all spiritual travelers.
Correction: In reality, they are refined and renamed according to the traveler's spiritual station.

Checkpoint

A) Bast
B) Uns
C) Haybah
D) Qabd

Key Takeaway
Spiritual growth transforms the basic emotions of fear and hope into higher states of intimacy, awe, majesty, and beauty.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Right now, as you read this lesson, which of the three stages most honestly describes your own relationship with Allah? Are you still in the stage of contraction and expansion, or have you advanced to intimacy and awe, or even majesty and beauty? Be truthful with yourself.
?
When you experience fear of Allah, is it a raw, unsettling contraction (qabd) that drives you away, or has it transformed into a reverent awe (haybah) that draws you closer? What does your answer reveal about your spiritual journey?
?
Is your hope in Allah still a selfish desire for relief (bast), or has it matured into a deep intimacy (uns) that seeks His beauty (jamal) for its own sake? What concrete step can you take today to move one step closer to the perfect stage?

Definitions of Fear, Grief, Hope, and Blameworthy Ambition
The author linguistically defines raja' and defines khawf, huzn, raja', distinguishing raja' from tama'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of fear, grief, and hope, and the key distinction between true hope and blameworthy ambition.

Core Idea
True hope works with means; without means, it becomes blameworthy ambition.

In this passage, the author provides concise definitions of key spiritual states: fear, grief, hope, and ambition. He clarifies that hope is not mere wishful thinking but requires active engagement with the means. Understanding this distinction is crucial for authentic Islamic spirituality.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get a sense of the definitions.
Note the linguistic point about the pronunciation of 'raja'.
2
Identify the three definitions
Find the definitions of fear, grief, and hope. Notice how each is defined in relation to time (future or past) and the heart's state.
3
Understand the condition for hope
The passage adds a condition: hope requires taking means. Identify this condition and see how it distinguishes hope from ambition.
4
Reflect on the distinction
Why is ambition blameworthy? Consider the Islamic emphasis on effort and reliance on Allah.
This connects to broader theological themes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله :(والرَّجاء) : أي بالمد وأمَّا بالقصر فمعناه الناحية قال تعالى : (وَالْمَلَكُ عَلَى أَرْجَابِهَا) (۱)؛ أي : نواحيها. ﴾ (۱) سورة الحاقة : (۱۷). الخوف والرجاء والخوف : هَمُّ وقَلَق لما هو آت . والحزن : هم لما فات . والرَّجاء : تَعلَّق القلب بمرغوب يحصل في المستقبل مع الأسباب، فإن لم يأخذ بالأسباب فَطَمع، وهو مذموم شرع
His statement: 'and hope (al-raja'):' i.e., with lengthened form (al-madd). As for shortened form (al-qasr), its meaning is the side. He said, Most High: 'And the angels upon its sides (arjabiha)' (Q. 69:17); i.e., its sides. Fear and hope. And fear (al-khawf) is worry and anxiety for what is coming. And grief (al-huzn) is worry for what has passed. And hope (al-raja') is the heart's attachment to a desired thing that will happen in the future while taking the means. If he does not take the means, then it is ambition (tama'), and it is blameworthy in the Sacred Law.
Source pages p. 427

Commentary
This passage provides definitions. It first clarifies the word 'raja'' (hope) linguistically: when pronounced with a long vowel (al-madd) it means hope; with a short vowel (al-qasr) it means 'side', citing the Quranic verse about angels on the sides (arjab) of the heaven. Then it defines three terms: khawf (fear) is worry and anxiety about future events; huzn (grief) is sadness over what has passed; raja' (hope) is the heart's attachment to a desired future outcome while using the necessary means. The passage makes a crucial distinction: if one hopes without taking means, it is called tama' (ambition), which is blameworthy in Islamic law. This guards against passive wishful thinking.

رجاء
خوف
حزن
طمع
مد
قصر
أرجاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hope in Allah means simply wishing without effort.
Correction: True hope (raja') requires taking the means; otherwise it is blameworthy ambition (tama').

Checkpoint
What distinguishes true hope (raja') from blameworthy ambition (tama') according to this passage?
A) The object of hope
B) Taking the means
C) The intensity of desire
D) The time frame

Key Takeaway
Hope in Islam must be accompanied by taking the means; without effort, it becomes blameworthy ambition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a current desire you have. Are you actively taking the means to achieve it, or are you only hoping without action? How does the lesson's distinction between hope and ambition expose your own negligence?
?
When you feel fear about the future, does it lead you to take protective means, or does it paralyze you? What would it mean to transform that fear into hope with action?
?
Consider a past grief. The passage defines grief as worry for what has passed. How does clinging to past grief prevent you from taking means in the present? What step can you take today to move from grief to hopeful action?
