The Command to Journey to Allah
Imperative for spiritual journey and its definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the command to journey to Allah and the definition of spiritual journey as the heart's attachment to Allah while opposing the soul's desires.

Core Idea
The spiritual journey is the heart's attachment to Allah, not physical travel.

The author begins with an imperative: 'Journey!' But this is not a physical journey; it is a spiritual one. He explains that it means to turn to your Lord without distancing from the straight path. He then defines the spiritual journey itself as the heart's attachment to Allah while opposing the soul's desires. This lesson unpacks that definition.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the imperative
Read the Arabic passage once. Notice the imperative 'Journey!' and how it is glossed with 'a swift journey' and 'your Mawla'.
2
Understand 'without distance'
Reflect on the phrase 'without distance' – it means not attaching your heart to other than Allah. This clarifies the spiritual nature of the journey.
Distancing from the straight path is defined as attaching the heart to other than Allah.
3
Grasp the definition of al-sayr
The passage defines al-sayr as the attachment of the heart to Allah while opposing the soul in its desires, preferring Allah over others. This is the core meaning.
It is not physical travel but an inner transformation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider what it means to prefer Allah over oneself. How does this definition shape the understanding of worship and devotion?

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(وسِرْ) سيراً حثيثاً (المولاك ) أي : سيدك وخالقك، (بلا تناء) أي : بلا تباعد عن الطريق المستقيم المُوصِل إلى الله تعالى؛ بأن تعلّق قلبك بغيره تعالى . وتقدم أنَّ السَّير عبارة عن تعلُّق القلب بالله تعالى مع مخالفة النفس في شهواتها إيثاراً له تعالى على غيره، وهذا هو الطريق المستقيم الموصل إلى الله تعالى، وهي طريق الشُّطَار من أهل المحبَّة والشَّوق إلى بارئ النَّسَم، ومبناها على الموت بالإرادة، لخبر: «موتوا قبل أن ... قوله : (سيراً حثيثاً أي سريعاً شديداً، والمعنى: أقبل على عبادة الله بكليتك، ولا تضيّع عمرك سبهللاً ؛ فإنَّه ذخيرة لك، ففي الحديث: واعمل لربك على قدر حاجتك إليه» (۱) قوله : ( بأن تعلّق قلبك بغيره): تصوير للتباعد عن طريق المستقيم. قوله : (إلى بارئ النَّسم) : أي : خالقها والنَّسم جمع نسمة كشجرة، وشجر فهو اسم جنس جمعي يفرّق بينه وبين واحده بالتاء . قوله : ( على الموت بالإرادة :أي: بالاختيار والقصد. (1) ينظر أحاديث الشيوخ الثقات للكعبي (٤٥٧) .
And journey with a swift journey to your Mawla, i.e., your Master and Creator, without distance, i.e., without distancing from the straight path that leads to Allah Most High; by not attaching your heart to other than Him. And it has preceded that journey (al-sayr) is the attachment of the heart to Allah Most High while opposing the soul in its desires, preferring Him over others. And this is the straight path that leads to Allah Most High, and it is the path of the virtuous ones among the people of love and yearning for the Creator of souls, and its foundation is dying by will, because of the report: 'Die before...' His statement: 'a swift journey' i.e., fast and intense, and the meaning: devote yourself entirely to the worship of Allah, and do not waste your life idly, for it is your provision. In the hadith: 'And work for your Lord according to your need for Him.' His statement: 'by not attaching your heart to other than Him' is a depiction of distancing from the straight path. His statement: 'to the Creator of souls' i.e., their Creator, and al-nasam is the plural of nasamah like shajarah and shajr; it is a collective noun distinguished from its singular by the ta'. His statement: 'upon dying by will' i.e., by choice and intention. (1) See Ahadith al-Shuyukh al-Thiqat by al-Ka'bi (457).
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage explains the imperative 'Journey!' (wa-sir). It glosses 'siran hathithan' as swift and intense travel, and 'mawlaka' as your Master and Creator. 'Bala tana'in' means without distancing from the straight path that leads to Allah. The author then expands on the concept of al-sayr (spiritual journey), which he previously defined as the heart's attachment to Allah while opposing the soul's desires and preferring Allah over others. This is the straight path, which is the path of the virtuous (shuttar) among the people of love and yearning for the Creator of souls. Its foundation is 'dying by will' (al-mawt bi'l-irada), based on the hadith 'Die before you die' (as known from other sources). The passage then provides further commentary on the phrases: the intensity of the journey means devoting oneself entirely to worship and not wasting one's life; attaching the heart to other than Allah is distancing from the straight path; 'Bari' al-nasam' means the Creator of souls, with a grammatical note on the plural; and 'dying by will' means by choice and intention.

Key Terms
سير
مولى
تناء
طريق مستقيم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The spiritual journey is about physical travel or pilgrimage.
Correction: The text defines it as attachment of the heart, not physical movement.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the definition of al-sayr (spiritual journey)?
A) Physical travel to a holy site
B) Attachment of the heart to Allah while opposing the soul's desires
C) Performing ritual prayers constantly
D) Isolating oneself from society

Key Takeaway
Spiritual journey means attaching your heart to Allah and opposing your desires.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In what area of your life are you still attaching your heart to something other than Allah—a person, a possession, a status—and what one concrete step will you take today to detach it and attach it to Him alone?
?
Think of a desire of your soul that you consistently indulge instead of opposing for Allah's sake. Will you commit to opposing that desire at its very next occurrence, even if it's uncomfortable?
?
You claim to be on the spiritual journey, but if your heart is tied to people's approval or worldly comfort, are you truly journeying without distance? What will you cut off today to remove that distance?

The Straight Path and Dying by Will
Straight path defined and foundation of dying by will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand what the straight path is and why its foundation is 'dying by will'—a voluntary death to worldly desires.

Core Idea
The straight path is the path of love and yearning, founded on dying before death.

Having defined the spiritual journey, the author now describes the straight path that leads to Allah. He identifies it as the path of the virtuous ones who love and yearn for their Creator. Its foundation is a profound concept: 'dying by will'—a voluntary death to worldly desires. This lesson explores that teaching and its supporting commentary.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage identifying the straight path
Read the Arabic passage. Notice how the straight path is described as the path of the virtuous people of love and yearning for the Creator.
2
Understand 'dying by will'

The commentary clarifies it means by choice and intention.
3
Connect to the hadith
The passage cites the hadith 'Die before...' (i.e., 'Die before you die'). This hadith is the scriptural basis for the concept. Understand that it calls for spiritual self-annihilation.
4
Consider the commentary
The accompanying commentary expands on the intensity of the journey and the grammatical notes, but focus on the core: dying by will is the foundation.

Fear, Sadness, and True Hope
Al-Sawi defines three spiritual states and distinguishes hope from covetousness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definitions of fear, sadness, and hope as spiritual states, and the crucial role of taking means in true hope.

Core Idea
True hope requires action; hope without effort is blameworthy desire.

Al-Sawi distinguishes three emotional states that every believer must understand. Fear and sadness are reactions to past and future, but hope is a proactive attachment that demands effort. Let's read his precise definitions.

Guided Path
1
Read the definitions of fear and sadness
Notice that both are defined as 'worry,' but differentiated by time: fear is for the future, sadness for the past.
These are straightforward emotional states.
2
Understand hope and its condition
Hope is attachment to a future desired outcome, but it must be accompanied by taking the necessary means. Without means, it becomes blameworthy.
This is the key teaching.
3
Note the warning against covetousness
Al-Sawi explicitly states that hope without means is covetousness (tama') and is legally blameworthy.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والخوفُ : هَمُّ وقَلَقٌ لِمَا هو آتٍ . والحزنُ : هَمٌّ لِمَا فاتَ . والرَّجاءُ : تَعَلُّقُ القلبِ بِمَرْغُوبٍ يَحصُلُ في المُستقبلِ مع الأسبابِ، فإنْ لَمْ يَأخُذْ بالأسبابِ فَطَمَعٌ، وهو مَذْمُومٌ شَرْعًا
And fear: is worry and anxiety for what is coming. And sadness: is worry for what has passed. And hope: is the attachment of the heart to a desired thing that occurs in the future with the means; if he does not take the means, then it is covetousness (tama'), and it is legally blameworthy.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is a set of definitions for three spiritual states. It begins by defining fear (al-khawf) as worry and anxiety for what is yet to come. Then sadness (al-huzn) is defined as worry for what has passed. Finally, hope (ar-raja') is defined as the heart's attachment to a desired outcome in the future, but crucially it must be accompanied by taking the necessary means. If a person hopes without taking the means, it is not true hope but covetousness (tama'), which is blameworthy in Islamic law.

Key Terms
الخوف
الحزن
الرجاء
الطمع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That hope (raja') is always praiseworthy regardless of effort.
Correction: Al-Sawi clarifies that true hope requires taking the means; otherwise it is blameworthy covetousness.

Checkpoint
According to al-Sawi, what distinguishes true hope (rajā') from blameworthy covetousness (ṭamaʿ)?
A) The intensity of the desire
B) Whether the desired thing is worldly or religious
C) Taking the necessary means to achieve it
D) The presence of fear alongside it

Key Takeaway
Fear and sadness are natural emotional responses to past and future, but true hope must be accompanied by action; hope without effort is covetousness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In which area of your life—worship, work, relationships—are you hoping for a good outcome without actually taking the necessary means? What would it look like for you to take those means starting today?
?
Think of a fear you have about the future. According to al-Sawi's definition, is it genuine fear (worry and anxiety about what is coming) or is it something else? How does labeling it correctly help you respond?
?
You often feel sadness over past mistakes. The lesson defines sadness as 'worry for what has passed.' Is your sadness pushing you to change, or is it just regret without action? What one step can you take this week to turn that sadness into productive change?

The Spiritual Journey and Death by Will
Al-Sawi explains the spiritual journey and foundation of death by will.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the concept of the spiritual journey to Allah and the meaning of 'death by will' as dying to one's ego by choice.

Core Idea
The spiritual journey is the heart's attachment to Allah while opposing the soul's desires.

In this passage, al-Sawi unpacks the meaning of a swift journey to Allah, explaining that it is not a physical travel but an inner movement of the heart. He clarifies that the straight path is founded on 'death by will' – a conscious dying to one's ego before physical death. This teaching draws on the prophetic tradition: 'Die before you die.'

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic text aloud or silently, then read the translation to grasp the overall meaning. Note the key terms: السير, الموت بالإرادة, الشطار.
2
Identify the two key concepts
Look for how al-Sawi defines 'the journey' (as-sayr) and 'death by will' (al-mawt bi'l-irada). How are they related? The journey is heart attachment with self-opposition; death by will is its foundation.
3
Reflect on the hadith references
Two hadiths are cited: 'Die before you die' and 'Work for your Lord according to your need of Him.' Consider how these support the spiritual discipline described.
4
Check for the common misunderstanding
The passage explicitly guards against the idea that this death is physical. Note how the commentator emphasizes choice and intention.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وسِرْ سيراً حثيثاً (المولاك) أي: سيدك وخالقك، (بلا تناء) أي: بلا تباعد عن الطريق المستقيم المُوصِل إلى الله تعالى؛ بأن تعلّق قلبك بغيره تعالى. وتقدم أنَّ السَّير عبارة عن تعلُّق القلب بالله تعالى مع مخالفة النفس في شهواتها إيثاراً له تعالى على غيره، وهذا هو الطريق المستقيم الموصل إلى الله تعالى، وهي طريق الشُّطَار من أهل المحبَّة والشَّوق إلى بارئ النَّسَم، ومبناها على الموت بالإرادة، لخبر: «موتوا قبل أن تموتوا». قوله: (سيراً حثيثاً) أي سريعاً شديداً، والمعنى: أقبل على عبادة الله بكليتك، ولا تضيّع عمرك سبهللاً؛ فإنَّه ذخيرة لك، ففي الحديث: «واعمل لربك على قدر حاجتك إليه». قوله: (بأن تعلّق قلبك بغيره): تصوير للتباعد عن طريق المستقيم. قوله: (إلى بارئ النَّسَم): أي: خالقها، والنَّسَم جمع نسمة كشجرة وشجر فهو اسم جنس جمعي يفرّق بينه وبين واحده بالتاء. قوله: (على الموت بالإرادة): أي: بالاختيار والقصد.
And journey (sir) a swift journey (to your Lord), meaning your Master and Creator, without separation (bala tana'), meaning without distancing from the straight path that leads to Allah Almighty; by your heart being attached to other than Him. And it has preceded that the journey (as-sayr) is an expression of the attachment of the heart to Allah Almighty along with opposing the soul in its desires, preferring Him over others, and this is the straight path that leads to Allah Almighty, and it is the way of the elite among the people of love and longing for the Creator of souls, and its foundation is death by will, due to the report: 'Die before you die.' His words: (siran hathithan) meaning swift and intense, and the meaning: turn to the worship of Allah with your whole being, and do not waste your life carelessly, for it is your treasure; thus in the hadith: 'And work for your Lord according to your need of Him.' His words: (by your heart being attached to other than Him): a depiction of distancing from the straight path. His words: (to the Creator of souls [al-nasam]): meaning their Creator, and al-nasam is plural of nasamah like shajarah and shajar, so it is a collective noun distinguished from its singular by the taa' marbutah. His words: (upon death by will): meaning by choice and intention.
Source pages p. 428

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of a verse or saying urging the spiritual journey. It begins by glossing 'sir siran hathithan' as a swift journey to one's Lord, emphasizing quick and intense movement. 'Bala tanaa'' is explained as not distancing from the straight path that leads to Allah. The author then recalls that the journey (as-sayr) has been previously defined as the heart's attachment to Allah while opposing the soul's desires, preferring Allah over all else. This is the straight path, the way of the elite (shuttar) among the lovers and yearners for the Creator of souls (bari' al-nasam). Its foundation is 'death by will' (al-mawt bi'l-irada), meaning dying to one's ego by choice and intention before physical death, as per the hadith 'Die before you die.' Then the commentator explains individual phrases: 'siran hathithan' means fast and strong, urging wholehearted worship and not wasting one's life. 'By your heart being attached to other than Him' illustrates distancing from the straight path. 'To the Creator of souls' explains the word 'al-nasam'. Finally, 'death by will' is clarified as choosing and intending this spiritual death.

السير
الموت بالإرادة
الشطار
بارئ النسم
تعلق القلب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Death by will means physical death or suicide.
Correction: It means dying to one's ego by choice and intention, opposing the soul's desires.

Checkpoint
What does 'death by will' (al-mawt bi'l-irada) mean in this passage?
A) Committing suicide to avoid sin
B) Dying physically before one's appointed time
C) Choosing to oppose the soul's desires by choice and intention
D) Being martyred in the path of Allah

Key Takeaway
The spiritual journey to Allah is an inner struggle of heart attachment and self-denial, epitomized by 'death by will' – a conscious dying to ego before physical death.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Your life is your treasure. Are you wasting it carelessly, or are you journeying swiftly? What one attachment to other than Allah must you sever today to begin that swift journey?
?
The foundation of this path is death by will—dying to your desires by choice. Which desire in your life have you not yet chosen to die to, and what is holding you back from that death?
?
The elite lovers long for the Creator of souls. Do you truly long for Allah, or are you content with distant admiration? What would change in your daily life if your heart were attached to Him alone?

Disciplining the Blameworthy Soul
Al-Sawi glosses Ibn al-Farid's poem on disciplining the blameworthy soul.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Sufi method of disciplining the blameworthy soul through ascetic practices as explained by al-Sawi's commentary on Ibn al-Farid's poem.

Core Idea
Discipline your soul before death disciplines it for you.

In this lesson, we delve into a poem by the celebrated Sufi poet Ibn al-Farid, glossed by al-Sawi. The poem addresses the struggle with the inner self (nafs) that constantly inclines towards disobedience. Al-Sawi unpacks each line, revealing how the poet tamed his soul through voluntary hardships, turning it from blameworthy to obedient. This passage provides both poetic inspiration and a practical method for spiritual self-discipline.

Guided Path
1
Read the poem and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic poem and its English translation to grasp the poet's message about the soul's nature and discipline.
Notice the contrast between obeying the soul and obeying God.
2
Examine the commentary on obedience and disobedience
Focus on al-Sawi's explanation of the first two lines: how obeying the soul leads to sin, and disobeying it leads to obedience to God.
This reveals the dialectical relationship.
3
Understand the hardships of discipline
Review the list of hardships al-Sawi enumerates (hunger, silence, seclusion, etc.) and the comparative 'easier than death'.
These are permitted ascetic practices, not self-harm.
4
Reflect on the fasting example
Consider the story of Ibn al-Farid's extended fasts as a concrete application of the principle.
It shows the extent of discipline needed.
5
Connect to the broader goal
Understand that the aim is to annihilate the soul's lusts, leading to spiritual rest.
This is the concept of 'fana' al-shahawat'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تموتوا (۱) ولذا قال سيدي عمر ابن الفارض (۲): [من الطويل]
ونفسي كانت قبل لوّامة
متى أُطِعْها عصت أو أعص كانت مطيعتي
فحملتها ما للموت أيسر بعضِهِ
وأتعبتها كيما تكون مريحتي
قوله: (متى أطعها): أي: في شهواتها ولذاتها.
وقوله: (عصت): أي: خالفت ربَّها.
وقوله: (أو أعص): أي أخالفها وأقمع شهواتها.
وقوله: (كانت مطيعتي): أي موافقة لي على ما أريد منها من طاعة الله تعالى.
قوله: (ما الموت أيسر بعضه): أي من الجوع، والهجر، والصمت، والعزلة والتَّغرُّب، ولبس خشن الثّياب، ونحو ذلك من المشاق التي يكون بها تربية النفس، وأفعل التفضيل على معنى (من).
والمعنى: حملتها متاعب الموت أسهل من بعضها؛ فإنَّه كان يواصل الجوع أربعيناً أربعيناً، فاتفق أنه طلبت نفسه شهوة فزادها عشراً، فصار أكله بعد كل خمسين.
وقوله: (وأتعبتها أي بتلك الأمور.
وقوله: (كيما تكون مريحتي) أي بفناء شهواتها.
Die before you die. And therefore my master Umar ibn al-Farid said (in the metre tawil):
'And my soul was previously blameworthy;
Whenever I obey it, it disobeys; or I disobey it, it becomes obedient to me.
So I burdened it with what, the easiest part of death, is easier;
And I fatigued it so that it may give me rest.'
His words: (whenever I obey it): i.e., in its lusts and pleasures.
His words: (it disobeys): i.e., it disobeys its Lord.
His words: (or I disobey it): i.e., I oppose it and suppress its lusts.
His words: (it becomes obedient to me): i.e., agreeing with me in what I want from it of obedience to Allah Almighty.
His words: ('the easiest part of death is easier'): i.e., than hunger, abandonment, silence, seclusion, exile, wearing coarse clothing, and similar hardships by which the soul is trained; and the comparative form is based on the meaning of 'than'.
And the meaning: I burdened it with the hardships of death that are easier than some of them; for he used to fast continuously for forty days, and it happened that his soul desired a pleasure, so he increased it by ten, so his eating was after every fifty days.
His words: (And I fatigued it) i.e., by those things.
His words: (so that it may give me rest) i.e., by the annihilation of its lusts.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage provides a line-by-line commentary on a poem by the Sufi poet Umar ibn al-Farid. The poem describes the soul's blameworthy nature and the method of disciplining it. The poet says that when he obeys his soul's desires, it disobeys its Lord; but when he disobeys the soul (i.e., suppresses its desires), it becomes obedient to him. Therefore, he burdens the soul with hardships that are easier than physical death—such as prolonged hunger, abandonment, silence, seclusion, exile, and coarse clothing—to train it. The commentator explains that the comparative 'easier' means these hardships are easier than the likes of those mentioned. He gives an example: the poet used to fast for forty days at a time, but when his soul sought a pleasure, he added ten more days, eating only every fifty days. By fatiguing the soul in this way, he aimed to annihilate its lusts and attain its rest in obedience.

Key Terms
نفس لوامة
تربية النفس
الموت
فناء الشهوات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The poem advocates physical self-harm or extreme asceticism that damages the body.
Correction: The hardships mentioned (hunger, silence, seclusion, etc.) are lawful, moderate practices intended to discipline the soul, not to harm it. Al-Sawi's example of fasting shows a regulated increase, not self-inflicted harm.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does Ibn al-Farid mean by 'the easiest part of death' (ما الموت أيسر بعضه)?
A) The moment of death is easy
B) Physical death is easier than disciplining the soul
C) Certain hardships of self-discipline are easier than some forms of death
D) Death is easier than hunger

Key Takeaway
Disciplining the soul through voluntary hardships is a means to transform it from blameworthy to obedient, ultimately attaining spiritual rest.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When your soul craves a comfort or pleasure that conflicts with Allah's command, do you habitually give in or oppose it? Reflect on the last time you chose to obey your desires and its spiritual consequence. What will you do differently tomorrow?
?
The poet fatigued his soul through hunger, silence, and seclusion. Which of these or similar practices have you ever undertaken to discipline your soul? If none, what is one small hardship you can voluntarily embrace this week to weaken a harmful attachment?
?
The goal is not the hardship itself but the rest that comes from annihilating lusts. Are you more focused on the discomfort of self-discipline or on the promised peace? How can you shift your intention to seek Allah's pleasure rather than just avoiding sin?

Disciplining the Soul: Hardship Easier Than Death
Commentary on Ibn al-Farid's verses on disciplining the soul

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the commentator explains Ibn al-Farid's poem about disciplining the soul through severe ascetic practices, and why such hardships are considered 'easier than death'.

Core Idea
Training the soul through hardship is easier than many forms of death.

This passage is a commentary on the renowned Sufi poet Ibn al-Farid's verses about taming the soul. The commentator, al-Sawi, unpacks each line, showing that the hardships of self-discipline—like hunger, sleeplessness, and seclusion—are actually lighter to bear than the spiritual death that comes from unchecked desires. This lesson will guide you through the commentary's explanation of key phrases.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation to understand the commentator's gloss on each line.
Notice the structure: each phrase of the poem is followed by an explanation.
2
Understand the key comparison
Focus on the phrase 'ما الموت أيسر بعضه' and its explanation. The commentator says that the hardships of disciplining the soul are easier than some types of death.
The comparative form 'أيسر' is used to show that death is easier than these hardships.
3
Reflect on the example of fasting
Consider the example of the poet fasting for forty days, and when his soul desired something, he added ten more days. This illustrates the extreme discipline.
The purpose of these hardships is to train the soul, not merely to suffer.

Source Text

قوله : (متى أطعها): أي: في شهواتها ولذاتها.
وقوله : (عصت) : أي : خالفت ربَّها
وقوله : (أو أعص أي أخالفها وأقمع شهواتها .
وقوله : (كانت مطيعتي) :أي موافقة لي على ما أريد منها من طاعة الله تعالى .
قوله : (ما الموت أيسر بعضه) :أي من الجوع، والشهر، والصمت، والعزلة والتَّغرُّب، ولبس خشن الثّياب، ونحو ذلك من المشاق الَّتي : يكون بها تربية النفس، وأفعل التفضيل على معنى (من) .

وقوله : (وأتعبتها أي بتلك الأمور.
وقوله : (كيما) تكون مريحتي) أي بفناء شهواتها .
His saying: 'When I obey it' meaning: in its desires and pleasures. And his saying: 'Disobeyed' meaning: disobeyed its Lord. And his saying: 'Or I disobey' meaning: I oppose it and suppress its desires. And his saying: 'It was obedient to me' meaning: agreeing with me on what I want from it of obedience to God Almighty. His saying: 'What is death, easier than some of it' meaning: easier than hunger, sleeplessness, silence, seclusion, estrangement, wearing coarse clothes, and similar hardships by which the soul is trained, and the comparative form is with 'min'. The meaning: I loaded it with hardships easier than some of death; for he used to fast for forty days at a time, and it happened that his soul desired a craving, so he increased it by ten, so he began eating every fifty days. His saying: 'And I tired it' meaning: with those things. His saying: 'So that it becomes my rest' meaning: through the annihilation of its desires.
Source pages p. 429

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a poem by Ibn al-Farid about disciplining the soul. The author (al-Sawi) explains each phrase. He interprets 'when I obey it' as following its desires. 'Disobeyed' means disobeyed its Lord. 'Or I disobey' means opposing and suppressing desires. 'It was obedient' means it agrees with the speaker's intention of obeying God. 'What is death, easier than some of it' means that the hardships of disciplining the soul are easier than many types of death; he gives examples like hunger, sleeplessness, silence, seclusion, wearing coarse clothes. The comparative 'أيسر' (easier) is used. He then illustrates with the example of the poet fasting forty days at a time, and when his soul desired something, he increased the fast by ten days, making it fifty days. 'I tired it' refers to these hardships, and 'become my rest' means that through the annihilation of desires, the soul finds rest.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the poet is advocating asceticism for its own sake.
Correction: The commentary shows that the hardships are for spiritual training and ultimately lead to peace through obedience to God.

Checkpoint
According to the commentary, what does 'ما الموت أيسر بعضه' mean?
A) Death is easier than some of these hardships
B) Some hardships are easier than death

Key Takeaway
The commentator emphasizes that disciplining the soul through harsh practices is a means to spiritual peace, not an end in itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
If you truly believed that disciplining your soul through temporary hardship leads to lasting peace, would you change anything about your daily routine? What is the one change you are most afraid to make?

The Comprehensive Scope of Gratitude
Al-Sawi explains that gratitude must be expressed through all limbs in performing commanded acts.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that true gratitude involves not just verbal praise but actively performing all commanded acts with one's limbs.

Core Idea
Gratitude is demonstrated through action, not just words.

The passage begins by defining gratitude as a comprehensive practice that engages the entire body. Al-Sawi explains that the grateful person uses all their limbs to carry out every command, whether obligatory or recommended. This lesson explores that foundational concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on gratitude in action
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the first sentence about acting with limbs.
2
Understand the commentary
The commentator clarifies that 'acts with his limbs' refers to serving the pillars of Islam, emphasizing that gratitude is holistic.
The phrase 'laff wa nashr mukhlabit' indicates a mixed order.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider how this teaching broadens the concept of gratitude beyond the tongue to include every action of obedience.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويعمل بجوارحه كل ما طلب منه من المأمورات، واجبة كانت أو مندوبة . ومن النعم التي يجب الشُّكر عليها التَّوفيق للتَّوبة، والشُّكرُ على الشُّكر، فالشُّكر لا نهاية له ولذا قال عليه الصلاة والسلام: سبحانك لا نحصي ثناء عليك أنت كما أثنيت على نفسك»(۱) والشكر بهذا الاعتبار عزيز جداً ؛ لأنَّه طريق الصِّدِّيقين، ولذا قال تعالى: (وَقَلِيلٌ مِّنْ عِبَادِيَ الشَّكُورُ ) (٢) . . (۲) وقوله: (ويعمل بجوارحه): راجع لخدمة الأركان، ففيه لفّ ونشر ملخبط . قوله : (والشكر على الشُّكر) : أي : والتَّوفيق على الشكر، ومنه قول بعضهم (۳) [من الطويل] إذا كان شكري نعمة الله نعمةً عليَّ له في مثلها يجب الشُّكرُ فكيف بلوغ الشكر إلَّا بفضله وإن طالت الأيَّامُ واتَّصل العمرُ قوله : ( لأنَّه طريق الصِّدِّيقين : أي: الأنبياء وكبار الأولياء، ومنه حديث : «أفلا أكون عبداً شكوراً؟!»(4).
He acts with his limbs in all that is demanded of him from commanded acts, whether obligatory or recommended. Among the blessings for which gratitude is obligatory are the success (tawfīq) for repentance, and gratitude upon gratitude. So gratitude has no end, and therefore he (peace be upon him) said: 'Glory be to You, we cannot enumerate praise upon You; You are as You have praised Yourself.' And gratitude in this respect is very rare, because it is the path of the truthful (ṣiddīqīn), and therefore the Exalted said: 'And few of My servants are grateful.' And his saying: 'And he acts with his limbs' refers to serving the pillars, so there is an irregular luff and nashr. His saying: 'And gratitude upon gratitude' means: success upon gratitude, and from it is the saying of some: [from ṭawīl] 'If my gratitude for Allah's blessing is a blessing from Him, then for a similar blessing gratitude is due. So how can gratitude be attained except by His grace, even if days prolong and life continues.' His saying: 'Because it is the path of the truthful' means: the prophets and the great saints, and from it is the hadith: 'Shall I not be a grateful servant?'
Source pages p. 435

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on two points regarding gratitude (shukr). First, it explains that gratitude must be expressed with the limbs in performing commanded acts. Then it discusses the concept of gratitude upon gratitude, showing that true gratitude is difficult and infinite, requiring divine assistance. The commentator also clarifies the phrases 'acts with his limbs' and 'gratitude upon gratitude', and defines 'the path of the truthful' as the way of prophets and saints. The poem illustrates the inescapable need for God's grace in being grateful. The hadith 'Shall I not be a grateful servant?' shows the Prophet's example.

أركان

Misconception: Some think gratitude is only verbal praise or inner feeling.
Correction: This passage shows that true gratitude must be demonstrated by actively obeying Allah with one's entire body.

Checkpoint

A) Merely physical movement in prayer
B) Serving the pillars of Islam
C) Only obligatory acts
D) Any random action

Key Takeaway
Gratitude is not just in the heart or on the tongue; it must be shown by using every limb in obedience to Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think about the last time you said 'Alhamdulillah'. Did your limbs move in obedience to Allah at that moment, or did your gratitude stop at your tongue? Which command of Allah have you been neglecting with your hands, feet, or eyes, and what will you change today?
?

?
Gratitude demands action. Look at your daily routine: which obligatory act (like prayer) are you performing with half-hearted effort, and which recommended act (like extra charity) have you abandoned? Write down one concrete step to engage your limbs fully in that act before sunset.

The Depth and Rarity of Gratitude
Al-Sawi discusses gratitude upon gratitude, its infinity, and why it is the path of the truthful.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why gratitude is considered infinite and very rare, requiring gratitude for the ability to be grateful.

Core Idea
True gratitude is a never-ending cycle of grace.

The passage deepens the discussion by introducing the concept of 'gratitude upon gratitude'. Al-Sawi shows that even the ability to be grateful is a blessing requiring further gratitude, leading to an infinite chain. This makes perfect gratitude a rare quality, belonging to the prophets and saints.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on gratitude's depth
Read the second part of the Arabic passage focusing on 'gratitude upon gratitude' and the proof from the hadith and Quran.
Pay attention to the poem and its meaning.
2
Understand the infinite cycle
The commentator explains that gratitude upon gratitude means success (tawfīq) for gratitude itself, creating a chain that only Allah can complete.
The poem illustrates this dilemma: if gratitude is a blessing, it requires more gratitude.
3
Recognize the rarity
The Prophet (peace be upon him) said he cannot fully praise Allah, and the Quran says 'few of My servants are grateful'. This shows how rare true gratitude is.
The path of the truthful (ṣiddīqīn) is that of prophets and saints.

Correction: The passage shows that gratitude is infinite and requires divine assistance, making it a deep and ongoing practice.

Checkpoint
Why is gratitude considered 'very rare' according to the passage?
A) Because it requires patience
B) Because it involves gratitude upon gratitude and infinite cycles
C) Because it is only for prophets
D) Because it is impossible

Key Takeaway
True gratitude is infinite and rare, requiring gratitude for the ability to be grateful, and can only be attained through Allah's grace.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When did you last thank Allah for the ability to thank Him? Stop and consider: every 'Alhamdulillah' you utter is a new grace. How many times today have you forgotten to thank Him for that very tongue that speaks? Make a concrete resolution now: after every prayer tonight, add a specific du'a thanking Allah for the tawfīq to pray and thank Him.
?
The Prophet ﷺ, despite his perfection, said, 'We cannot enumerate praise upon You.' If the Prophet felt his gratitude was incomplete, what does that say about your own? What is the one blessing you have been taking for granted that you will actively acknowledge as a gift from Allah right now? Write it down and commit to thanking Him for it every day this week.
?
You claim to be grateful, but the lesson says true gratitude is the path of the siddiqīn—the truthful ones. Is your gratitude merely lip service, or does it move you to action? Think of one blessing you received today—did it increase your obedience? If not, what will you do tomorrow to make your gratitude real?

Definition and Comprehensive Scope of Patience (Sabr)
Al-Sawi defines patience and explains its encompassing role in religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Al-Sawi's definition of patience and how patience and gratitude together encompass all commanded and prohibited acts in religion.

Core Idea
Patience and gratitude are the two pillars that cover all of religious practice.

Al-Sawi now turns to the third principle of the path: patience (ṣabr). He defines it in a practical, spiritual sense and then makes a profound claim: patience and gratitude together encompass the entire religion. This passage will show you that sabr is not just about bearing trials but includes every act of obedience and avoidance of sin, while shukr manifests through tongue, heart, and limbs.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of patience
Start by reading Al-Sawi's definition: patience upon affliction is restraining the soul with contentment at the divine decree without agitation. Notice the key elements: restraint, contentment, and absence of agitation.
The definition is action-oriented, not passive.
2
Identify the three types of patience
The passage later clarifies that patience is of three kinds: upon obedience, away from sin, and upon calamity. Look for these in the commentary and the main text.
This expands the common understanding of patience.
3
Connect patience with gratitude
Al-Sawi pairs patience and gratitude, stating that together they encompass all religion. Gratitude is also threefold: with tongue, heart, and limbs. See how these six categories cover all commanded and prohibited acts.
This is the key insight of the passage.
4
Reflect on the comprehensiveness
Ask yourself: How does patience on obedience differ from patience on calamity? How does gratitude with the heart differ from gratitude with limbs? This shows the depth of these two virtues.

The principles of the path leading to Allah. Thirdly: Patience. The third is patience upon affliction, and it is: restraining the soul from what befalls it of disagreeable things, with contentment at the decree of the Sovereign Owner without agitation. And to it he pointed by his saying: 'And be upon His affliction, from illness, narrowness of livelihood, loss of wealth, family, harm from someone, and other things, and including the taklīfī rulings like prayer and fasting, patient' i.e., greatly patient, for the Exalted loves His patient servant. He said: 'And give glad tidings to the patient' and 'Indeed the patient will be given their reward without account.' And patience is a description of those of determination and high aspirations. And there have come concerning it and concerning gratitude such verses and noble hadiths that if they were followed they would lead to excessive length taking away from the purpose. In summary, both of them encompass all of religion, from commanded and prohibited acts. So sufficient are they as praise for one who is characterized by them; reflect. He justified the demand for patience by his saying: 'For every matter' i.e., patience is demanded of you because everything that emerges in existence is by the decree, i.e., by its cause. His saying: 'Patience upon affliction' is like patience upon obedience and away from sin. His saying: 'Both encompass all of religion' and its clarification: that patience is either upon obedience, or away from sin, or upon calamity; and gratitude is either with the tongue, or with the heart, or with the limbs. And there is no doubt that they both have gathered the landmarks of religion, which is compliance with commanded acts and avoidance of prohibited acts.

The passage emphasizes that patience and gratitude together encompass all of religion, providing a breakdown: patience has three types (upon obedience, away from sin, upon calamity) and gratitude has three types (with tongue, heart, limbs). This shows that these two virtues cover all commanded and prohibited acts.

Definition and Types of Patience (Sabr)
Sawi defines patience, enumerates its three types, and asserts that patience and gratitude cover all religion.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the definition of patience according to Sawi, its three types, and how patience and gratitude together encompass all of the religion's commands and prohibitions.

Core Idea
Patience is steadfastness in obedience, restraint from sin, and acceptance of divine decree.

Patience (ṣabr) is a foundational virtue in Islam. In this lesson, we explore Sawi's definition and classification of patience. He explains that patience involves restraining the soul from agitation in the face of hardship, while being satisfied with Allah's decree. Moreover, patience and gratitude collectively cover all aspects of religious life—both the commanded and the prohibited. Understanding this comprehensive view helps us appreciate the central role of patience in the path to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to get an overview. Pay attention to the definition and the enumeration of types.
2
Identify the three types of patience
Find the explicit listing: patience upon obedience, from disobedience, and upon calamity. Note how each differs.
The passage also mentions three types of gratitude to contrast.
3
Understand the encompassing statement
Reflect on the claim that patience and gratitude cover all of religion. Consider how fulfilling commands and avoiding prohibitions relates to these two qualities.
This is a key doctrinal point.

(١) سورة البقرة : (١٥٥).
(٢) سورة الزمر : (١٠).

The foundations of the path leading to Allah: thirdly: patience. And it is: restraining the soul from that which befalls it of what does not suit it, with satisfaction at the decree of the sovereign chooser, without agitation. And to it He alluded by His saying: 'And be upon His trial, from illness, straitened living, loss of wealth, family, and harm from someone, and other than that, and from it the legal obligations like prayer and fasting, patient' i.e., very patient, for He, exalted, loves His patient servant. And patience is a description of those of resolve and high ambitions. And there is no doubt that both have gathered the landmarks of religion, which is compliance with the commanded and avoidance of the prohibited. (1) Surah al-Baqarah: 155. (2) Surah az-Zumar: 10. 6

The Sixth Principle: Voluntary Hunger
Explains the sixth principle: voluntary hunger, its practice, benefits, and supporting sayings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual significance of voluntary hunger, its definition, benefits, and the wisdom behind it as taught by the gnostics and the Prophet (peace be upon him).

Core Idea
Voluntary hunger is a spiritual discipline that humbles the soul and purifies the heart.

The sixth principle of the spiritual path is voluntary hunger. This lesson explores why hunger is sought, how it is practiced, and the profound benefits it brings, as explained by the author and illuminated by sayings of the gnostics and a Prophetic hadith.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition of voluntary hunger
Read the opening lines to grasp what voluntary hunger means: not eating more than one light meal per day and night from lawful food, especially avoiding doubtful sources.
The practice is initially difficult and requires much fasting as a bridle.
2
Learn why hunger is sought
Notice the three benefits mentioned: it produces humility, dissolves earthy and watery parts, and purifies the heart. Also, it weakens the thoughts of the lower self.
These benefits are interconnected and lead to spiritual progress.
3
Reflect on the sayings of the gnostics
Read the three sayings that contrast hunger and satiety. Each highlights a different aspect: keys to worlds, fire and fuel, and the animalistic nature of eating twice daily.
These sayings reinforce the wisdom of hunger.
4
Consider the Prophetic hadith
The hadith warns against filling the stomach, which is the worst vessel. This underscores the virtue of hunger and moderation.
This final proof from the Prophet (PBUH) seals the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادساً: الجوع
السادس: الجوع اختياراً، بألا يأكل أكثر من أكلة خفيفة في يومه وليلته من الحلال، وهو ما جهل أصله ولا يمكنه ذلك في ابتداء أمره إلا بكثرة الصوم؛ فإنه لجام السائرين.
قوله: (الجوع اختياراً) إنما طلب الجوع؛ لأن به يحصل الذل، ويتحلل من الأجزاء الترابية والمائية بقدر ما يكون، فيصفو القلب، ولأن خواطر النفس لا تضعف إلا بذلك.
قال بعض العارفين: (مفتاح الدنيا الشبع، ومفتاح الآخرة الجوع). وقال بعضهم: (الشبع نار والشهوة مثل الحطب يتولد منه الإحراق، ولا تنطفئ ناره حتى تحرق صاحبها). وقال بعضهم: (من أراد أن يأكل في اليوم مرتين فليبن له معلفاً). وفي الحديث: «ما ملأ ابن آدم وعاء شراً من بطنه».
Sixthly: Hunger. The sixth: voluntary hunger, by not eating more than a light meal in his day and night from what is lawful, and it is what one does not know its origin and he cannot do that at the beginning of his affair except by much fasting; for it is the bridle of the wayfarers. His saying: (voluntary hunger) – hunger is sought only because through it humility is obtained, and the earthy and watery parts are dissolved to the extent possible, so the heart becomes clear, and because the thoughts of the soul do not weaken except by that. Some gnostics said: 'The key to this world is satiety, and the key to the Hereafter is hunger.' And some of them said: 'Satiety is fire and desire is like fuel, from which burning is generated, and its fire is not extinguished until it burns its owner.' And some of them said: 'Whoever wants to eat twice a day, let him build a manger for himself.' And in the hadith: 'The son of Adam does not fill a vessel worse than his stomach.'
Source pages p. 467

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the sixth principle in the spiritual path to God: voluntary hunger. It opens by defining it: one should not eat more than a light meal each day and night from lawful food, especially food whose origin is unknown (doubtful matters), though this is not possible at first without much fasting, which is described as the 'bridle of the wayfarers' (i.e., it restrains the soul). The author then explains why hunger is sought: it produces humility (al-dhull), dissolves the physical elements of earth and water that weigh down the heart, thereby purifying the heart, and it also weakens the thoughts of the lower self (al-nafs). Three sayings from the gnostics (al-ʿārifīn) reinforce these benefits: first, that the key to this world is satiety (shabaʿ) and the key to the next world is hunger; second, that satiety is a fire and desire is its fuel, which burns the person unless extinguished; third, that eating twice a day is a sign of animalistic behavior (building a manger). The passage concludes with the Prophetic hadith that the worst vessel a person fills is his stomach, underscoring the virtue of hunger over satiety.

Key Terms
الجوع
الاختيار
الذل
لجام السائرين
الشبع
المعلف
خواطر النفس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hunger is merely physical deprivation with no spiritual benefit.
Correction: The passage clarifies that voluntary hunger is a spiritual discipline that produces humility, purifies the heart, and weakens the lower self, leading to closeness to Allah.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is described as the 'bridle of the wayfarers'?
A) Hunger
B) Fasting
C) Lawful food
D) Humility

Key Takeaway
Voluntary hunger is a foundational spiritual practice that leads to humility, heart purification, and control over the lower self, as affirmed by the gnostics and the Prophet (PBUH).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you felt genuine spiritual humility because of hunger? If never, what is holding you back from deliberately experiencing this discipline?
?
The passage says 'the key to this world is satiety, and the key to the Hereafter is hunger.' Which key are you using to unlock your daily life? What would it take to reach for the other key starting tomorrow?
?
Satiety is called a fire and desire its fuel. What specific desire in your life is feeding that fire? Are you willing to starve that desire by voluntarily embracing hunger, even just one meal a week?

The Sixth Station: Hunger by Choice and Its Spiritual Benefits
Al-Sawi explains the discipline of hunger and its effects on the heart.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual practice of hunger by choice, its benefits for purifying the heart, and its connection to the quality of one's deeds.

Core Idea
Hunger breaks the ego, purifies the heart, and determines the quality of your deeds.

This lesson explores the sixth spiritual station: hunger by choice. Al-Sawi explains how controlling one's appetite through voluntary hunger leads to humility, heart purification, and righteous actions. He supports this with prophetic wisdom and sayings of the gnostics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and English version to get an overview of the station.
Notice the hadith and the quotes from the gnostics.
2
Identify the definition of hunger by choice
Find how Al-Sawi defines 'hunger by choice' and its requirements, including the role of fasting.
3
Understand the benefits of hunger
Look at the reasons given: humility, dissolving earthly and watery parts, purifying the heart, and weakening the thoughts of the soul.
These are the spiritual fruits of voluntary hunger.
4
Reflect on the sayings and hadith
The quotes contrast satiety and hunger. Reflect on the metaphor of satiety as fire and desire as firewood.
The hadith warns against filling the stomach excessively.
5
Apply the principle of food affecting deeds
The concluding principle teaches that the type of food (lawful, forbidden, ambiguous) directly influences the quality of one's deeds.
This is a key spiritual law: righteousness is the fruit of food.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سادساً : الجوع
السادس : الجوع اختياراً، بألا يأكل أكثر من أكلة خفيفة في يومه الحلال، وهو ما جهل أصله ولا يمكنه ذلك في ابتداء أمره وليلته من إلا بكثرة الصّوم؛ فإنّه لجام السائرين.
قوله : (الجوع اختياراً : إِنَّما طلب الجوع؛ لأنَّ به يحصل الذُّلُّ، ويتحلَّل من الأجزاء الترابية والمائية بقدر ما يكون، فيصفو القلب، ولأنَّ خواطر النَّفس لا تضعف إلَّا بالجوع).
قال بعض العارفين: (مفتاح الدُّنيا الشبع، ومفتاح الآخرة الجوع).
وقال بعضهم : الشَّبع نار والشَّهوة مثل الحطب يتولد منه الإحراق، ولا تنطفئ ناره حتّى تحرق صاحبها.
وقال بعضهم : (من أراد أن يأكل في اليوم مرتين ؛ فليبن له معلفاً).
وفي الحديث: «ما ملأ ابنُ آدم وعاء شراً من بطنه».
واعلم أنَّ العدل ثمرة المأكول، فالأكل الحرام لا ينشأ عنه إلا أعمال خبيثة محرَّمة، والحلالُ الصِّرف لا ينشأ عنه إلا الأعمال الصَّالحة، والمتشابه ينشأ عنه أعمال مختلطة لا تخلو عن الرّياء والعجب والخواطر الرّديّة.
Sixth: Hunger. The sixth: Hunger by choice, meaning one eats no more than a light meal in his day of lawful food, whose origin is unknown, and he cannot do that at the beginning of his affair and his night except by much fasting; for it is the bridle of the wayfarers. His saying: (Hunger by choice: Hunger is sought only because through it humility is obtained, and one is dissolved from earthly and watery parts as much as there is, so the heart becomes clear, and because the thoughts of the soul do not weaken except through hunger). Some of the gnostics said: 'The key of this world is satiety, and the key of the Hereafter is hunger.' And some of them said: 'Satiety is a fire, and desire is like firewood; from it ignition is produced, and its fire is not extinguished until it burns its owner.' And some of them said: 'Whoever wants to eat twice a day, let him build a manger for himself.' And in the hadith: 'The son of Adam does not fill a vessel worse than his stomach.' And know that righteousness is the fruit of food: forbidden food produces nothing but evil, forbidden deeds; pure lawful food produces nothing but righteous deeds; and ambiguous food produces mixed deeds that are not free from ostentation, self-admiration, and evil thoughts.
Source pages p. 468

Commentary
This passage defines the sixth spiritual station: hunger, specifically hunger by choice. It begins by stating the practice: not eating more than a light meal per day of lawful food whose origin is unknown, and that this is only possible through frequent fasting, which is described as the bridle of the wayfarers. The commentary then explains the reason for seeking hunger: it produces humility, dissolves earthly and watery elements from the body thereby purifying the heart, and weakens the thoughts of the ego. The passage then quotes several sayings: a gnostic states that the key to this world is satiety and the key to the Hereafter is hunger; another compares satiety to fire and desire to firewood; another says that anyone who wishes to eat twice daily should build a manger, implying that such behavior is animalistic. The hadith from the Prophet (peace be upon him) is cited: the worst vessel the son of Adam can fill is his stomach. Finally, a general principle is added: the quality of one's food determines the quality of one's deeds—forbidden food leads to forbidden actions, pure lawful food leads to righteous actions, and ambiguous food leads to mixed actions tainted by ostentation and self-admiration.

Key Terms

الذل
الصوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Hunger is asceticism for its own sake, a way to punish the body.
Correction: Hunger is not an end in itself, but a means to break the ego, purify the heart, and align one's deeds with divine pleasure.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the primary reason for practicing hunger by choice?
A) To weaken the body and reduce physical strength
B) To produce humility, purify the heart, and weaken evil thoughts
C) To earn reward through fasting only
D) To save money on food

Key Takeaway
Hunger by choice is a spiritual discipline that purifies the heart and determines the quality of one's deeds, as food type directly influences actions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
What indulgence in your daily life—whether in food or comfort—have you been using to avoid the humility that hunger would bring? Are you willing to trade that temporary fullness for a clear heart and a stronger connection with Allah? Start by skipping one meal this week with the intention of breaking your ego.
?
You know that the key to the Hereafter is hunger, yet how many times have you filled your stomach to the point of heaviness and spiritual dullness? What concrete change can you make today to choose hunger over satiety, knowing that your deeds depend on what you consume? Commit to one day of voluntary hunger before this week ends.
?
The scholars said the key to this world is satiety—what worldly attachment keeps you from seeking the key to the Hereafter? Is your love for a full belly stronger than your love for meeting Allah? Ask yourself: if I died tonight, would my last meal have been a means of righteousness or a source of regret?

The Seventh Station: Seclusion and Its Benefits
Al-Sawi explains the importance of seclusion except for shaykh and necessary interactions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the importance of seclusion in spiritual development, its necessary exceptions, and its grounding in prophetic and scholarly tradition.

Core Idea
Seclusion from people protects the heart's light, except when guidance or necessity calls.

Al-Sawi continues describing the spiritual stations by addressing seclusion. He makes clear that retiring from society is not about isolationism but about safeguarding the heart from the spiritual darkness that even innocent social mixing can bring. This lesson unpacks his precise guidelines and their scriptural basis.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and exceptions
Notice that Al-Sawi first defines seclusion as being from all people, then immediately lists three exceptions: one's shaykh, a righteous helper, and necessary transactions. This careful balance prevents misunderstanding.
2
Understand the reason given
The reason mixing with people brings darkness to the heart, even if no sin occurs. Why do you think that is? Consider how much of our attention and spiritual energy can be drained by constant social interaction.
3
Examine the supporting evidence
Al-Sawi supports his teaching with a hadith about the best people being the mujahid and then the one who withdraws to worship, a saying about the benefits of seclusion, and a maxim from Ibn 'Ata'illah about reflection.
The hadith is from Muslim and others.
4
Reflect on the applied wisdom
The passage ends with a hadith fragment warning of a time when maintaining religion becomes extremely difficult. Seclusion is presented as a shield for one's faith.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
سابعاً : العزلة
السابع : العزلة عن النَّاس قاطبةً إلا عن شيخه المربّي له، أو أخ صالح يعينه على الطَّاعة والهمة، وإلا لضرورة بيع أو شراء، إذ مخالطة النّاس تُكسب القلب ظلمة، لو فرض أنّها تخلو عن ارتكاب محرم.
قوله : (العزلة عن النَّاس قاطبة) أي لما فيها من خيري الدنيا والآخرة ؛ لما ورد أنَّ رجلاً قال : يا رسول الله ؛ أيُّ النَّاس أفضل ؟ قال : رجل يجاهد بنفسه وماله في سبيل الله ، قال : ثم من؟ قال : «رجل يعتزل في شعب من الشعاب يعبد ربه». وقال بعضهم : ( من أراد أن يسلم له دينه، وأن يستريح بدنه، ويقل غمه ؛ فليعتزل النَّاس).
وقال السَّكندري في حكمه : (ما نفع القلب مثل عزلة يدخل بها میدان فكرة).
وفي الحديث : ليأتين على النَّاس زمان لا يسلم لذي دين دينه إلَّا
Seventh: Seclusion. The seventh: Seclusion from all people except from one's shaykh who trains him, or a righteous brother who helps him in obedience and resolve, and except for necessity of buying or selling, because mixing with people earns the heart darkness, even if it is supposed to be free from committing a forbidden act. His saying: (Seclusion from all people) i.e., because of the good of this world and the Hereafter in it; based on what has been related that a man said: 'O Messenger of God, which people are best?' He said: 'A man who strives with his life and wealth in the way of God.' He said: 'Then who?' He said: 'A man who withdraws into a mountain pass to worship his Lord.' And some of them said: 'Whoever wants his religion to be safe for him, his body to rest, and his grief to be little, let him withdraw from people.' And Al-Sakandari said in his maxims: 'Nothing benefits the heart like seclusion by which it enters the field of reflection.' And in the hadith: 'There will come upon people a time when no one with religion will keep his religion except...'
Source pages p. 469

Commentary
This passage defines the seventh spiritual station: seclusion from all people. It specifies exceptions: one's training shaykh, a righteous brother who aids in obedience and determination, and necessary transactions like buying and selling. The reason given is that mixing with people brings darkness to the heart, even if no sin is committed. The commentary then elaborates: seclusion contains the good of both worlds, citing the hadith where the Prophet (peace be upon him) ranks a mujāhid first, then a man who withdraws to worship his Lord in a mountain pass. Another saying encourages seclusion for safety of religion, bodily rest, and reduced grief. A maxim from Al-Sakandari (Ibn 'Ata'illah) states that nothing benefits the heart like seclusion that allows entry into the field of reflection. A final hadith fragment warns of a future time when maintaining one's religion will be extremely difficult.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Seclusion means completely isolating oneself from all people at all times.
Correction: Seclusion has specific exceptions: one's shaykh, a righteous helper, and necessary transactions. It is a disciplined practice, not total isolation.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the effect of mixing with people on the heart?
A) It brightens the heart
B) It gives the heart darkness even if free of sin
C) It strengthens the heart
D) It has no effect

Key Takeaway
Seclusion is a spiritual discipline that protects the heart from spiritual darkness, but it must be balanced with guidance from a shaykh, righteous companionship, and necessary worldly engagements.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
Al-Sakandari said: 'Nothing benefits the heart like seclusion by which it enters the field of reflection.' When did you last sit alone with your thoughts purely for the sake of Allah, without distractions? If it has been a while, what is the first step you will take tomorrow to reclaim that space?

Silence Except for Remembrance of Allah
Al-Sawi explains the eighth principle: silence except for remembrance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual principle of silence and its role in achieving concentration on Allah, as well as the conditions and wisdom behind it.

Core Idea
Silence preserves concentration; speech disperses the heart.

This lesson explores the eighth principle of the spiritual path: silence except for remembrance. Al-Sawi explains why silence is a means to attain spiritual unification (al-jam'iyya), especially when mixing with people is necessary. He connects it to the purification of the heart from distractions and cites scriptural and traditional evidence for preferring silence.

Guided Path
1
Read the principle and its explanation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the principle is stated and immediately qualified: silence except for remembrance, and only when mixing with people out of necessity.
The principle is derived from the earlier dictum about purifying the heart from other than Allah.
2
Understand the condition of necessity
The passage emphasizes that silence is recommended only when one is forced to interact with people. This is not an absolute command to avoid all speech, but a practical measure for spiritual focus.
The word 'aghyar' (others) includes wealth, family, status, and even knowledge and deeds that distract from Allah.
3
Grasp the wisdom behind preferring silence
Al-Sawi quotes a hadith ('Whoever desires safety, let him adhere to silence') and lists the afflictions of speech, such as self-display and harming the soul. The spiritual masters chose silence because they recognized these dangers.
The simile of the fox fleeing from place to place illustrates the caution needed to protect one's religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثامن : الصَّمتُ إلّا عن ذكر الله تعالى؛ فإنَّ الكلام يوجب التَّفَرُّقَ ، والمطلوب الجمعية، وهذا على تقدير مخالطة النّاس لضرورة، وهذه مأخوذة من قولنا وخلص القلب من الأغيار أي : ممّا سوى الله تعالى؛ من مال وزوجة وولد وجاه وعلم وعمل وغيرها من كلِّ مشغل عن تعلُّق القلب بالرَّبِّ ، (بالجد) - بكسر الجيم - أي: الاجتهاد؛ أي: بسببه قال تعالى : (وَالَّذِينَ جَهَدُوا فِينَا لَنَهْدِيَنَّهُمْ سُبُلَنَا ) فرَّ بدينه من قرية إلى قرية ومن شاهق إلى شاهق، ومن جحر إلى جحر ؛ كالثعلب الذي يزوغ قوله :(الصَّمت) أي لما ورد من سره أن يسلم فليلزم الصَّمت وإنَّما آثر القوم السكوت لما علموا في الكلام من الآفات وحفظ النَّفس وإظهار صفات المدح والميل إلى أن يتميز عن أشكاله النُّطق، وغير ذلك من آفات الكلام.
The eighth: silence except for remembrance of Allah the Exalted; for speech causes dispersion, and what is sought is concentration (al-jam'iyya), and this is on the assumption of mixing with people out of necessity. And this is taken from our saying: 'and the heart is purified from others (al-aghyar),' meaning from other than Allah the Exalted—from wealth, spouse, child, status, knowledge, deed, and everything that preoccupies from the attachment of the heart to the Lord. (Bi al-jidd) – with kasra of the jim – meaning: exertion; that is, because of it He said: 'And those who strive in Us, We will surely guide them to Our ways.' He fled with his religion from village to village, from peak to peak, from den to den like a fox that zigzags. His saying: 'silence' means because it has come: 'Whoever desires safety, let him adhere to silence.' And indeed the people preferred silence because they knew of the afflictions in speech, the preservation of the soul, the display of praiseworthy qualities, and the inclination to distinguish oneself from one's peers through speech, and other such afflictions of speech.
Source pages p. 470

Commentary
This passage defines the eighth principle of the spiritual path: silence (al-samt) except for the remembrance of Allah. The author explains that speech causes dispersion (tafarruq) while what is desired is concentration or unification (al-jam'iyya) of the heart on Allah. This rule applies when one is forced to mix with people out of necessity. The principle is derived from the earlier statement 'cleanse the heart from others (al-aghyar)' meaning anything other than Allah that distracts the heart. Then he cites a verse from Surat al-Ankabut (29:69) about striving (jihad) as the means to guidance, followed by a simile of a fox fleeing to protect his religion, indicating the need for caution. He then comments on the word 'silence' in the original text, explaining that it refers to the hadith: 'Whoever desires safety, let him adhere to silence.' Finally, he notes that the spiritual masters preferred silence because they recognized the many afflictions of speech, such as harming the soul and ostentation, and they sought to distinguish themselves through silence.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That silence is always required in all circumstances.
Correction: The passage explicitly limits this principle to situations of mixing with people out of necessity, and it excludes remembrance of Allah. Silence is a means to achieve concentration, not an absolute command.

Checkpoint

B) Because speech causes dispersion and the desired state is concentration on Allah

Key Takeaway
Silence is a spiritual practice that guards against dispersion of the heart, but it is not absolute—it is prescribed when mixing with people out of necessity and always allows for remembrance of Allah.

The Proper Fear of Allah: Not Just Fear of Punishment
Al-Sawi distinguishes fear of punishment from reverential fear.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between fear of Allah's punishment and reverential fear of His majesty, and why the spiritual journey requires the latter.

Core Idea
True fear of Allah is reverence for His majesty, not dread of punishment.

Spiritual striving (mujahada) is not merely about obeying rules out of fear of punishment. Al-Sawi explains that the highest form of fear is rooted in awe and veneration of Allah's majesty. In this passage, he unpacks a crucial condition for the spiritual journey, supported by Quranic verses, and warns against being a 'bad servant' who only acts when threatened.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Let the author's words establish the core idea before diving into interpretation.
Pay attention to the Quranic citations – they are central to the argument.
2
Understand the definition of mujahada
Notice that mujahada is defined as opposing the soul's desires together with fear of Allah after repentance. This sets the stage for the condition that follows.
The fear here is not unspecified – it is connected to repentance.
3
Identify the condition for the spiritual journey
Find where the author states the condition: one should not be afraid of Allah's punishment alone. Otherwise, he would be a bad servant. The true fear is out of veneration and awe.
The phrase 'فافهم' (so understand) signals a crucial point.
4
Examine the Quranic evidence
Note how the author uses two verses: one mentions 'fearing the station of his Lord' (مقام ربه) and the other does not say 'punishment.' This proves that fear of majesty is the intended type.
Reflect on why the author emphasizes the wording of the Quran.
5
Contrast the two types of fear
Compare the one who fears majesty (fear never ceases, even in extreme worship) and the one who fears punishment (focus only on commands and prohibitions). This highlights the depth of the spiritual path.
The author calls these two 'different paths' (مشربين).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والمجاهدة تكون بمخالفة النَّفس في هواها مع الخوف من الله تعالى بعد التوبة، قال تعالى : (وَأَمَّا مَنْ خَافَ مَقَامَ رَبِّهِ، وَنَهَى النَّفْسَ عَنِ أَهْوَى فَإِنَّ الْجَنَّةَ هِيَ الْمَأْوَى) أي: جنَّة الشُّهود في الدُّنيا، وجنَّة الخلود في العقبى. إلَّا أنّ شرط السَّير ألا يكون خائفاً من عذاب الله، وإلّا كان عبد سوء لا يعمل إلّا إذا خاف العقاب، بل يخافه إجلالاً ومهابة ولذا قال تعالى : (وَلِمَنْ خَافَ مَقَامَ رَبِّهِ) ولم يقل : عذاب ربه، فافهم. قوله : (ألا يكون خائفاً من عذاب الله) أي: ألا يقصر خوفه على العذاب، بل يجعل خوفه من جلال الله وهيبته، وصاحب هذا المقام لا ينقطع خوفه ولو تقطَّع إرباً إرباً في العبادة، وأما الخائف من العذاب؛ فمداره على امتثال المأمورات، واجتناب المنهيات. قوله : (فافهم) : إنَّما أمر بالفهم ؛ لدقة المقام وتغاير المشربين.
And striving (mujahada) consists of opposing the soul in its whims together with fear of Allah the Exalted after repentance. He said: 'But as for he who feared the station of his Lord and forbade the soul from [its] desire, then indeed Paradise is the refuge.' Meaning: the Paradise of witnessing in this world and the Paradise of eternity in the afterlife. However, the condition of the journey is that one should not be afraid of the punishment of Allah; otherwise he would be a bad servant who only acts when he fears punishment, rather he fears Him out of veneration and awe. Therefore He said: 'And for him who fears the station of his Lord' and did not say 'the punishment of his Lord'; so understand. His saying: '(that he should not be) afraid of the punishment of Allah' means that he should not limit his fear to punishment, but rather make his fear from the majesty of Allah and His awe. The possessor of this station does not cease his fear even if he were cut into pieces in worship. As for the one who fears punishment, his concern is limited to performing commands and avoiding prohibitions. His saying: 'so understand' – indeed he commanded understanding because of the subtlety of the station and the diversity of paths.
Source pages p. 471

Commentary
This passage explains the true nature of spiritual striving (mujahada) and the correct kind of fear of Allah. It begins by defining mujahada as opposing the soul's desires accompanied by fear of Allah after repentance. The author then cites a Quranic verse (al-Nazi'at 79:40-41) about fearing the 'station of the Lord' and interprets Paradise as both the Paradise of witnessing (divine manifestation) in this world and the eternal Paradise in the afterlife. He then introduces a critical condition: the spiritual journey (al-sayr) requires that one's fear not be limited to fear of punishment, for that would make one a bad servant who only acts out of dread. Rather, one should fear Allah out of veneration (ijlaal) and awe (mahaaba). This is supported by another verse (al-Rahman 55:46) which specifies 'fearing the station of his Lord' not 'the punishment of his Lord.' The author then comments on the phrase from the text, explaining that fear should not be confined to punishment but should be rooted in Allah's majesty and awe. Such a person remains in a state of fear even if cut to pieces in worship, while the one who fears punishment only focuses on obedience and avoidance of sin. Finally, the author emphasizes the need to understand this subtle distinction because of the deep nature of the station and the different spiritual paths (mashrabayn).

Key Terms
المجاهدة
الخوف
مقام الرب
إجلال
مهابة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That any fear of Allah is sufficient for spiritual growth.
Correction: Fear must be of Allah's majesty and station, not merely fear of punishment, otherwise one remains a 'bad servant' who acts only when threatened.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the condition for the spiritual journey concerning fear?
A) One must fear Allah's punishment at all times
B) One must not fear Allah at all, only love Him
C) One should not fear punishment alone but fear Allah out of veneration and awe
D) Fear is unnecessary after repentance

Key Takeaway
Genuine fear of Allah is rooted in awe of His majesty, not merely in dread of punishment, and this distinction defines the sincere servant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you examine your worship, is it driven more by a desire to avoid Hellfire or by a profound sense of awe at who Allah is? What does that reveal about the depth of your faith? Will you ask Allah to grant you fear of His station, not just fear of His punishment?
?
The one who fears the station of his Lord never loses his fear, even if cut to pieces in worship. Does your fear of Allah persist when you are comfortable and alone, or does it fade when there is no immediate threat? What would it take for you to reach that unshakable state?
?
Are you content with being a 'bad servant' who only acts when reminded of punishment, or do you long to be among those who fear Allah out of veneration? What specific change can you make today to begin shifting your motivation from punishment to majesty?
