?
The passage says that Allah bestows Muhammadan character upon you as a garment. Do you live as though you are clothed in these traits, or are you still wearing the rags of your old habits? What is one trait from the list (forbearance, mercy, humility, etc.) that you lack the most, and what concrete step will you take to acquire it through struggle?
?
If the ultimate result of mujahada is becoming Allah's vicegerent on Earth, do you believe your current state reflects that station? Where is the gap between what you claim to be and what you actually are, and will you commit to praying for divine help to close it?

The Sign of Spiritual Purification: Equanimity Towards Praise and Blame
The author identifies the sign of removal of human pretensions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the sign of spiritual purification as the equality of praise and blame in one's heart, and the preference for blame.

Core Idea
The sign of true purification is complete equanimity towards praise, blame, giving, and withholding.

The author not only describes the results of mujahada but also provides a clear sign for recognizing genuine purification. This sign involves detachment from people's opinions and reactions, even preferring blame and denial over their opposites.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on the sign
Carefully read the Arabic passage that defines the sign of purification, then its translation.
Note the key phrase: 'wa 'alāmatu zawāli ar-ru'ūnāt...'
2
Understand the concept of equanimity
The sign is that praise and blame, giving and withholding, people's turning towards and away become equal in one's sight. Reflect on how difficult this is.
3
Analyze the preference for blame
The passage adds that the purified person even prefers blame, denial, and turning away. Why would that be? Consider the danger of attachment to praise.
This indicates complete detachment from ego.
4
Apply to daily life
Think of personal experiences where you were affected by praise or blame. How can you work towards this equanimity?
This is a practical spiritual goal.

?

The Fruit of Spiritual Struggle: Transformation of the Self
Al-Sawi describes the transformation through spiritual struggle and the attainment of prophetic character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the fruit of sincere spiritual struggle is the transformation of blameworthy traits into praiseworthy ones and the attainment of Muhammadan character, making one a vicegerent of Allah.

Core Idea
Struggle against the self transforms blame into praise and clothes you in prophetic character.

Al-Sawi begins by quoting a verse on the value of intellect and discipline, then explains the process of spiritual struggle. He describes how God replaces blameworthy attributes with praiseworthy ones, ultimately clothing the seeker with the very character of the Prophet (peace be upon him). This passage lays out the direct result of sincere self-discipline.

Guided Path
1
Read the opening verse and its explanation
The lesson opens with a poetic verse emphasizing that intellect and discipline are God's best gifts and constitute the life of a person.
Reflect on why discipline is paired with intellect.
2
Understand the process of struggling against the soul
Al-Sawi explains that when you struggle against the soul through discipline, you achieve deliverance from the darkness of others and your blameworthy attributes become praiseworthy.
This transformation is the direct fruit of jihad al-nafs.
3
Identify the Muhammadan character traits
The passage lists traits such as clemency, knowledge, compassion, mercy, humility, asceticism, piety, and generosity as the garments of prophetic character.
These are the qualities that God bestows on the seeker.
4
Recognize the ultimate goal
Al-Sawi states that the one adorned with these traits becomes God's vicegerent on earth.
This is a high station – a representative of Allah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ما وهب الله لامرئ هبه أحسن من عقله وأدبه هما حياة الفتى فإن عدما فإن فقد الحياة أجمل به

فإذا جاهدت النفس بما مرهان عليها - إن شاء الله تعالى - الخلوص من ظلمة الأغيار، وتبدلت صفاتها المذمومة بالصفات الممدوحة، فيخلع الحق تبارك وتعالى عليك خلع الأخلاق المحمدية من الحلم والعلم، والشفقة والرأفة والخضوع، والزهد والورع والسخاء، وغير ذلك من مكارم الأخلاق، كما أشرت إلى ذلك بقولي: لترتقي معالم الكمال أي: إلى معالم هي الأخلاق المحمدية، وحينئذ يكون هذا العبد خليفة الله في أرضه. وعلامة زوال الرعونات البشرية من القلب، والتحلي بالأخلاق المرضية: أن يستوي عنده المدح والذم، والمنع والإعطاء، وإقبال الناس عليه وإدبارهم، بل يرجح الذم والمنع والإدبار على مقابلها.

الكمالات، وهي

فتشبهوا إن لم تكونوا مثلهم إن التشبه بالرجال فلاح

قوله: (بل يرجح الذم والمنع ... إلخ): قال صاحب الحكمة في هذا المعنى: (ورود الفاقات أعياد المريدين)
What God has given to a man, His best gift is his intellect and his discipline; they are the life of the youth, and if they are lost, then loss of life is more beautiful. So when you struggle against the soul through the discipline imposed upon it—if God wills—[you will achieve] deliverance from the darkness of others, and its blameworthy attributes will be transformed into praiseworthy attributes. Then the Truth, Blessed and Exalted, will clothe you with the garments of Muhammadan character: clemency, knowledge, compassion, mercy, humility, asceticism, piety, generosity, and other noble traits of character, as I alluded to in my saying: 'That you may ascend to the landmarks of perfection'—meaning to the landmarks which are the Muhammadan characters. Then this servant becomes the vicegerent of God on His earth. And the sign of the removal of human frivolities from the heart and the adornment with pleasing character is that praise and blame, denial and giving, people's turning toward him and turning away from him become equal in his view; rather, he gives preference to blame, denial, and turning away over their opposites. [The perfections, and they are] Imitate, if you are not like them; for imitation of (noble) men is success. His saying: 'Rather, he gives preference to blame and denial ... etc.': The author of al-Ḥikam said concerning this meaning: 'The arrival of needs are the feast of the seekers.'
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a line of poetry and elaborates on the fruits of spiritual struggle (jihad al-nafs). The author begins by quoting a verse: the best gifts God gives are intellect and discipline, which constitute the life of a person. Then he explains that when one struggles against the soul with proper discipline, God willing, the soul is purified from the darkness of otherness (al-aghyar) and blameworthy traits are replaced with praiseworthy ones. God then clothes the seeker with the prophetic character traits (al-akhlaq al-Muhammadiyya), such as clemency, knowledge, compassion, humility, asceticism, and generosity. At this stage, the servant becomes God's vicegerent on earth. The sign of this transformation is complete equanimity: praise and blame, giving and withholding, people's acceptance or rejection all become equal to him; in fact, he prefers blame and deprivation over their opposites, because this indicates detachment from ego. The author then inserts a line of poetry encouraging emulation of the perfect, and cites a maxim from Ibn 'Ata'illah's al-Ḥikam: 'The arrival of needs are the feast of the seekers'—meaning that trials and needs are occasions for drawing closer to God. The overall teaching is that true spiritual maturity is measured not by external approval but by inner detachment and preference for humility.

Key Terms
جهاد النفس
الأخلاق المحمدية
الرعونات البشرية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the direct result of struggling against the soul through discipline?
A) Increased wealth and status
B) Transformation of blameworthy traits into praiseworthy ones
C) Ability to perform miracles
D) Popularity among people

Key Takeaway
The fruit of spiritual struggle is the transformation of character and attainment of prophetic traits, making one a vicegerent of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which specific blameworthy attribute of your soul—like pride, stinginess, or harshness—are you still protecting instead of struggling against it? What would it cost you to truly wage jihad against it today?
?
The passage lists prophetic traits: clemency, knowledge, compassion, mercy, humility, asceticism, piety, generosity. Which one do you feel most distant from in your daily life, and what will you do this week to draw closer to it?
?
Are you living as a vicegerent of God on earth, actively transforming your character and taking responsibility for His creation, or are you content with a comfortable, self-centered existence? What one change can you make starting today to step into that role?

The Sign of Prophetic Character: Equanimity in Praise and Blame
Al-Sawi presents the sign of attaining prophetic character: equanimity towards praise and blame, with preference for hardship.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the ultimate sign of spiritual maturity is complete equanimity towards praise and blame, even preferring what is difficult.

Core Idea
True spiritual maturity is measured by indifference to praise and preference for blame.

Having explained the transformation, Al-Sawi now gives the definitive sign that one has truly attained prophetic character: that praise and blame become equal, and one even prefers blame and hardship. He supports this with a profound maxim from Ibn 'Ata'illah. This passage helps us test our own spiritual state.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of the sign
Al-Sawi states that the sign of having removed human frivolities is that praise and blame, giving and denying, people's turning toward and away become equal.
This is a state of inner detachment from external approval.
2
Notice the stronger condition
The passage adds that the truly advanced person actually prefers blame, denial, and turning away over their opposites.
This shows a deep preference for humility and hardship.
3
Reflect on the poem about imitation
The inserted verse encourages emulating the perfect, indicating that one should strive to reach this state.
Imitation of the noble leads to success.
4
Consider the supporting maxim
Al-Sawi quotes Ibn 'Ata'illah: 'The arrival of needs are the feast of the seekers' – trials are occasions for spiritual growth.
This reinforces the idea that difficulties are gifts.

The Supplication of the Broken-Hearted and the Call for Humility
Al-Sawi presents a supplication for protection from worldly distractions and emphasizes the state of broken-heartedness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the supplication taught by Imam al-Sawi for protection from worldly distractions and the spiritual state of brokenness that it requires.

Core Idea
God is with the broken-hearted—humility is the key to His presence.

Imam al-Sawi presents a powerful supplication that the seeker should utter with complete humility. This lesson focuses on the du'a itself and the underlying spiritual condition of brokenness that invites God's presence. Let us explore the meaning of this sacred plea.

Guided Path
1
Read the supplication in Arabic
Read the Arabic text of the du'a carefully. Notice its wording: 'O Lord, do not cut me off from You by any cutter from every trial.'
The supplication is short but profound.
2
Understand the state of brokenness
Al-Sawi explains that the supplication should be uttered with lowliness (dhull) and that God is with those whose hearts are broken. Reflect on how humility and brokenness are prerequisites for divine nearness.
He cites a sacred hadith: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken for My sake.'
3
Identify what the supplication seeks
The du'a asks not to be cut off from God by any 'cutter' (qāṭiʿ) that distracts the heart from worship. The 'cutters' include love of wealth, children, status, and desires.
These are explained further in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
دعاء

(وقل) متضرعاً إلى ربك قولاً ملتبساً بالذل، فإن الله تعالى عند المنكسرة قلوبهم: يا رب لا تقطعني عنك بقاطع من كل فتنة يشتغل القلب بها عن العبودية، من حب المال والولد والجاه والشهوات إنما أموالكم وأولادكم فتنة، زين للناس حب الشهوات من النساء والبنين... الآية، يأيها الذين آمنوا لا تلهكم أموالكم ولا أولادكم عن ذكر الله ومن يفعل ذلك فأولئك هم الخسرون.

قوله: (بذل): جعله الشارح متعلقاً بمحذوف صفة لمصدر محذوف مفعول مطلق لـ (قل)، والباء للملابسة وفيه كلفة والأسهل جعل الجار متعلقاً بمحذوف حالاً من فاعل (قل)، والتقدير: قل: يا رب لا تقطعني ... إلخ، حال كونك ملتبساً بالذل.

والمجرور

قوله: (فإن الله تعالى عند المنكسرة قلوبهم): تعليل لما قبله، وفيه اقتباس من الحديث القدسي: «أنا عند المنكسرة قلوبهم من أجلي»

قوله: (من كل فتنة): بيان للقاطع.

وقوله: (من) حب المال ... إلخ) بيان للفتنة.

قوله: (إنما أموالكم وأولادكم فتنة ... إلخ): هذه أدلة ثلاثة على ما ذكره من أن حب المال والولد والشهوات من جملة القواطع.
Supplication. (And say), humbly imploring your Lord, a saying clothed in lowliness, for indeed God Almighty is with those whose hearts are broken: 'O Lord, do not cut me off from You by any cutter from every trial that occupies the heart with it from worship, from the love of wealth, children, status, and desires. [Qur'an: ] Your wealth and your children are only a trial. [Qur'an: ] Adorned for people is the love of desires from women and children... the verse. [Qur'an: ] O you who believe, let not your wealth and your children divert you from the remembrance of God; and whoever does that, they are the losers.' His saying: 'with lowliness' ('bādhal'): The commentator made it attached to an omitted [word] as an attribute of an omitted verbal noun which is the direct object of 'say' (qul), and the 'ba' for accompaniment, but there is difficulty in that; the easier [analysis] is to make the prepositional phrase attached to an omitted [word] as a circumstantial clause from the subject of 'say', and the estimation is: Say: O Lord, do not cut me off... etc., while you are clothed in lowliness. And the ma'rūd? His saying: 'For indeed God Almighty is with those whose hearts are broken': this is a reason for what precedes, and it contains a borrowing from the sacred hadith: 'I am with those whose hearts are broken for My sake.' His saying: 'from every trial': it is an explanation of the cutter. And his saying: 'from the love of wealth... etc.' is an explanation of the trial. His saying: 'Your wealth and your children are only a trial... etc.': these are three proofs for what he mentioned, that the love of wealth, children, and desires is among the cutters.
Source pages p. 482

Commentary
This passage presents a supplication (du'a) that the seeker should utter, clothed in humility and lowliness. The supplication asks God not to cut the servant off from Him by any 'cutter' (qāṭi')—meaning anything that distracts the heart from worship. The 'cutters' are specified as love of wealth, children, status, and desires. The author then provides grammatical analysis of the word 'bādhil' (meaning clothed in lowliness), noting two possible syntactic constructions. He then explains the clause 'for indeed God is with those whose hearts are broken' as a justification for the supplication, and cites a sacred hadith (al-ḥadīth al-qudsī) that God says 'I am with those whose hearts are broken for My sake.' The phrase 'from every trial' (min kull fitna) is an explanation of the 'cutter' (qāṭi'), and 'from the love of wealth...' specifies the nature of the trial. Finally, the author cites three Quranic verses (64:15, 3:14, 63:9) as evidence that these worldly attachments are indeed trials and distractions from God. The lesson emphasizes that the state of brokenness (‘taking recourse to God with a lowly heart’) is the condition that draws God's presence, and that genuine spiritual progress requires vigilance against the temptations of worldly attractions.

Key Terms
المنكسرة قلوبهم
الذل
الدعاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that the supplication asks for removal of all trials and difficulties.
Correction: The supplication specifically asks not to be cut off from God by trials, meaning that trials themselves can be beneficial if they do not distract from God. It is about maintaining connection despite trials, not avoiding them entirely.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what state should the supplication be uttered in?
A) With anger
B) With humility and lowliness
C) With confidence and pride
D) With doubt

Key Takeaway
Humility and brokenness before God are the conditions for His nearness, and the supplication seeks protection from being cut off by worldly distractions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you last made du'a, did you truly feel lowly and broken before God, or were you merely reciting words? What does that reveal about your heart's attachment to this world?
?
Name one 'cutter'—love of wealth, children, status, or desires—that has most recently pulled your heart away from Allah. Will you today beg Him to protect you from it, with tears of humility?
?
If God is with the broken-hearted, why do you so often try to appear strong and self-sufficient before Him? What would change in your life if you let yourself be truly broken in front of Him tonight?

Understanding the Cutters: Grammatical Analysis and Quranic Proofs
Al-Sawi provides grammatical analysis and Quranic proofs for the supplication's meaning.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the grammatical structure of the phrase 'with lowliness' (bādhal) and the Quranic evidence that worldly attachments are trials that can cut one off from God.

Core Idea
Worldly attachments are 'cutters' that distract from worship, confirmed by Quranic verses.

In the previous lesson, we learned the supplication and its emphasis on humility. Now, Imam al-Sawi provides a grammatical analysis of the word 'bādhal' (with lowliness) and cites three Quranic verses proving that love of wealth, children, and desires are indeed trials that can cut one off from God. Let us delve into this explanatory portion.

Guided Path
1
Analyze the grammar of 'bādhal'
Al-Sawi discusses two possible syntactic analyses of 'bādhal' (with lowliness). Read the explanation and try to understand both interpretations.
The first analysis sees it as an attribute of an omitted verbal noun; the second as a circumstantial clause.
2
Understand the 'cutter' and 'trial'
Al-Sawi says 'from every trial' (min kull fitna) explains the 'cutter', and 'from the love of wealth...' explains the 'trial'. Grasp this layered explanation.
The 'cutter' is the trial itself; the specific trials are those attachments.
3
Reflect on the three Quranic verses
Al-Sawi cites three verses: 'Your wealth and children are only a trial' (64:15), 'Adorned for people is the love of desires...' (3:14), and 'Let not your wealth and children divert you...' (63:9). These prove that these attachments are indeed cutters.
Each verse emphasizes the distracting nature of worldly things.

Worship for Reward as an Obstacle
Al-Sawi enumerates obstacles and explains the subtle blocker of worshipping for reward.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why worshipping for reward is considered an obstacle in the path to Allah, and what the correct intention for worship should be.

Core Idea
Worship for reward reduces you from a true servant to a mercenary.

This passage from al-Sawi's commentary warns against a subtle spiritual disease: worshipping Allah not for His sake, but for what you can get in return. The true believers worship Allah for His essence and out of obedience, not for reward. Any reward received is from His bounty, not due to any right of the servant.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and list the obstacles
Read the Arabic passage once. Then note the five obstacles mentioned at the beginning: pride, rancor, hypocrisy, self-admiration, and worship for reward.
Worship for reward is the focus of the lesson.
2
Understand the problem with worship for reward
The author argues that worshipping for reward or spiritual opening lowers the servant to the level of 'evil servants'. Ask yourself: why is this considered an obstacle?
The commentary clarifies it is not a sin but a low station.
3
Reflect on the correct intention
Read the explanation of worship for Allah's essence and the quote from David. Notice that Allah deserves worship even without Paradise or Hell. Then consider the servant's duty: self-purification, not demanding spiritual gifts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ومن القواطع : الكِبْرُ والحقد والرِّياء والعُجب، ومنها: العبادة لأجل حصول ثواب، أو حصول فَتْح لدنّي ليكون م أولياء الله، وإنَّما شأنهم أن يعبدوا الله تعالى لذاته وامتثالاً لأمره ونهيه، ثم إن حصل لهم فتح فذلك من فضله وإن حجبوا فذلك من عدله، إذ ليس للعبد على مولاه حقٌّ ، وإِنَّما الحقُّ له تعالى على العبد، فالعبد مطلوب بأن يخلّص نفسه من الرُّعونات النَّفسيَّة، وليس على الله تعالى أن يهبه المعارف القدسيّة، والذي يعبده لذلك معدود عندهم من عبيد السُّوء

قوله: (ومنها : العبادة ... إلخ) أي من جملة القواطع عن الله تعالى .

قوله : (وإنَّما شأنهم أن يعبدوا الله تعالى لذاته) : أي : لكونه مستحقاً وأهلاً للعبادة ورد في مناجاة داود : «يا داود؛ إن لم أخلق جنَّة ولا ناراً، أفلا أستحقُ أن أعبد ؟ » (۱) .

قوله : (إذ ليس للعبد على مولاه حقٌّ أي وأما قوله تعالى: كتب رَبُّكُمْ عَلَى نَفْسِهِ الرَّحْمَةُ ) (۲) ؛ فمعناه على سبيل التَّفضُّل والإحسان .

قوله : (من عبيد السُّوء): ليس المراد أنَّ ذلك حرام يعاقب عليه، بل المراد أنَّ ذلك انحطاط عن المراتب العليَّة .

الذين إذا لم يؤجروا لم يعملوا، وهذا ينافي كونه عبداً محضاً، قال العارف بالله تعالى ابن عطاء الله السكندري في «الحِكَم» (تشرُّفُك إلى ما بطن فيك من العيوب خير من تشوفك إلى ما حجب عنك من الغيوب) (1) .

قوله : (تشرُّفك إلى ما بطن فيك من العيوب) : أي : تطلُّعك وقصر نظرك على عيوبك واشتغالك بها وتخليص نفسك منها .

قوله: (خير من تشوفك إلى ما حجب عنك) : أي : أفضل من تطلعك إلى ما ستر عنك من المغيَّبات؛ لأنَّه تعالى لا يجب عليه شيء لعبيده .
And among the obstacles (qawati'): pride, rancor, hypocrisy, and self-admiration. And among them: worship for the sake of obtaining reward, or obtaining a spiritual opening (fatḥ) to be among the friends of Allah. Rather, their (the friends') affair is that they worship Allah the Exalted for His Essence and in compliance with His command and prohibition. Then if an opening occurs for them, it is from His bounty; and if they are prevented, it is from His justice, for the servant has no right (ḥaqq) over his Master. Rather, the right belongs to Him the Exalted over the servant. So the servant is required to purify himself from the follies of the soul, and it is not incumbent upon Allah the Exalted to bestow upon him sacred knowledge. And whoever worships Him for that (reward) is counted among them as evil servants ('abīd al-sū').

His saying: '(And among them: worship... etc.)' means from the totality of obstacles (qawāṭiʿ) from Allah the Exalted.

His saying: 'Rather, their affair is that they worship Allah the Exalted for His Essence' means because He is worthy and deserving of worship, and it is related in the intimate conversation of David: 'O David, if I had not created Paradise or Hell, would I not deserve to be worshipped?' (1)

His saying: 'For the servant has no right over his Master' means, and as for His saying the Exalted: 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself' (2), its meaning is by way of favor and beneficence.

His saying: 'from evil servants (ʿabīd al-sū')': it is not meant that this is forbidden and punishable, but rather it is a decline from the high stations.

Those who, if not recompensed, do not work—this contradicts being a pure servant. The knower of Allah the Exalted, Ibn ʿAṭāʾ Allāh al-Sakandarī, said in al-Ḥikam: 'Your concern with what is hidden within you of faults is better than your concern with what is veiled from you of the unseen.' (1)

His saying: 'Your concern (tasharrufuka) with what is hidden within you of faults' means your looking and focusing your sight on your faults and occupying yourself with them and purifying yourself from them.

His saying: 'better than your concern with what is veiled from you' means better than your looking into what is concealed from you of the unseen, because the Exalted is not obligated to give anything to His servants.
Source pages p. 484

Commentary
This passage is a classification and explanation of the obstacles (qawāṭiʿ) that hinder the servant from Allah. The author begins by listing some major obstacles: pride, rancor, hypocrisy, and self-admiration. Then he specifies one particularly subtle obstacle: worship done for the sake of obtaining reward or a spiritual opening (fatḥ) in order to become a friend of Allah (walī).

He then clarifies the correct intention: the true servants worship Allah for His essence, because He is worthy of worship, and in obedience to His commands and prohibitions, not for any gain. If an opening comes, it is from Allah's bounty; if they are deprived, it is from His justice. This is because the servant has no inherent right over his Master—all rights belong to Allah over the servant. The servant's duty is to purify himself from the follies of the ego; Allah is not obligated to grant him sacred knowledge (maʿārif qudsiyya).

The commentary then expands on the text. First, it confirms that the phrase 'and among them: worship' means it is part of the obstacles. Then it explains that worship for Allah's essence means because He is the worthy and deserving object of worship, citing the divine saying to David: even if there were no Paradise or Hell, would I not deserve to be worshipped? Next, it addresses the potential objection: does not the verse 'Your Lord has prescribed mercy upon Himself' imply a right? The answer: that mercy is by way of favor and beneficence, not an obligation. The phrase 'evil servants' does not mean the action is forbidden and punishable, but rather it is a lower station, contrary to the high state of pure servitude.

The author then quotes Ibn ʿAṭāʾ Allāh's Ḥikam: being concerned with one's own hidden faults is better than seeking knowledge of hidden things (mugheyyabāt) because Allah is not obliged to reveal them. The commentary explains that 'your concern' (tasharrufuka) means focusing on your own faults and working to purify yourself from them, which is better than trying to uncover what Allah has veiled from you of unseen matters, since Allah is not obliged to give anything to His servants.

Key Terms
قواطع
عبيد السوء
العبادة لذاته
فتح
حق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Worshipping for reward is a sin that incurs punishment.
Correction: The passage clarifies it is not a sin but a decline from high spiritual stations, as it makes one like a mercenary rather than a pure servant.

Checkpoint
According to the text, what is the problem with worshipping Allah for the sake of reward or spiritual opening?
A) It is a sinful act that deserves punishment.
B) It reduces the servant to a mercenary and contradicts pure servitude.
C) It is allowed but discouraged only for beginners.
D) It is the highest form of worship because it shows hope in Allah.

Key Takeaway
Worshipping Allah for any worldly or otherworldly gain lowers one's spiritual station; true worship is for His essence alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you ever check if your heart is expecting something in return—whether it's a good feeling, answered prayers, or even Paradise? What would it take for you to worship Allah as if there were no reward at all?
?
The text says, 'the servant has no right over his Master.' Think of a time when you felt disappointed or angry at Allah for not giving you something you prayed for. How does this lesson confront that feeling of entitlement? What would change in your dua if you truly believed you have no right?
?
Ibn Ata'illah says: 'Your concern with what is hidden within you of faults is better than your concern with what is veiled from you of the unseen.' What fault in yourself are you most avoiding? How does busying yourself with that fault—instead of seeking spiritual experiences—purify your worship?

When Does Asking for Openings Become an Obstacle?
The author answers an objection about asking for openings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between asking for spiritual openings as a permissible request from Allah's bounty versus making worship contingent upon receiving such openings.

Core Idea
Asking for openings is fine; worshipping to get them is the obstacle.

The author now addresses a potential contradiction in his earlier teaching. If worshipping for the sake of spiritual openings is an obstacle, then why does the seeker's supplication ask for such openings? The answer hinges on the intention and context of the request.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: if worship for the sake of opening is a qāṭiʿ, how can the seeker be commanded to ask for it? Note the apparent contradiction.
The objector thinks that any asking for opening is inconsistent with avoiding obstacles.
2
Study the author's answer
The author distinguishes between asking in exchange for worship (which is prohibited) and asking from Allah's bounty while maintaining uprightness (istiqāma). The latter is like asking for provision or health.
The key phrase: 'min fayḍ faḍl Allāh taʿālā lā fī muqābalati shay'.'
3
Connect to Qur'anic evidence
Notice that Allah commands us to ask for guidance in al-Fatiha 17 times daily. This shows that asking for spiritual openings is not inherently problematic; it depends on the motive.
The example of hidāya shows that asking for spiritual benefit is part of the religion.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لا يقال : إذا كانت العبادة من أجل الفتح من القواطع، فكيف يصح أن تأمره بطلبه بقولك : (وقل بذل ربِّ لا تقطعني عنك بقاطع). لأنَّا نقول : طلب الفتح من فيض فضل الله تعالى لا في مقابلة شيء لكن مع الاستقامة أمر مطلوب شرعاً كطلبك منه سعةَ الرّزق ، وصحة البدن والشّفاء من الأمراض الحسيَّة، ألا ترى أنَّه أوجب عليك طلب الهداية في كلِّ يوم وليلة سبعة عشرة مرَّة في قوله تعالى : اهْدِنَا الصِّرَاطَ الْمُسْتَقِيمَ (۲) ، وطلب منك ندباً غير ذلك في النوافل كثيراً بلا

قوله : (لا يقال ... إلخ ) : عبَّر بذلك ؛ إشارة لضعف هذا التّوهُم وبعده .
It is not said: If worship for the sake of the opening (fatḥ) is among the obstacles (qawāṭiʿ), then how is it correct that you command him to request it by your saying: 'And say: O my Lord, do not cut me off from You by any obstacle (qāṭiʿ)'? For we say: Requesting the opening from the overflow of the bounty of Allah the Exalted is not in exchange for anything, but with uprightness (istiqāma) it is a matter requested by the Law, like your requesting from Him abundance of provision, health of body, and healing from sensory illnesses. Do you not see that He obligated upon you the request for guidance every day and night seventeen times in His saying the Exalted: 'Guide us to the straight path' (2), and He requested from you supererogatorily other than that in the supererogatory prayers many times without...

His saying: 'It is not said... etc.': He expressed it thus; an indication of the weakness and remoteness of this supposition.
Source pages p. 485

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer (shubha wa jawāb). The objector argues: if worship done for the sake of obtaining a spiritual opening (fatḥ) is considered an obstacle (qāṭiʿ), then how can the author himself command the seeker to ask for such an opening in the supplication 'O my Lord, do not cut me off from You by any obstacle'? This seems contradictory.

The author answers by making a key distinction: requesting the opening is permissible when it is not in exchange for worship, but rather as a request from Allah's bounty while maintaining uprightness (istiqāma). This is similar to asking for provision, health, or healing—things that are not considered obstacles because they are not the motive of worship. The author further supports this by noting that Allah Himself commands the request for guidance (hidāya) in Sura al-Fatiha seventeen times daily, which is a request for spiritual opening, and He also encourages many other supplications in supererogatory prayers. Thus, the objection is based on a misunderstanding: the problem is not asking for openings per se, but making the opening the purpose of worship. The commentary adds that the phrasing 'It is not said' (lā yuqālu) itself indicates the weakness of the supposition.

Key Terms
فتح
قاطع
طلب الفتح
الاستقامة
العبادة لأجل الفتح

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any asking for spiritual openings is a qāṭiʿ (obstacle).
Correction: The author clarifies that asking while maintaining uprightness and not making it the motive of worship is permissible and even commanded.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, why is asking for spiritual openings (fatḥ) not always a qāṭiʿ?
A) Because asking is a form of worship itself.
B) Because the opening is given only to those who do not ask.
C) Because it is requested from Allah's bounty, not in exchange for worship, similar to asking for provision.
D) Because it is only prohibited if asked during mandatory prayers.

Key Takeaway
Asking for spiritual openings is not inherently an obstacle; it becomes one only when worship is performed for the sake of obtaining them rather than for Allah alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you recite 'Ihdinas siratal mustaqeem' 17 times each day, do you truly mean it as a desperate plea for spiritual opening, or has it become a mere ritual that you no longer feel? What would change if you actually expected an answer?
?
Examine your hidden intentions: In your worship, have you ever caught yourself hoping that your prayers would unlock a spiritual experience or a state of ecstasy? If that desire came true, would you still worship without it? This lesson says that worship for the sake of the opening is an obstacle. Is there any such obstacle hidden in your heart right now?
?
The author distinguishes between asking from Allah's bounty vs asking in exchange. When you ask Allah for anything—provision, health, guidance—do you feel entitled to receive it because of your worship? How can you shift from a transactional mindset to a receptive one, as this lesson teaches?

The Closing Praise and Blessing: Gratitude for the Blessing of Tawhid
The commentator explains the author's closing praise of God and blessing on the Prophet.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author concludes his book with praise of God and blessing on the Prophet, and the role of the Prophet as the means of every blessing, especially the science of tawhid.

Core Idea
Every blessing reaches us through the Prophet, so we must praise God and bless the Prophet.

This final passage from Hashiyat al-Sawi unpacks the author's closing formula. The commentator shows that the act of writing and completing this book is a blessing from God, requiring gratitude. Additionally, because the Prophet is the intermediary through whom every blessing, especially knowledge of tawhid, reaches us, the author invokes blessings upon him. This lesson explores the theological grounding for these closing words.

Guided Path
1
Read the Opening Passage
Read the Arabic text and its translation to understand the author's closing words and the commentator's initial gloss.
Notice the structure: the author first praises God, then blesses the Prophet.
2
Identify the Two Reasons
Identify the two reasons the commentator gives for praising God and for blessing the Prophet.
The first reason is gratitude for the blessing of completing the book. The second is that every blessing, especially tawhid, comes through the Prophet.
3
Explore Key Terms
Understand the commentator's explanation of terms like 'wajaba' (emphasized) and 'al-khātim' (the seal of prophets).
These terms reinforce the necessity and centrality of the Prophet.
4
Reflect on the Prophet's Role
Consider why the commentator emphasizes that the Prophet is the intermediary for divine blessings, especially knowledge of tawhid.
This underscores the importance of love and respect for the Prophet.

(١) سورة الفتح: (٢٩).
Conclusion of the book
Conclusion of the author
Since the composition of this book and the enabling to do it are among the blessings of God Most High, and since gratitude to the Benefactor is obligatory, he concluded his book with praise of God, saying: 'And praise be to God for the completion of this book.'
And since every blessing that reaches us, especially the blessing of the science of tawhid, is through his mediation (peace and blessings be upon him), it was incumbent upon him to invoke blessings upon him, saying: 'And the most excellent prayer and peace' — meaning the greatest kinds of blessings and greetings from the Lord of creation, upon the Prophet — meaning the one who informed about God Most High, calling to tawhid and worship of the One, and to fairness in all matters, and to the final outcome of the affair of the obedient one.
His statement: 'from the blessings of God': the prepositional phrase is attached to an omitted predicate of 'was'; the estimation is 'existing and occurring'; 'blessings' (ni'am) is the plural of 'blessing' (ni'mah), which is every praiseworthy thing whose outcome is commendable according to the Law.
His statement: 'he concluded' his book is the answer to 'since' (lammā).
His statement: 'for the completion': he chose praise for the act because it is praise without intermediary, unlike praise for the blessing.
His statement: 'was incumbent' (wajaba): i.e., it was emphasized.
His statement: 'and fairness in all matters': i.e., moderation in them.
His statement: 'and the final outcome of the affair of the obedient one': i.e., by good tidings.
And the final outcome of the affair of the disobedient one (al-Hāshimī) — a relative attribution to Hāshim, the grandfather of his father (peace and blessings be upon him) — (al-Khātim) i.e., the seal, the completer of the prophets and messengers.
And upon his Family (Ālih) i.e., his followers, and upon his Companions (Sahbih) — a specific conjunction after a general one — (al-akārim) plural of akram, for they generously gave themselves in support of God and His Messenger, possessing noble morals, compassion, and mercy: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God, and those with him are stern against the disbelievers, merciful among themselves. You see them bowing and prostrating, seeking bounty from God and [His] pleasure.' (1)
And His statement: 'and the final outcome of the affair of the disobedient one': i.e., by warning.
His statement: 'the grandfather of his father': meaning that he is Muhammad ibn 'Abdillāh ibn 'Abd al-Muttalib ibn Hāshim ibn 'Abd Manāf ibn Quṣayy ibn Kilāb ibn Murrah ibn Ka'b ibn Lu'ayy ibn Ghālib ibn Fihr ibn Mālik ibn al-Naḍr ibn Kinānah ibn Khuzaymah ibn Mudrikah ibn Ilyās ibn Muḍar ibn Nizār ibn Ma'add ibn 'Adnān.
His statement: 'i.e., the seal of the prophets and messengers': i.e., in time and honor.
His statement: 'i.e., his followers in faith, thus including every believer, even if disobedient.'
His statement: 'al-akārim' is a description of the companions, as evidenced by the commentator's branching.
His statement: 'Muhammad is the Messenger of God... etc.' is a proof for what precedes it.
(1) Surah al-Fath: (29).
Source pages p. 492

Commentary
This passage is the concluding section of Hashiyat al-Sawi, where the commentator explains the author's closing words. The author ends his book by praising God for enabling its completion and by invoking blessings on the Prophet. The commentator provides a detailed gloss on each phrase. He first clarifies that the book's composition is a blessing from God, and gratitude is obligatory, so the author praised God. Then he explains that every blessing, especially knowledge of tawhid, reaches us through the Prophet, making it necessary to invoke blessings upon him. The commentator parses grammatical points: 'from the blessings of God' is a prepositional phrase attached to an omitted predicate; 'he concluded' is the answer to 'since' (lammā). He notes that the author chose to praise the act of completion directly rather than the blessing itself, because that praise is without intermediary. 'Wajaba' (was incumbent) is interpreted as emphasis. 'Fairness' is explained as moderation. The 'outcome of the obedient' is good tidings, while the 'outcome of the disobedient' is warning. For the Prophet, he gives the lineage going back to Hāshim, and describes him as al-Khātim (the seal) who completes the prophets in time and honor. The 'Family' (Āl) are his followers in faith, including every believer even if sinful. The 'Companions' (Sahb) are described as 'al-akārim' (the generous), and the commentator cites Qur'an 48:29 as evidence of their noble qualities and sacrifices. The commentary thus unpacks the theological and linguistic dimensions of the author's standard closing formula.

Misconception: One might think the blessing on the Prophet is a mere formality without deeper significance.
Correction: The commentator shows it is obligatory because all blessings, especially knowledge of tawhid, reach us through the Prophet.

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Companions as 'Al-Akarim': A Quranic Proof
Al-Sawi explains that 'al-akārim' describes the Companions and cites a verse from Surat al-Fath.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the term 'al-akārim' describes the Companions and how the Quranic verse from Surat al-Fath supports this description.

Core Idea
The Quran itself testifies to the noble character of the Companions.

In this brief passage, Al-Sawi clarifies a key term in the text: 'al-akārim' (the most generous). He explains that this description refers specifically to the Companions of the Prophet. To support this, he cites a well-known verse from Surat al-Fath that portrays the Companions with attributes of strength and mercy. This lesson will help you see how the commentator grounds his interpretation in the Quran itself.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage carefully

The Arabic contains some OCR errors; focus on the intended meaning.

Al-Sawi explicitly states that 'al-akārim' is a description of the Companions. Note the evidence from the commentator's branching.
3
Examine the Quranic proof
The verse from Surat al-Fath is presented as an inference (istidlāl) for the preceding claim. Consider how the verse's description matches the Companions' noble qualities.
4
Correct a common misunderstanding
Some might think 'al-akārim' refers to the Prophet. Al-Sawi clarifies it describes the Companions. Why is this distinction important?
The verse includes the Prophet as well, but the term specifically modifies the Companions.

The verse is a proof text (istidlāl) supporting the praise of the Companions.

Prophetic Advice to Imam Ali: Forbearance and Concealing Faults
Prophetic counsels to Imam Ali on moral character.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three prophetic counsels given to Imam Ali and their promised spiritual rewards.

Core Idea
Character is refined by suppressing anger, concealing faults, and avoiding reproach.

In this lesson, we explore a precious prophetic counsel (waṣiyya) from the Prophet Muhammad (ﷺ) to Imam ʿAlī (ra). The advice is concise but profound, linking each virtuous action to a specific divine reward. As you read, notice how the Prophet ties inner virtue to tangible blessings from Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage

The poetic line about hardship and relief sets the tone for the counsels that follow.

Each command is followed by a result clause starting with a verb.

Reflect on the connection to imamate

4
Apply the lesson
Consider how you can embody these teachings in your own life. What does it mean to 'conceal faults' and 'not reproach'?
This is about character, not just theory.

Benefits recorded at the end of the manuscript: 'How often had severity fought me with its army, so my heart was straitened and disturbed. Then when I despaired of its removal, the glad tidings of relief came to me from nine [sources]?' And from the counsels of our master the Messenger of God, may God bless him and grant him peace, to our master Ali, that he said to him during a discussion of the imamate, in signs by which the difference between the righteous and the corrupt, and between the learned and the ignorant, is known. He said to him: 'Suppress anger, and God will fill your heart with light, and your chest with knowledge, forbearance, and mercy. Conceal the faults of people, and God will conceal your fault. Do not reproach anyone for what is in him, lest God test you and spare him.'
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage presents a series of benefits (fawāʾid) recorded at the end of the manuscript. It begins with a poetic reflection on hardship and relief, then moves to a prophetic counsel (waṣiyya) from the Prophet (ﷺ) to ʿAlī (ra). The Prophet mentions that during a discussion of the imamate, he gave signs to distinguish the righteous from the corrupt and the learned from the ignorant. Then he gives specific advice: suppress anger, conceal faults, and avoid reproaching others. Each action has a corresponding divine reward. The passage emphasizes personal virtue and social ethics based on prophetic guidance.

The Virtue of Sitting with the Poor
Counsel on sitting with the poor vs. rich.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the spiritual benefits of sitting with the poor and the harms of sitting with the rich, as taught by the scholars.

Core Idea
Company shapes the soul—choose the poor for mercy.

This passage advises us on the company we keep. The scholars have noted that sitting with the poor draws mercy and remembrance of Allah, while sitting with the rich can harden the heart. Let's examine these profound counsels.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage carefully, then the English translation. Notice the contrast between the two types of company.
2
Contrast the two types of company
Identify what sitting with the rich does (kills the heart, forgetfulness) versus sitting with the poor (enlivens the heart, reminds of Allah's bounty).
3
Reflect on personal application
Consider how your own choice of company affects your spiritual state. What can you learn from this advice?
This is a moral teaching, not a legal obligation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جالس الفقراء؛ فإن رحمة الله لا تفارقهم. مجالسة الأغنياء تميت القلب وتنسي الموت والقبر، ومجالسة الفقراء القلب، وتذكر ما عند الله من الفضل. للسعيد ثلاث علامات قول الحق ولو على نفسه، والرغبة في طاعة الله تعالى، والفرار عن معصيته . وللشقي ثلاث علامات جمود العين وقسوة القلب، وحب الدنيا .
Sit with the poor, for the mercy of God does not depart from them. Sitting with the rich kills the heart and makes one forget death and the grave. But sitting with the poor enlivens the heart and reminds one of the bounty that is with God. The blessed have three signs: speaking the truth even against oneself, desire for obedience to God Almighty, and fleeing from His disobedience. The wretched have three signs: hardness of the eye, hardness of the heart, and love of the world.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage gives two pieces of counsel: first, the virtue of sitting with the poor as a means of mercy and remembrance of God, contrasted with sitting with the rich which hardens the heart. Then it enumerates three signs of the blessed (saʿīd) and three of the wretched (shaqī). The blessed speak truth even at their own expense, desire obedience, and flee sin. The wretched have dry eyes (indicating lack of tears), hard hearts, and love of this world. These are moral and spiritual indicators.

Key Terms
جالس الفقراء
مجالسة الأغنياء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What does sitting with the rich do according to the passage?
A) Enlivens the heart
B) Kills the heart and makes one forget death
C) Increases love of the world
D) Reminds one of Allah's bounty

Key Takeaway
The company you keep affects your spiritual state—choose to sit with the poor for mercy and remembrance of Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you deliberately chose to sit with someone less fortunate than you, not out of pity but to learn and be reminded of Allah's mercy? What stopped you?
?
If your heart feels dead toward death and the grave, could it be that your closest companions are those who value this world? Who must you distance yourself from to revive your heart?
?
The mercy of Allah never departs from the poor, yet you avoid them. What does that say about the state of your own heart, and what one concrete step will you take this week to sit with the poor?

Three Signs of the Blessed and Three Signs of the Wretched
Enumeration of signs for the blessed and wretched.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will know the three signs of the blessed and the three signs of the wretched as outlined in traditional Islamic teachings.

Core Idea
True success is shown in truth, obedience, and fear; woe is shown in hardness and love of world.

This passage provides a concise list of spiritual indicators. The blessed exhibit truthfulness, desire for obedience, and avoidance of sin. The wretched show dryness of eye, hardness of heart, and love of this world. Let's explore these signs.

Guided Path
1
Read the signs of the blessed
First read the three signs of the blessed (saʿīd). Notice each one: speaking truth, desiring obedience, fleeing from sin.
2
Read the signs of the wretched
Then read the three signs of the wretched (shaqī): hardness of eye, hardness of heart, love of the world.
3
Contrast and reflect
Compare the two lists. Which traits do you see in yourself? Use this as a tool for self-assessment.
These are indicative, not absolute final judgments.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
جالس الفقراء؛ فإن رحمة الله لا تفارقهم. مجالسة الأغنياء تميت القلب وتنسي الموت والقبر، ومجالسة الفقراء القلب، وتذكر ما عند الله من الفضل. للسعيد ثلاث علامات قول الحق ولو على نفسه، والرغبة في طاعة الله تعالى، والفرار عن معصيته . وللشقي ثلاث علامات جمود العين وقسوة القلب، وحب الدنيا .
Sit with the poor, for the mercy of God does not depart from them. Sitting with the rich kills the heart and makes one forget death and the grave. But sitting with the poor enlivens the heart and reminds one of the bounty that is with God. The blessed have three signs: speaking the truth even against oneself, desire for obedience to God Almighty, and fleeing from His disobedience. The wretched have three signs: hardness of the eye, hardness of the heart, and love of the world.
Source pages p. 497

Commentary
This passage gives two pieces of counsel: first, the virtue of sitting with the poor as a means of mercy and remembrance of God, contrasted with sitting with the rich which hardens the heart. Then it enumerates three signs of the blessed (saʿīd) and three of the wretched (shaqī). The blessed speak truth even at their own expense, desire obedience, and flee sin. The wretched have dry eyes (indicating lack of tears), hard hearts, and love of this world. These are moral and spiritual indicators.

Key Terms
السعيد
الشقي
جمود العين
قسوة القلب
حب الدنيا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: These signs are absolute judgments of a person's final state.
Correction: These are indicative traits that guide self-assessment and repentance, not fixed decrees.

Checkpoint
Which of the following is a sign of the wretched?
A) Speaking truth against oneself
B) Desire for obedience
C) Hardness of heart
D) Fleeing from sin

Key Takeaway
Our actions and inner states reveal our spiritual direction—strive for the signs of the blessed.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of the three signs of the blessed do you find most difficult to embody in your daily life? What is holding you back from speaking truth even when it costs you?
?
Consider the last time your heart felt hard towards a reminder of Allah. What was the worldly attachment that caused that hardness? Will you let it remain?
?
You claim to desire obedience to Allah, but when did you last flee from a sin rather than entertain it? Be honest with yourself. What step can you take today to strengthen that desire?

Four Signs of the Gnostic
The text enumerates four signs of the gnostic.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four signs that distinguish the gnostic (arif) from worldly attachment.

Core Idea
The arif is marked by freedom from worldly worries.

This passage from al-Sawi presents the spiritual profile of the true knower of God. Unlike the common person who is tossed by wealth and poverty, the arif remains steady. Let's examine each sign.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the four signs are all negations: 'does not...'
2
Reflect on each sign
For each of the four signs, ask yourself: why is this a sign of gnosis? How does detachment from wealth, poverty, provision, and the world indicate spiritual knowledge?
The arif trusts in Allah's decree.
3
Consider the opposite
Think about the opposite traits: rejoicing at wealth, fearing poverty, worrying about provision, and coveting the world. How do these reflect ignorance?
This connection will be made explicit in the next lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللعارف أربع علامات لا يفرح بالغنى، ولا يخاف الفقر، ولا يهتم بالرزق، ولا يحرص على الدنيا . وللجاهل أربع علامات ينازع من فوقه في العلم، وينكر على من دونه في العلم، ويذكر عيوب الناس وييئس من معاشرة أهله وولده.
The gnostic has four signs: he does not rejoice at wealth, does not fear poverty, does not worry about provision, and does not covet the world. The ignorant has four signs: he argues with those above him in knowledge, criticizes those below him in knowledge, mentions the faults of people, and despairs of living with his family and children.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage lists four signs for the ʿārif (gnostic, knower of God) and four for the jāhil (ignorant). The ʿārif is detached from worldly concerns: no excessive joy at wealth, no fear of poverty, no anxiety over provision, and no greed for the world. The jāhil, in contrast, engages in unnecessary debate with superiors, criticizes those less knowledgeable, spreads people's faults, and finds it difficult to live harmoniously with his own family. These signs are practical indicators of spiritual state.

Key Terms
العارف
لا يفرح بالغنى
لا يخاف الفقر
لا يهتم بالرزق
لا يحرص على الدنيا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think the arif must physically renounce all wealth and live in poverty.
Correction: The signs are about inner detachment, not outward poverty. The arif may possess wealth but not be attached to it.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT a sign of the gnostic?
A) He does not rejoice at wealth
B) He does not fear poverty
C) He does not worry about provision
D) He does not seek knowledge

Key Takeaway
The gnostic is characterized by inner detachment from worldly concerns—wealth, poverty, provision, and the world itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you secretly fear poverty more than you fear displeasing Allah? When you check your bank account, does your heart sink or rise based on the number? That fear is precisely what the arif has been freed from. Are you ready to ask yourself honestly: why do I still cling to that fear?
?
You say you trust Allah for provision, but when you wake up each morning, is your first thought about your to-do list for making money or about the One who appointed the means? The arif does not worry about provision. What would have to change in your daily habits for that to become true of you?
?
When you see others with more wealth or comfort, does envy stir in your chest? The arif does not covet the world. What is the one worldly thing you find hardest to let go of, and what does that reveal about the state of your heart?

Four Signs of the Ignorant
The text enumerates four signs of the ignorant.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four signs that reveal ignorance (jahl) in a person.

Core Idea
The ignorant is trapped in pride, criticism, and despair.

Having described the gnostic, the text now turns to the ignorant. These signs are practical warnings for self-reflection. Notice how each stems from a lack of inner knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Note the four signs: arguing, criticizing, fault-finding, and despairing.
2
Analyze each sign
For each sign, consider the underlying cause: pride, envy, or lack of self-awareness. How does ignorance manifest in these behaviors?
3
Contrast with the gnostic
Compare these signs with the four signs of the gnostic. What do these contrasts teach us about the path to knowledge?
The gnostic is detached; the ignorant is entangled.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وللعارف أربع علامات لا يفرح بالغنى، ولا يخاف الفقر، ولا يهتم بالرزق، ولا يحرص على الدنيا . وللجاهل أربع علامات ينازع من فوقه في العلم، وينكر على من دونه في العلم، ويذكر عيوب الناس وييئس من معاشرة أهله وولده.
The gnostic has four signs: he does not rejoice at wealth, does not fear poverty, does not worry about provision, and does not covet the world. The ignorant has four signs: he argues with those above him in knowledge, criticizes those below him in knowledge, mentions the faults of people, and despairs of living with his family and children.
Source pages p. 498

Commentary
This passage lists four signs for the ʿārif (gnostic, knower of God) and four for the jāhil (ignorant). The ʿārif is detached from worldly concerns: no excessive joy at wealth, no fear of poverty, no anxiety over provision, and no greed for the world. The jāhil, in contrast, engages in unnecessary debate with superiors, criticizes those less knowledgeable, spreads people's faults, and finds it difficult to live harmoniously with his own family. These signs are practical indicators of spiritual state.

Key Terms
الجاهل
ينازع من فوقه
ينكر على من دونه
يذكر عيوب الناس
ييئس من معاشرة أهله وولده

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think ignorance is simply lack of knowledge; these signs show it is also bad character.
Correction: Ignorance (jahl) here refers to spiritual ignorance that manifests in harmful behaviors, not just lack of information.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does the ignorant do regarding those below him in knowledge?
A) He teaches them patiently
B) He criticizes them
C) He ignores them
D) He praises them

Key Takeaway
Ignorance is marked by pride in knowledge, fault-finding, and inability to maintain family bonds.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Which of these four signs did you see in yourself today? Be specific about a recent interaction.
?
When you argue with someone more knowledgeable, what is the true motive behind your words? Are you seeking truth or defending your ego?
?
How does your behavior at home reflect the last sign? Are you part of the problem or the solution in your family's harmony? Commit to one concrete change this week.
